# Episode 1: Loose Screws

The kitchen chair had developed a personality.

It began with a small complaint—a metallic sigh when Miles shifted his weight during breakfast, the kind of sound that suggested the chair had been carrying burdens heavier than a German Shepherd's morning toast and marmalade. By the time Lillian settled into it with her customary flourish, the chair had progressed to a full-throated protest, a groan that seemed to echo through the kitchen's warm morning light.

"That chair's judging me," Lillian announced, spreading marmalade across her toast with the precision of someone defusing a bomb. "I can feel its disapproval radiating through the seat cushions."

Miles looked up from his crossword puzzle, pencil poised above the newspaper like a conductor's baton. "It's not judging you. It's just... structurally exhausted. Like everything else in this house."

The house had indeed begun to show its age in small, domestic ways. The floorboards in the hallway had developed a Morse code of creaks that Miles had learned to interpret—a particular groan near the bathroom meant the pipes were feeling temperamental, while the sharp crack outside Sarah's room suggested the house was settling into its foundations with the resigned sigh of middle age. The kitchen cabinets had started closing at odd angles, as if they'd grown tired of their decades-long commitment to parallel lines.

"Everything's falling apart," Lillian said, but her voice carried the fond exasperation of someone discussing a beloved relative's eccentricities rather than genuine complaint. "The chair, the floor, that mysterious stain on the ceiling that looks like Australia—"

"That's been there since 2017," Miles reminded her.

"Exactly! Even our stains are getting old."

Sarah emerged from her room wearing an oversized sweater that had once belonged to Miles, her golden fur barely visible beneath the gray wool. She moved with the quiet efficiency of someone who'd learned to navigate the house's quirks—stepping over the creaky floorboard near the bathroom, giving the kitchen chair a respectful berth as she headed for the kettle.

"Morning," she said, her voice still soft with sleep. "The chair's dying again?"

"It's not dying," Miles said, setting down his pencil. "It's just... expressing itself."

"Everything's expressing itself this morning," Lillian observed. "The chair's expressing structural fatigue, the floor's expressing existential dread, and Miles is expressing himself through the cryptic crossword, which I notice he's been working on for forty-five minutes and has only filled in three answers."

"They're difficult clues," Miles said with dignity. "Fourteen across: 'Sound of contentment, briefly (3).' The answer is clearly 'purr' but that doesn't fit with seven down, which is 'Ancient Greek marketplace (5)'."

"Maybe the chair's trying to tell us something," Sarah suggested, settling carefully onto a different chair that responded with only a polite creak. "Like those people who speak in tongues, but with furniture."

The morning continued its familiar rhythm—tea, toast, the gentle negotiation of shared space that had become their domestic choreography. Lillian hummed while she ate, a melody that seemed to rise from the house itself, something between a lullaby and a work song. Miles methodically worked through his crossword, occasionally making small sounds of triumph or defeat. Sarah sketched in the margins of yesterday's newspaper, turning the financial section into a landscape of gentle hills and wandering paths.

It was, Miles reflected, the kind of morning that made him understand why people wrote poems about breakfast tables. The light filtering through the kitchen window had that particular quality of late summer—golden and slightly tired, like it had spent the whole season working overtime and was ready for autumn's more reasonable hours. The air carried the familiar scent of Lillian's bergamot tea mixing with the faint sweetness of Sarah's sketching pencils, overlaid with the comforting smell of toast and the underlying musk of a house that had been lived in long enough to develop its own personality.

Then the chair gave up entirely.

It happened with the suddenness of a small tragedy—one moment Lillian was reaching for the marmalade, her voice rising in a crescendo about the philosophical implications of breakfast spreads, and the next she was tilting sideways with the slow-motion inevitability of someone watching their life flash before their eyes. The chair's left front leg detached completely, sending up a small puff of sawdust that caught the morning light like fairy dust, or possibly the ghost of furniture past.

"Oh!" Lillian exclaimed, arms windmilling in a way that would have been comical if it weren't happening in real time. Miles moved with the speed of someone who'd spent years developing reflexes for exactly these moments, catching her before she hit the floor with the practiced ease of a man who'd learned that marriage to Lillian meant always being ready to catch whatever was falling—whether it was furniture, plans, or occasionally Lillian herself.

"I've got you," he said, steadying her against his chest as the chair collapsed entirely, its remaining legs giving up the ghost with the resigned dignity of soldiers who'd fought the good fight and were ready for honorable discharge.

"Well," Lillian said, looking down at the ruins of what had been, until moments ago, a perfectly serviceable kitchen chair. "That's one way to end breakfast."

Sarah had already moved to examine the wreckage, her artist's eye cataloging the details—the clean break where the leg had separated from the seat, the accumulation of dust in the joints, the way the wood had aged from the inside out. "It's been dying for a while," she said quietly. "Look at the grain here—it's been stressed for months, maybe years. We just weren't paying attention."

"The chair's been trying to tell us it needed help," Miles said, still holding Lillian steady. "We just weren't listening."

Lillian stepped away from the wreckage, her movements careful and deliberate. "Everything's trying to tell us something this morning, isn't it? The chair's telling us it needs repair, the house is telling us it needs attention, and I'm telling you both that we need to start paying better attention to the things that hold us up."

She looked around the kitchen with the critical eye of someone seeing familiar space through new perspective. The hairline crack in the ceiling that did indeed resemble Australia, the cabinet doors that hung slightly askew, the window that stuck when you tried to open it, requiring a particular combination of pressure and prayer.

"This house is like a relationship," she announced. "It needs maintenance. It needs attention. It needs someone to notice when things are starting to fall apart before they actually do."

Miles bent to collect the chair's remains, handling the pieces with the gentleness of someone gathering the bones of a beloved pet. "I can fix it," he said. "The leg's a clean break. I just need to re-glue the joint, maybe add some reinforcement—"

"Of course you can fix it," Lillian said, but her voice carried a new note, something between pride and concern. "You can fix anything. That's what you do. You fix chairs and cabinets and relationships and probably the fundamental fabric of the universe if someone asked you nicely enough."

Sarah had moved to the window, looking out at the garden where the late summer light was beginning to shift toward autumn's more angular approach. "Maybe some things aren't meant to be fixed," she said quietly. "Maybe some things are meant to be replaced."

The words hung in the air like a question mark, or possibly a prophecy. Miles set down the chair pieces and looked at his family—Lillian standing in the middle of the kitchen with marmalade on her cheek and determination in her eyes, Sarah silhouetted against the window with the thoughtful expression of someone who'd learned to see beyond surfaces to the structures underneath.

"We'll fix the chair," he said finally. "But maybe we should also look at what else needs fixing. The floorboards, the cabinets, that window that sticks when it rains—"

"The foundation," Lillian added quietly. "The things that hold everything else up."

They spent the rest of the morning making lists. Miles produced his small notebook, the one he kept for household projects and grocery runs, and began cataloging the house's various complaints. Sarah contributed observations about structural stress points and aesthetic opportunities. Lillian provided commentary that ranged from practical suggestions to philosophical musings about the nature of home maintenance as a metaphor for personal growth.

It was, Miles thought, the kind of morning that reminded him why he'd chosen this life—this house, this family, this particular corner of Islington where the light fell just so and the neighbors knew your business but also brought soup when you were sick. The chair would be fixed, and the floorboards would be quieted, and the window would learn to open smoothly again. But more importantly, they would do it together, with the easy collaboration of people who'd learned to read each other's rhythms and anticipate each other's needs.

The email arrived at 11:47 AM, while they were still discussing whether to tackle the kitchen cabinets before or after the floorboards. Miles's phone chimed with the particular notification he'd assigned to work messages, and he glanced at it with the automatic reflex of someone who'd learned to balance professional obligations with domestic life.

The subject line read: "Opportunity in Berlin - Time-Sensitive."

He didn't open it immediately. Instead, he set the phone face-down on the table and returned to the conversation about cabinet hinges, his voice steady and his attention apparently fully engaged in the discussion about whether European or traditional hardware would better serve the kitchen's aesthetic needs.

But Lillian noticed. She noticed everything, really—the way his shoulders shifted slightly when the phone chimed, the fraction of a second his gaze lingered on the screen before he turned it over, the careful neutrality that settled over his expression like a mask he'd forgotten he was wearing.

"Work?" she asked, her voice carrying only casual interest, but her eyes sharp with the particular attention she reserved for things that mattered.

"Just the usual," Miles said, picking up his pencil and returning to his crossword with the focused intensity of someone who'd suddenly remembered that puzzles required concentration. "Nothing urgent."

But the chair had been trying to tell them something that morning, and the floorboards had been whispering their own messages, and the house itself seemed to be holding its breath, waiting to see what would happen next. The screw had fallen out of the kitchen chair, and something else had loosened in the process—something more fundamental than furniture joints or floorboard nails.

Sarah, who'd been watching the exchange with the quiet attention of someone who'd learned to read the spaces between words, went back to her sketching. But her pencil moved differently now, tracing lines that seemed to suggest movement rather than stillness, change rather than permanence.

The morning continued, but something had shifted. The light coming through the kitchen window seemed different somehow—more aware of its own transience, more conscious of the way it illuminated not just their breakfast table but the careful architecture of a life that had been built slowly, deliberately, and might now be facing its own kind of renovation.

Miles picked up the chair pieces and carried them toward his workshop, his movements steady and purposeful. But his mind was already traveling elsewhere, to a city where the light fell differently and the chairs might be made of different wood and the questions might be asked in a language he hadn't spoken in years but still understood perfectly.

The house would be fixed. The chair would be repaired. The floorboards would be quieted and the cabinets would be straightened and the window would learn to open smoothly again.

But some things, once loosened, could never be quite the same.
# Episode 2: Home Economics

Miles had discovered that learning German in secret required the same skills as conducting an affair—careful scheduling, plausible excuses, and the ability to look innocent while thinking about something else entirely.

The language app sat minimized on his tablet, its cheerful yellow icon a constant reminder of the deception he was cultivating in fifteen-minute intervals. He'd started with the basics—*Guten Morgen*, *Danke schön*, *Wo ist die Bibliothek*—phrases that felt like building blocks for a life he hadn't yet decided whether he wanted to build. The pronunciation exercises were particularly challenging; he'd taken to practicing them in the garden shed, where the sound of his own voice speaking foreign words couldn't travel through the house's thin walls and into Lillian's sharp ears.

"You're spending a lot of time in the shed," Lillian observed from the kitchen doorway, watching him wipe grease from his hands with the methodical precision of someone who'd learned that cleanliness was next to invisibility. "The chair's been fixed for three days, and you're still out there every morning like you're hiding from creditors."

"I'm organizing," Miles said, which was technically true. He'd organized the shed, then organized his tools, then organized his thoughts into German vocabulary lists that he kept hidden behind the boxes of nails and screws. "The space has potential. I'm thinking about turning it into a proper workshop."

"A workshop for what? You've already fixed everything in this house that can be fixed. Unless you're planning to start breaking things just so you can repair them again." Lillian moved into the kitchen with her usual theatrical presence, her voice carrying the particular melody she used when she was preparing for a performance. "Though I suppose that would be one way to stay busy."

The kitchen had become her rehearsal space in recent weeks. She'd taken to practicing scales while the kettle boiled, her voice rising and falling with the steam. The acoustics were surprisingly good—something about the way the morning light came through the window and the way the tiles reflected sound made her voice seem larger than the room could reasonably contain. She'd been invited to perform at a small venue in Camden, a place that served coffee during the day and hosted folk singers at night, and the owner had promised her a regular slot if tonight's set went well.

"How's the setlist coming?" Miles asked, grateful for the change of subject. He moved to the sink, washing his hands with extra thoroughness to give himself something to do besides think about the German word for "opportunity"—*Gelegenheit*—which had been circling in his mind like a bird that couldn't find a place to land.

"It's coming." Lillian's voice carried the particular tension of someone who was preparing to be vulnerable in public. "I'm doing that Cohen song—you know, the one about the cracks—and then maybe that original piece I wrote about the garden. Though I'm not sure about the key change in the middle. It might be too much for a coffee shop audience."

"Coffee shop audiences are more sophisticated than you think," Miles said, drying his hands with the towel that hung by the sink. "They come for the music, not just the caffeine."

Sarah emerged in the doorway, her sketchbook tucked under her arm and her expression carrying the particular concentration of someone who'd been up since dawn working on something she wasn't ready to discuss. "Are we talking about the gig tonight? Because I think you should do the garden song. It's honest. People respond to honest."

"Since when are you an expert on audience psychology?" Lillian asked, but her voice carried the fondness she always used when Sarah offered opinions about art and life and the complicated territory between them.

"Since I started paying attention to what makes people feel things." Sarah moved to the table, opening her sketchbook to reveal a series of drawings that seemed to capture the essence of their kitchen—the way the light fell across the table, the particular angle of the chair that had been broken and repaired, the expression on Lillian's face when she thought nobody was watching. "The garden song is about staying in one place and growing roots. That's what people want to hear right now. Especially here."

Miles felt the words like a small weight in his chest. *Staying in one place and growing roots.* The German app chimed softly from the other room, a reminder that he'd missed his morning lesson. He'd started setting alarms throughout the day, little breadcrumbs of language learning that he followed like a path leading away from the life he'd spent years building in this kitchen, with these people, in this house that creaked and groaned and expressed itself in the language of familiar complaint.

"I should check on the shed," he said, but his voice carried the particular note of someone inventing reasons to leave rooms where conversations were becoming too specific. "I want to measure the space properly before I start planning the workshop layout."

"You do that," Lillian said, but her eyes followed him with the particular attention of someone who'd learned to read the spaces between words and the pauses between actions. "I'll be here, practicing my key changes and wondering why my husband has suddenly developed an intense interest in shed architecture."

The garden was warm with late summer light, the kind of golden warmth that seemed to accumulate in the leaves and flowers like stored memory. Miles had planted new roses along the back fence, their blooms heavy and fragrant in the morning air. He'd told himself it was just gardening, just the usual maintenance of a space that required attention and care, but he'd chosen the varieties carefully—*Peace* roses, white with pink edges, flowers that carried names like *Remember Me* and *Lasting Love* and *Forever Yours*.

The shed stood at the garden's far end, its weathered wood blending into the landscape like something that had grown there rather than been built. Inside, he'd created a sanctuary of sorts—a place where tools hung in neat rows and the air smelled of sawdust and possibility. He'd installed a small workbench, organized his collection of screws and nails into glass jars that caught the light like amber specimens, and created a space that felt both functional and contemplative.

He'd also, he realized, created a place where he could practice German pronunciation without being overheard. The language app opened to reveal his progress—twenty-three days of consistent practice, vocabulary building slowly like the construction of a bridge he wasn't sure he wanted to cross. *Berlin* was a word that appeared frequently in the exercises, usually paired with phrases about opportunity and change and the kind of professional advancement that came with international experience.

The email from the Berlin firm had been sitting in his inbox for three days now, unread but not unconsidered. He'd opened it once, long enough to scan the subject line and the first paragraph, which mentioned something about a "dream position" and "unprecedented opportunity" and "the kind of career-defining moment that comes once in a lifetime." He'd closed it again almost immediately, as if the words themselves might be contagious, might spread through his life like an infection he wasn't sure he wanted to catch.

But he'd also started learning German. That had to mean something, didn't it? The careful accumulation of vocabulary, the methodical practice of pronunciation, the way he'd begun thinking in simple German sentences during his morning walks. *Ich gehe zum Garten.* *Die Sonne scheint.* *Ich habe eine Familie.* I have a family. I have a life. I have roots that go deep into this soil, this house, this particular corner of Islington where the light falls just so and the neighbors know your business but also bring soup when you're sick.

The shed door opened behind him, and Sarah stepped inside with the quiet efficiency of someone who'd learned to move through spaces without disturbing their essential nature. She looked around with her artist's eye, taking in the organized tools, the labeled jars, the small notebook where he'd been keeping track of household projects and, he realized with a small jolt of panic, German vocabulary lists.

"You've been busy," she said quietly, moving to examine the workbench with the careful attention of someone who understood that spaces revealed their creators' intentions through their organization. "This isn't just about fixing things, is it?"

"It's about being useful," Miles said, which was true but not complete. "About having a place where I can work with my hands and make things better."

"Or make things different." Sarah picked up one of the vocabulary lists, her eyes scanning the German words with the particular focus of someone who'd learned to read between lines and underneath surfaces. "Berlin's a long way to go for different."

The words hung in the air like a question mark, or possibly an accusation. Miles took the list from her hand with careful gentleness, folding it and tucking it into his pocket like something precious and dangerous.

"It's just language learning," he said. "Keeping my mind active. You know how I get when I don't have projects."

"I know how you get when you're preparing for something you're not ready to talk about." Sarah moved to the window, looking out at the garden where the roses grew in their careful rows, each plant labeled with its name and planting date. "The roses you've been planting—they're all about memory, aren't they? *Remember Me*. *Lasting Love*. You've been building a memorial garden while you're learning how to ask for directions in German."

Miles felt caught, exposed, like someone who'd been speaking in code only to discover that everyone else had learned the language. "It's not like that."

"It's exactly like that." Sarah's voice carried the particular wisdom of someone who'd learned to see patterns and connections, who understood that people revealed their intentions through their choices. "You're preparing for something and mourning it at the same time. That's what the garden's about. That's what the shed's about. That's what the German lessons are about."

Through the shed window, they could see Lillian moving through the kitchen, her voice carrying snippets of melody as she practiced for tonight's performance. She moved with the unconscious grace of someone who'd learned to inhabit space completely, who sang not because she was performing but because music was how she understood the world.

"She doesn't know," Miles said quietly. "About the email. About Berlin. About any of it."

"She knows something's changing. She's not stupid. She's just... trusting." Sarah turned back to him, her expression carrying the particular concern of someone who'd learned to love the family she'd chosen while understanding how fragile such choices could be. "The question is whether you're going to trust her back."

The language app chimed again, a soft reminder that he'd missed his afternoon lesson. Miles silenced it with a gesture that felt like surrender. "I don't know what I'm doing," he admitted. "I don't know if I'm preparing to leave or preparing to stay or just... preparing."

"Maybe that's what this season's about," Sarah said quietly. "Not the leaving or the staying, but the preparing. The noticing what needs fixing and what needs letting go and what needs holding onto with both hands."

She moved toward the shed door, pausing to look back at him with the particular expression of someone who'd learned to speak difficult truths with gentleness. "Tonight, when Mum's doing her set, listen to the songs she chooses. Listen to what she's really saying about home and staying and growing roots. Then decide what you want to say back."

The shed felt different after she left—smaller and larger at the same time, a space that had contained a conversation that couldn't be unheard or unsaid. Miles looked around at his careful organization, his labeled jars and alphabetized tools, his small sanctuary of control and competence. He thought about the German vocabulary lists hidden in his pocket, the email waiting in his inbox, the roses growing in careful rows outside the window.

He thought about Lillian's voice carrying through the afternoon air, practicing melodies about staying and leaving and the complicated territory between them. He thought about Sarah's drawings, which captured not just what things looked like but what they meant. He thought about the house that creaked and groaned and expressed itself in the language of familiar complaint, and the garden that grew according to his careful planning but also according to its own mysterious logic.

The shed door stood open to the afternoon light, and through it he could see the life he'd built spreading out in all directions—the house where he'd raised a daughter and loved a wife and learned to fix things that broke, the garden where he'd planted roses with names like promises, the neighborhood where people knew your business but also brought soup when you were sick.

He could also see, in the distance, the road that led away from all of it, toward cities where the light fell differently and the chairs might be made of different wood and the questions might be asked in a language he was learning to speak but hadn't yet decided whether he wanted to use.

The German app chimed again, patient and persistent. Miles looked at it for a long moment, then tucked it into the drawer with the screws and nails, where it could continue its patient instruction in a language he might never need to speak.

Tonight, Lillian would sing about home and staying and growing roots. Tonight, he would listen not just to the melodies but to what they meant, to the conversation they'd been having for years without either of them quite realizing they were speaking in music and maintenance and the careful architecture of daily life.

The shed waited, patient and quiet, while he made his way back toward the house where his family was preparing for an evening that might change everything, or nothing, or something in between that they would only understand later, looking back at this particular afternoon when the light fell just so and the questions hung in the air like pollen, invisible but everywhere.
# Episode 3: Graduation Station

The letter arrived on a Tuesday that smelled like burnt toast and lemon cleaner.

Lillian had just finished waging war on the kitchen counters, wielding a sponge in one paw and a spray bottle in the other like a dual-wielding domestic paladin. The late-summer light slanted through the window above the sink, turning floating dust motes into tiny, indifferent galaxies. Somewhere under the table, Mozzarella snored in the deep, rattling way of former strays who had finally learned that sleep could be trusted.

The knock at the door was a polite little tap-tap instead of the usual full-bodied Ramon hammering or Rosalia’s operatic doorbell ringing. It sounded official. Timid. British.

Miles looked up from where he was crouched near the baseboard, tightening a loose socket plate with the small, precise movements of someone who found comfort in screws and symmetry.

"Expecting anyone?" he asked.

"Always," Lillian replied, tossing the sponge in the sink and drying her paws on her hips. "Whether I like it or not."

She padded down the hallway, fur still slightly damp where she’d splashed herself, silk house-robe hanging open enough that a more self-conscious woman might have adjusted it. Lillian did not adjust. The house adjusted to her.

On the doorstep stood a fox in a sensible cardigan and an expression that suggested she had never once in her life been late for anything important. She held a clipboard, a satchel, and the slightly apologetic smile of a person whose job involved ruining or remaking people’s days with envelopes.

"Mrs Ferguson?" the fox asked.

"Depends who’s asking and if they’ve brought biscuits," Lillian answered.

The fox’s smile twitched. "I’m from the academy. Sarah’s final records and…" She checked her clipboard. "Notification of options. We’re required to deliver them in person."

"Ah." Lillian’s chest loosened and tightened at the same time. "The future, in envelope form. Come in before it catches a draft."

She ushered the woman through the hallway, already calling out. "Sarah! Pup! Your destiny has arrived in triplicate and a sensible cardigan!" 

From upstairs, there came the unmistakable thud of someone leaping off a bed, followed by the muffled curse of someone who had forgotten they were still tangled in their headphones.

Miles met them at the kitchen doorway, wiping his paws on a rag. His fur was smudged with a faint grey streak where he’d leaned against the wall without realising. He caught Lillian’s eyes for a second—just a second—and she felt, underneath her own excitement, a small tremor of something unnamed. He’d been like that lately. Polite. Present. Somewhere else, just around the corner of his gaze.

The fox ran through the formalities. She sat at their small kitchen table, accepted a mug of tea because refusing tea in this house counted as a minor crime, and produced an envelope thick enough to be a novella.

"Sarah Ferguson," she said, sliding it onto the table. "End-of-course portfolio assessment, final remarks, and confirmed place at the Royal London Institute of Applied Arts, should she choose to accept." She smiled, genuinely warm now. "Your daughter is… exceptional."

Lillian, who had always known this, nodded solemnly as if she were hearing it for the first time and wanted to appear modest about it. "She gets that from her father," she said. "And the dramatic flourishes from me."

Miles made a small, self-effacing noise into his mug.

Sarah arrived a moment later, breathless and barefoot, fur puffed slightly from bedhead and anxiety. She wore paint-splattered leggings and a T-shirt that read *Drawn To Trouble*.

For a heartbeat, she froze at the sight of the envelope. Then she recovered, shoulders lifting, jaw setting in that particular way that said she could already feel the lines of a new drawing forming in her mind.

"That’s… it?" she asked.

"That’s it," the fox said kindly. "You don’t have to open it now."

Which, of course, meant everyone would collectively pretend not to hope she opened it right this second.

Lillian slid a paw around Sarah’s shoulders, feeling the solid, living weight of her. "Whenever you like," she said. "Kitchen’s seen everything from first words to failed soufflés. It can handle this." 

Sarah swallowed. "I’ll… take it upstairs. Think first."

Her voice was light, but her tail betrayed her, twitching once before she tucked it closer.

Miles, who knew the language of tails better than most, stepped aside to let her pass. Their eyes met in the doorway—her gaze quick, searching, then gone.

The fox finished her tea, left them a folder full of pamphlets about scholarships, deadlines, and something called "student resilience services," and departed with the brisk efficiency of a weather front moving east.

The kitchen exhaled.

For a moment, none of them moved. The fridge hummed. The old clock above the stove ticked in its slightly irregular rhythm—two short ticks, pause, one long. Somewhere in the pipes, air shifted and made the familiar gurgling complaint that Lillian had once described as "the house doing indigestion."

"Well," Lillian said eventually, clapping her paws together once, too loudly. "This calls for carbs."

"Everything calls for carbs," Miles said automatically.

"Yes, but this calls for celebratory carbs," she countered. "Possibly cinnamon. Definitely obscene amounts of butter."

He watched her move about the kitchen, body finding its old choreography between cupboards and drawers and microwave. She sang under her breath as she went, some half-remembered tune from a gig years ago. He knew that one; it was about train stations and choices and the ache of leaving home while the station stayed exactly where it was.

He thought about a different station. One with departures boards that flicked between *London* and *Berlin*.

He reached for the rag and started wiping a patch of already-clean counter.

"You’re scrubbing that like it insulted your mother," Lillian noted, watching him.

"Just… keeping ahead of the grease," he murmured.

"The grease is already three moves ahead of you, darling. You look like you’re attending a seminar in your own head." She tilted her snout. "You okay?"

He met her eyes for a fraction too long, then looked away. "Just thinking."

"Dangerous habit," she said lightly.

But her voice had softened. She didn’t push. Not yet.

---

Upstairs, Sarah sat cross-legged on her bed, the envelope untouched on the duvet in front of her.

She studied it the way she studied reference photos: angles, shadows, negative space. The part of her brain that always started composing—lines, tones, balance—was already at work, mapping out possibilities.

Open the envelope and find success.

Open the envelope and find *enough*.

Open the envelope and find the kind of failure that would confirm every late-night whisper that lived between her ribs.

On her wall, sketches from the past year watched her silently. Clay figures in charcoal, half-finished cityscapes, the crooked-but-living bodies that had finally started to replace the perfect, hollow sculptures she’d once made. On her desk, a small, battered clay figure of a badger with a paintbrush in one paw and a bowl in the other grinned up at her. Lillian, remade in terracotta and stubborn affection.

Sarah exhaled slowly.

She tugged at the envelope’s corner.

The paper gave with a soft rip.

Downstairs, as if connected by invisible string, Lillian looked up from the pan where sugar and butter were beginning to dance together.

"She’s doing it," she murmured.

Miles didn’t ask how she knew. After all these years, he simply accepted that some things about Lillian hovered firmly in the category of *witchcraft, benevolent*. He listened anyway—ears literally pricking, the faint stiffening of his shoulders betraying how invested he was.

They both stared at the ceiling as if they could see through it.

In Sarah’s room, papers slid free. Her eyes moved left to right, widening and narrowing in turn. There were numbers. Comments. Phrases like *distinctive voice* and *unconventional but compelling* and *would benefit from further technical refinement*.

She flipped to the final page.

Her breath hitched.

Downstairs, Lillian heard the pause and nearly dropped the wooden spoon.

"If they’ve written anything about her ‘potential’ in that horrid vague tone—" she began.

"Lillian," Miles warned.

"—I will march down to that academy and sing them all into emotional breakdowns," she continued. "Every tutor. Every administrator. Even the janitor, for complicity."

"You can’t emotionally blackmail an institution," he said.

"Watch me."

Before she could work herself up further, footsteps sounded on the stairs. Not dragging, not running. A measured, careful descent, as if each tread was an experiment in gravity.

Sarah appeared in the doorway, papers in one paw, envelope in the other.

Her eyes were bright. Unreadable.

For a terrifying moment, Lillian saw her as she’d seen her the first week she’d come to live with them—all bones and big eyes and polite terror. The ghost of that girl still lived in the corner of certain expressions, surfacing like a watermark when life pressed too hard.

"Well?" Lillian said, because patience had never been her primary instrument.

Sarah swallowed. Her tail flicked once.

"I got in," she said.

Silence, for the span of a heartbeat.

Then the kitchen exploded.

"YES!" Lillian roared, so loudly that Mozzarella bolted out from under the table and knocked into a chair.

Miles’ mug slipped from his paw, bounced off the counter, and miraculously didn’t break. His ears flattened with shock and then lifted in helpless, unfiltered pride.

"You—" He cleared his throat. Tried again. "You did it, pup."

Sarah smiled, slow and a little stunned. "My final piece… ‘Mercy’… they said it wasn’t what they expected. But that it felt—" She tapped the paper, as if the word might come loose. "Honest."

Lillian’s paws were already on her, pulling her in, squeezing so tightly that Sarah squeaked.

"Careful," Miles murmured, but he was moving closer, one arm joining the tangle.

"I am being careful," Lillian protested, almost breathless with joy. "I’m carefully crushing our daughter with the weight of generational pride. This is tradition."

Sarah laughed into Lillian’s shoulder. The sound was muffled, but real.

"There’s more," she said when she wriggled free enough to breathe. "The place is… it’s there. Waiting. But there’s something else."

She unfolded another page. This one bore the logo of a different institution. Not the posh, stone-fronted building near the Thames they’d all seen on prospectuses, but something smaller, stranger.

"There’s a partnership with a studio residency," she explained. "In Brighton. Coastal stuff, community projects, art therapy workshops. They offered me a place for a year before the institute. If I want it."

The word *Brighton* hung in the air like an unexpected chord.

Lillian blinked. "Brighton is…" She gestured vaguely southwards, as if the kitchen walls might peel back to reveal the sea. "Lovely. Full of gulls and questionable tattoos."

"And two hours away by train," Miles said quietly.

Sarah heard the not-quite-question in his tone.

"It’s not forever," she said quickly. "It’s… a bridge. A year to figure out who I am when I’m not being graded every week. They do community stuff. Workshops with kids. Art as…" She fumbled. "As… care."

Something in Lillian’s chest squeezed. Food as care, music as care, now art as care—her girl had collected gentlenesses the way other people collected degree certificates.

"You want to do it," Lillian stated, not asked.

Sarah hesitated. That was her tell, more than any tail twitch. She went still when something mattered.

"I think so," she said at last. "Part of me doesn’t want to leave. Obviously. But another part—"

"Wants to see if you can be you somewhere that isn’t this kitchen," Miles supplied.

She nodded, grateful.

They stood in a triangle of silence around the kitchen table. On it lay the papers that represented years of worry, work, late-night sketching, breakdowns over portfolios, and cautious reassembly. Outside the window, the garden shimmered in the early afternoon light—the roses Miles had planted nodding in the faint breeze.

Somewhere in the shed, unseen from here, a tablet lay silent in a drawer, its language app notification badges frozen at day twenty-three.

---

They decided, without quite saying it, to walk to the station.

"You don’t need a station to read a letter," Rosalia pointed out later, when they told her the story over dinner.

"Of course you do," Lillian had replied. "It’s called thematic resonance."

For now, though, it was just the three of them.

The day had settled into that late-summer warmth that wrapped London in a kind of nostalgic haze, as if the city were remembering every other summer all at once. The pavements on the way to Highbury & Islington were uneven in familiar ways; Miles knew exactly which paving stones wobbled, which curb edges caught feet. He steered them around obstacles like a quiet tugboat.

Sarah held the acceptance letter rolled in one paw, the residency brochure in the other. Every so often, she tapped the two ends together as if checking they still existed.

"You don’t have to decide today," Miles said.

"I know." Tap. Tap.

"Or this week. Or this month." Tap. Tap.

"I know," she repeated, a little more firmly.

Lillian walked on her other side, pacing herself a half-step slower than usual. The old Lillian stride—a full-throated, hip-swinging declaration—had mellowed over the years into something still powerful but more measured. Age wasn’t a thief, she’d decided. It was just a different costume designer.

"When I was your age," she said, because it was legally required of all parents at times like these, "no one gave me an envelope telling me what I was allowed to become."

"Because you were too busy turning dive bars into churches," Miles murmured.

"Exactly," she said, unoffended.

Sarah smiled. "Did you ever want that?" she asked. "A letter. A plan."

Lillian thought about it.

"Sometimes," she admitted. "On nights when the gig paid in drink tickets and bus fare, and the only people clapping were half asleep or half something else. I would’ve loved a piece of paper that said, ‘Congratulations, you are officially Enough.’" She shrugged. "But then I might’ve believed whoever signed it had more authority than I did over my own life. So perhaps it’s good no one tried."

They reached the station entrance. The glass façade reflected them back in faint, distorted lines—badger, shepherd, retriever, three shapes overlapping, stretching, contracting.

Inside, the air smelled of metal, coffee, and the complicated perfume of hundreds of passing lives. The departure boards clicked softly overhead, trains rolling in and out of the future.

"We’re not going anywhere," Sarah said, suddenly self-conscious.

"We are always going somewhere," Lillian corrected gently. "Sometimes it’s just a short trip from one thought to another."

They descended to the Overground platforms, following the familiar orange line signs. At this hour, the platform wasn’t crowded. A few commuters scrolled on their phones. A child in a pram stared at Sarah’s tail with open fascination.

They sat on one of the metal benches, the kind that seemed scientifically engineered to prevent comfortable lounging.

"So," Lillian said. "Tell us what’s in your head. Both versions." 

"Both?" Sarah echoed.

"You have at least two films running," Miles said, backing his wife’s instinct with quiet certainty. "Version A: you stay. Go straight into the institute. Commute, probably. Version B: you spend a year in Brighton. Then the institute. What do they look like?"

Sarah looked down at the letters, then out at the tracks.

"Version A," she began slowly, "I live at home. I take the tube. I draw in studios with high ceilings and professors who say things like ‘interrogate the line’. I come back here every night. We eat dinner at this table. I know which floorboard squeaks. I know which cupboard sticks. If I mess up a piece, I can throw it across my bedroom and you won’t call the police." 

"Generous of us," Lillian said.

"Version B…" Sarah hesitated. "I share a tiny flat with three strangers who leave hair in the sink. I buy groceries that don’t automatically refill. I forget laundry in the machine and have to rewash it. I go to the studio and there are people who’ve never heard of Penny or Ramon or… you." She swallowed. "I walk to the sea when I’m stuck. I teach clay workshops to kids who don’t know me yet. I… find out who I am when I’m not defined by this house." She nodded toward them, eyes bright. "When I introduce myself and don’t have to explain how I got here."

Miles listened, the muscles around his eyes tightening. He thought about his own Version B—the one that began with boarding a plane in London and ended with a flat in Berlin where no one knew how many times he’d repainted this house’s hallway.

"Both versions sound like you," he said. "Just… tuned to different keys."

Sarah huffed a soft laugh. "You’ve been talking to Mum’s music metaphors too much."

"Contagious," he agreed.

A train rolled in, all orange stripes and squealing brakes. Doors opened in a hiss of air. People disgorged, merged into the platform’s currents, disappeared up the stairs. Others boarded, claimed temporary territories on plastic seats.

The three of them sat still, an island in the flow.

"When you think about Brighton," Lillian asked quietly, "are you excited-scared, or just scared-scared?"

Sarah considered.

"Both," she admitted. "But more the first." 

"Then that’s your answer," Lillian said, almost immediately.

Miles blinked. "That’s… it?" he asked.

"That’s not science," Sarah added, half-teasing.

Lillian spread her paws. "I am not a lab report. I am a woman who’s flown across continents for gigs that paid in exposure and sandwiches. The good kind of fear—the kind that means you might grow—is fizzy. In the ribs. Like someone shook a bottle of lemonade in your chest. The bad kind is heavy. Sits in your stomach like a rock. You learn the difference." She shrugged. "If Brighton feels fizzy, then we start pricing train tickets and terrible student mattresses."

Sarah went quiet, checking herself. Ribcage. Stomach.

"Fizzy," she said at last, almost surprised. "It’s… fizzy. And heavy. But the heavy part feels like… missing you. Not dread."

"Missing we can handle," Lillian said, throat tight.

Miles stared out at the tracks. The next train announcement crackled overhead, the electronic voice pronouncing place names with politically correct precision.

*Brighton* wasn’t on the board today. But *Berlin* might as well have been stamped on the backs of his eyelids.

"What about you?" Sarah asked suddenly, turning to him. "What were your version A and B? When you were my age."

He smiled, bemused. "I was never as ambitious as you," he said. "Version A was, ‘Get a job that pays for rent and petrol.’ Version B was, ‘Try to build a motorbike that runs on compressed air and the power of disappointment.’"

"You could still build that," Lillian said. "Call it the Midlife Crisis."

"Limited market," he replied.

But he felt the weight of the question all the same. Because these days, his versions had names.

Version A: Stay. Keep fixing chairs and pipes and the quiet fissures that sometimes ran through late-night conversations. Grow the garden. Grow old in this house that knew his footsteps.

Version B: Go. Berlin. A lab with state-of-the-art equipment and colleagues who spoke the language of engineering with the same fluency Lillian used for music. A salary that could fix more than leaky roofs.

He could already feel the language learning pathways in his brain, lit up like new wiring.

"I think," he said slowly, choosing each word as if it were a component that might mis-thread, "that at some point you realise both versions are still you. You don’t escape yourself just because the postcode changes."

Lillian glanced at him. There it was again—that flicker. The way he said *postcode* like it was doing more work than four syllables had any right to.

"Deep," she murmured. "Look at us. Having an emotionally resonant conversation on public transport infrastructure."

"Very on brand," Sarah said.

Another train came and went. A gust of warm air tugged at the edges of Sarah’s papers, rattling the residency brochure.

She flattened it with her paw.

"I think I want to go," she said quietly. "To Brighton. Just for the year. Then here for the institute. I want to…" She gestured helplessly at the tracks. "I want to know I chose it. Not that it was the only thing that ever happened to me."

Lillian closed her eyes for a moment. When she opened them, they shone, but her voice was steady.

"Then that’s what we’ll do," she said. "We will buy far too many tote bags and whatever passes for a duvet in student accommodation, and we will pretend we’re not going to text you every hour for the first week."

"Please don’t," Sarah said, horrified.

"Every *other* hour," Lillian amended.

"Better," Sarah conceded.

Miles didn’t trust himself to speak immediately. He swallowed around a lump the size of a cricket ball.

"We’ll make checklists," he said eventually, defaulting to competence. "Bank accounts. Emergency contacts. Nearest walk-in clinic. Toolkit."

"Toolkit?" Sarah echoed.

"I’m not sending you into the world without a decent set of screwdrivers," he said, as if this were self-evident.

She smiled, relief and dread and excitement tumbling in her expression.

A train to Crystal Palace arrived. A woman pushed a bike onboard. A man with headphones bobbed his head to silent music. Two teenagers argued cheerfully about crisps.

The world did not pause to congratulate them on their decision.

But something inside the three of them aligned. Not a perfect fit—there were still gaps, aches, questions—but close enough that, for a moment, the platform felt like an extension of their kitchen. Just with worse seating.

---

The day they got home, Ramon and Rosalia were waiting on the front steps with a bottle of prosecco and a banner that read *CONGRADULATIONS*.

"It was on sale," Rosalia said defensively, catching Lillian’s raised brow. "I am not paying full price to spell in English."

"You absolute menaces," Lillian said, fond.

Sarah flushed as they bundled her into hugs, demands for details, and arguments about whether Brighton was full of artists or simply full.

Miles slipped inside under cover of the chaos. The house felt cooler, shadows stretching a little longer along the hallway. He went straight to the shed before he could talk himself out of it.

Inside, the air smelled of sawdust and the faint metallic tang of tools. His notebook lay open where he’d left it, a list of household jobs on one page, a neat column of German vocabulary on the other.

He picked it up.

For a long moment, he stood there between the two columns—the things he’d built here, the words that belonged elsewhere.

Then he took a pencil and, with careful strokes, drew a line between them.

*Haus*.

Home.

*Familie*.

Family.

Some words meant the same thing no matter where you lived.

He closed the notebook and set it aside. The tablet still lay in its drawer, the app waiting patiently, yellow icon bright and insistent.

He didn’t open it.

Not yet.

From the house, he heard Lillian’s voice rise in mock-outrage, Sarah’s laughter, Ramon’s theatrical gasp. The familiar soundtrack of his life, playing in a key he knew better than any language course could teach.

He stepped out into the garden, pausing to touch one of the roses Sarah had liked best. Its label caught the light.

*Lasting Love*.

He smiled, faint and private.

Whatever choices lay ahead—for Sarah, for him, for all of them—the tracks didn’t just lead away. They also circled back, over and over, to this patch of earth. This kitchen. This station of a house where people arrived, departed, and, more often than not, stayed just a little longer than they’d planned.

Inside, the kettle began to whistle. He turned toward home.
# Episode 4: The Garden Shed

The shed had always been an eyesore.

Even in the flattering light of early evening, it managed to look both defeated and mildly accusatory, like a structure that resented still existing.

Once, long before they’d moved in, it might have been useful. A place for tools, for bikes, for spiders to pursue fulfilling careers.

Now, the roof sagged in the middle. The door hung slightly off its hinges, giving it a permanent expression of surprise. One side panel had developed a worrying lean, as if it were trying to sidle away from the rest of the garden.

Lillian stood at the back door, mug of tea in paw, and squinted at it.

“It’s you,” she said.

From behind her, Miles made a small, polite noise.

“I’m sorry?”

“You,” she repeated, nodding her snout toward the offending structure. “That’s what your insides look like right now.”

He came to stand beside her, shoulders almost brushing hers, and followed her gaze.

“Rotting?” he asked mildly. “Hosting wasps?”

“Precarious,” she said. “Threatening to fall over at any moment and crush an unsuspecting geranium.”

He considered this.

“I feel I’m holding together a bit better than that,” he said.

“That’s only because you moisturise,” she said. “Don’t get cocky.”

He huffed a quiet laugh.

The garden itself was, by their standards, in good shape. The grass was mostly under control. The raised beds they’d put in two summers ago were full of herbs and the odd ambitious tomato plant. Sarah’s terracotta pots lined the little path, each containing something in various stages of thriving.

And then, at the end of it all, like a punchline, sat the shed.

“We could knock it down,” Lillian said, sipping her tea. “Make space for a hot tub. Or a trampoline. Or a shrine to cheese.”

“We’re not getting a hot tub,” Miles said automatically.

“Why?”

“Because we’d never leave it,” he said. “We’d prune away like overcooked tortellini. And also the neighbours would complain about the steam.”

“The neighbours are literally planning to move to the seaside,” she pointed out. “Let them have one last scandal.”

He didn’t answer.

He rarely answered, these days, when conversation skidded too close to departures.

She side-eyed him.

“Or,” she said carefully, “we could rebuild it. Properly. Turn it into something useful. A studio. A workshop. A panic room for when Sarah shows us her overdraft.”

His ears twitched.

“A… studio,” he repeated.

“Or whatever,” she said, feeling suddenly exposed, as if she’d just suggested they build a second house on a whim. “Just… it seems silly, doesn’t it? All that space. Wasted on mildew and memories of ten Christmases ago.”

He rested one paw on the doorframe, the wood familiar under his pads.

The Berlin email pulsed at the back of his brain like a notification he refused to swipe away.

“I’ll take a look at it,” he said.

“At the email?” she asked, too quick.

“At the shed,” he said.

She made a face.

“Same thing,” she muttered.

He pretended not to hear.

---

The shed, up close, smelled of damp wood and old dust.

Miles stepped inside and the floor protested with a creak that was just shy of alarming.

Sunlight slid in through a crack in the roof, catching on suspended motes. An old rake leaned drunkenly in one corner. A cardboard box labelled *MISC* sagged under the weight of things no one had missed in years.

He ran a paw along the inside wall.

Rot.

Not everywhere. Not yet. But in the places that mattered.

“You and me both, mate,” he said under his breath.

From the doorway, Sarah’s voice floated in.

“Are you talking to the fungus again?”

He turned.

Sarah stood there, sketchbook tucked under one arm, phone in the other. Mozzarella hovered at her feet, tail wagging cautiously, nose twitching.

“It’s wood rot, actually,” Miles said. “Different organism. Same principle.”

“Very comforting,” she said. “Mum said you were communing.”

“I’m assessing,” he said.

She peered in.

“So?”

He sighed.

“So,” he said, “we either ignore it until it collapses in dramatic fashion, or we… rebuild it.”

Sarah stepped over the threshold gingerly.

“Rebuild,” she said. “Obviously. We’re a narrative-driven family.”

He smiled despite himself.

“It’s not a small job,” he warned. “We’d have to strip it back. Replace panels. Maybe redo the foundation.”

She shrugged.

“You love all those words,” she said. “Strip. Replace. Foundation. They’re, like, your favourite verbs.”

Mozzarella sniffed a suspicious patch of wall, then sneezed.

Miles watched the dust settle.

“It might be nice,” Sarah added, softer. “To have… a place. Out here. For… stuff.”

She waved the sketchbook.

“For your art,” he said.

“For your… thinking,” she countered.

He looked at her.

She held his gaze, clear and steady.

She knew something was up. Of course she did. She’d always been able to read the currents under their calm surface.

“Let me draw it before you murder it,” she said lightly, stepping back into the garden.

He watched her settle on the grass, cross-legged, pencil moving quickly.

Her first sketch captured the shed as it was: skewed, sagging, slightly tragic.

The second was… different.

The same shape, but straightened. A new roof. A window that actually opened. Light spilling out.

He felt something in his chest tilt toward that second drawing.

“I’ll make a list,” he said.

“Of materials?” she asked.

He nodded.

“Of excuses not to go to Berlin,” Lillian would have said, if she’d been there.

He stepped fully inside the shed.

The floor groaned again.

“All right,” he told it. “Let’s see what we can save.”

---

The next week became, unofficially, Shed Week.

Every day after work, Miles changed out of his shirt and into his oldest, paint-flecked trousers. He disappeared into the garden with a toolbox and a look of quiet determination.

Lillian watched from the kitchen window, mug in paw, pretending she wasn’t watching.

“You know,” Penny said one afternoon, leaning against the counter as if she lived there, “for someone allegedly torn about leaving the country, he’s putting a lot of energy into reinforcing the property value.”

“Do not psychoanalyse my husband,” Lillian said. “That’s my job.”

Penny shrugged.

“I’m just saying,” she went on, “this is a very ‘I’m staying’ project. Foundations. Timber. Sweat equity. It’s giving… roots.”

Lillian scowled at the word.

“He hasn’t decided,” she said, more to herself than to Penny.

Outside, Miles carefully pried off a rotten plank, revealing the frame beneath.

He nodded, satisfied, and measured the gap.

“He has,” Penny said, watching him.

“Shut up,” Lillian said, without heat.

Penny did, for once.

But she kept watching.

---

Rebuilding the shed was, in many ways, the exact kind of problem Miles understood.

There were measurements. Angles. Loads to calculate. Weatherproofing to consider.

He made a plan.

He bought timber, brackets, screws of appropriate length (Ramon approved these at the fence, nodding gravely). He laid out his tools in neat rows on a makeshift workbench.

He took photos of the existing structure, sketched out the changes he wanted, then began dismantling.

Each piece that came off — a warped plank, a rusted hinge, a spiderweb-thickened shelf — eased something inside him.

He couldn’t dismantle the Berlin offer so easily. It sat in his inbox, subject line bold, patiently waiting for an answer.

But he could take down a rotten wall.

He could replace it with something solid.

Sarah joined him when she wasn’t working or drawing or messaging Clare.

She held boards in place while he drilled, passing screws without being asked.

“You’re very calm,” she remarked one evening, as he levelled a new beam.

“That’s the aim,” he said.

“No, I mean… about this,” she said, gesturing around. “Most people would be… stressed.”

He chuckled.

“Most people don’t take apart sheds for fun,” he said.

“You do it for emotional regulation,” she said.

He paused.

“I don’t… disagree,” he admitted.

She smiled.

“It’s nice,” she said. “Seeing you build something that isn’t… us.”

He looked at her, startled.

“What do you mean?”

She shrugged, considering.

“You’ve always been… fixing,” she said slowly. “The house. The boiler. Mum’s mood. My…

She waved a paw vaguely at herself.

“Everything,” she finished. “It’s like you think if you sand enough edges, nothing will ever hurt.”

He tightened his grip on the drill.

“Is that… bad?” he asked.

She shook her head.

“It’s lovely,” she said. “It’s saved us more times than you know. I just… I like watching you build something because you want to. Not because something’s broken.”

He swallowed.

“This was broken,” he said hoarsely.

“The wood was,” she agreed. “You weren’t.”

He looked away, blinking.

“Pass me the screws,” he said.

She did.

Later, she drew that moment: his back bent over the frame, the late light catching the fur along his neck, the line of concentration between his brows.

In the corner of the page, she scribbled: *Stewardship.*

She wasn’t entirely sure what it meant yet.

But it felt right.

---

The day the new roof went on, the sky threatened rain.

“Typical,” Lillian muttered, watching dark clouds gather as Miles and Ramon wrestled a large, awkward panel into place.

Rosalia leaned on the fence, arms folded, providing moral support and unsolicited advice.

“Angle it more,” she called. “You want the water to run off, not sit.”

“Yes, dear,” Ramon called back, rolling his eyes.

Miles, balanced on the ladder, adjusted his grip.

“Please don’t die,” Lillian called. “I’m not emotionally prepared to be a widow. I haven’t got the wardrobe for it.”

He glanced down long enough to smile.

“I’m fine,” he said.

“Famous last words,” she replied.

Sarah sat on the back step, sketchbook on her knees, capturing the scene: two men, one ladder, one increasingly concerned badger, one amused Spanish-Italian chorus.

The first fat drops of rain hit the new felt just as Miles secured the final nail.

He stepped down, flexing his paws.

“Done,” he said.

Lillian exhaled as if she’d been holding her breath for an hour.

“Good,” she said. “Now if the sky falls, at least it will bounce.”

They all retreated to the kitchen as the drizzle turned into proper rain.

Condensation bloomed on the window as the kettle boiled.

“So,” Ramon said, cradling his mug. “What is it going to be?”

“What is what going to be?” Miles asked.

“The shed,” Rosalia said. “Is it a man cave? A music palace? A storage facility for your unresolved emotions?”

Lillian snorted.

“That last one doesn’t have enough insulation,” she said.

Miles stared into his tea.

“I’m not sure yet,” he said.

“Baby,” Rosalia said. “That is the fun part.”

Sarah watched him carefully.

“You don’t have to know now,” she said. “You can… see what it wants to be.”

Lillian’s ears twitched.

“Are we talking about the shed or your father’s midlife crisis?” she asked.

“Yes,” Sarah and Miles said in unison.

They all laughed.

Outside, the new roof held.

Rain slid neatly off its edge, landing in the flowerbed where the roses drank deeply.

---

At night, when the others were asleep, Miles sometimes slipped out to the garden.

The shed — not quite finished, but structurally sound — loomed at the end of the lawn like a promise.

He’d put in one small window on the side, high enough for privacy, low enough to let in light.

He stood there now, paws in his pockets, and watched the faint glow from the kitchen paint the grass.

His phone buzzed in his pocket.

Berlin.

Again.

He didn’t open it.

Instead, he stepped inside the shed.

The new floor didn’t creak.

He inhaled the smell of fresh wood and sawdust.

He imagined shelves along one wall. A workbench. Maybe, if he let himself think it, a tiny mixing desk. A place for Sarah to store canvases. A corner where Lillian could rehearse without waking the neighbours.

Or it could just be a very fancy box for tools.

Both options appealed to different parts of him.

He sat on an upturned crate and let his back rest against the cool wall.

In the quiet, he could hear the house.

The muffled hum of the fridge. The faint tick of the hallway clock. Lillian’s soft snore when she’d drifted onto her back.

He thought of Berlin’s hum instead: trains, trams, a language that wasn’t his.

He had loved that sound once.

He still did, in theory.

But his body, pressed against this wall, responded more to the radiator’s familiar groan.

He took out his phone.

He opened the email.

He read the offer again.

He imagined writing, *Thank you, but…*

He didn’t write it.

Not yet.

Instead, he typed a different line.

> Could I have a little more time to consider?

He stared at it.

Then deleted it.

He put the phone away.

“Coward,” he told himself, not unkindly.

The shed said nothing.

But its new beams, solid and patient, seemed to offer him a different interpretation.

Maybe leaving a door open a little longer wasn’t cowardice.

Maybe it was… care.

For them. For him. For this place.

He stayed there until the chill seeped into his bones, then went back inside.

The house welcomed him with its usual chorus: creaks, sighs, a dog lifting its head and thumping its tail once in recognition.

He slipped into bed.

Lillian muttered something incomprehensible and rolled toward him.

“How’s your little thinking box?” she mumbled.

He smiled into the dark.

“Sturdy,” he whispered.

“You too,” she said, already half back asleep.

He lay awake a little longer, listening to her breathing, feeling the weight of the roof over their heads, the new roof at the end of the garden, the sky over both.

For the first time in weeks, the thought of staying didn’t feel like something he was failing to do.

It felt like something he was building.

---

On Sunday, they painted.

“Not white,” Lillian decreed, hands on hips as Miles laid out dust sheets.

“White is classic,” he said.

“White is boring,” she countered. “White is ‘we might move out at any moment.’ We are not moving out at any moment. We are… aggressively present.”

Sarah, already spattered with test colours, snorted.

“Aggressively present is my new band name,” she said.

They compromised on a soft, warm green for the interior walls, the same shade as the kitchen but a little lighter.

“Continuity,” Sarah said approvingly, rolling paint in long strokes.

Rosalia contributed a string of fairy lights.

“Housewarming,” she said, even though it wasn’t a house.

Ramon brought an old rug that didn’t fit their new place but fit perfectly in the shed.

“You can spill paint on this without crying,” he said.

Penny appeared with a framed print that read *BREATHE, IDIOT* in tasteful lettering.

“For ambience,” she said.

By late afternoon, the shed — the new shed, the not-just-a-shed — had transformed.

The walls glowed softly in the slanting light.

The rug took the chill off the floor.

Shelves waited for whatever they decided to put on them.

It didn’t quite know what it was yet.

Neither did they.

But standing in the doorway, shoulder to shoulder, Lillian and Miles both felt it:

This was a space that assumed a future here.

“We should name it,” Lillian said.

“It’s a shed,” Miles said.

“Ships get names. Cats get names. Blogs get names,” she said. “This deserves at least as much as Penny’s mindfulness newsletter.”

“Hey,” Penny said mildly from the corner.

“The Bark Box,” Ramon suggested.

“Too postal,” Rosalia said.

“The Den,” Sarah said quietly.

They all looked at her.

“You know,” she said, cheeks flushing. “Because… it’s where we go. To… den.”

There was a beat of silence.

“I love it,” Lillian said.

Miles nodded.

“The Den,” he repeated.

The word fit, somehow.

Like the last piece of a panel sliding into place.

He looked at the walls he’d rebuilt.

At the beams he’d measured twice, cut once.

At his family, gathered in this small, green, hopeful box at the end of their garden.

Berlin buzzed faintly in his pocket.

He didn’t reach for it.

Instead, he reached for Lillian’s paw.

“Welcome home,” she said.

He squeezed her fingers.

“Yeah,” he said. “I think so.”

# Episode 5: The Potluck Plot

The first sign that the universe was up to something came in the form of a group chat.

The second sign was that Ramon had named it.

The notification pinged in the middle of a quiet Saturday morning, when the house was steeped in that particular kind of domestic peace that made Miles suspicious. The kitchen was full of soft noises—the kettle just beginning to murmur on the hob, the low rustle of Sarah’s sketchbook pages, the rhythmic tick of the old clock above the stove. Lillian hummed in a register just under her breath, the melody threading through the clink of mugs.

Miles was halfway through tightening the hinge on the under-sink cupboard when his phone buzzed on the table.

He wiped his paws on a tea towel and checked the screen.

"What’s exploded?" Lillian asked without turning around.

"Nothing," he said automatically. Then, more cautiously: "Yet."

She glanced over her shoulder, one brow arched. He held up the phone.

"New group chat," he said. "Ramon started it."

Sarah looked up from her drawing. "What did he call it?" she asked, because when you knew Ramon, this was never a neutral question.

Miles read the name aloud. "‘Operation Culinary Diplomacy’."

The three of them stared at one another.

"Oh, no," Lillian said. "He’s organising feelings. With food."

"That’s your job," Sarah pointed out.

"Exactly," Lillian replied. "Territorial incursion."

Miles opened the chat. A flurry of messages greeted him.

> RAMON: Darlings! Emergency potluck! Our place tonight? (Well, technically not "our" for much longer but we’ll get to that xx)
>
> ROSALIA: DO NOT WRITE LIKE THAT IN GROUP CHATS
>
> ROSALIA: And it’s not an *emergency*.
>
> ROSALIA: (It is important.)
>
> RAMON: Fine. Important potluck! Bring something that says "home".
>
> RAMON: @Miles you bring those obscene brownies or I riot
>
> ROSALIA: You are banned from rioting in postal code N5, remember?
>
> RAMON: Technicalities

Miles felt his stomach sink in a way that had nothing to do with brownies.

"Trouble?" Lillian asked, padding closer to read over his shoulder.

He tipped the screen so she could see. Her mouth tightened at the parenthetical—*not "our" for much longer*—but she said nothing, yet.

Sarah wiped graphite from her paw onto her jeans. "Potluck could just mean Ramon’s bored," she offered. "Or that he’s invented a new lasagne and needs victims."

"He calls those ‘focus groups’," Lillian said faintly.

She accepted the phone when Miles passed it to her, rereading the messages more slowly. The words *for much longer* glowed in her mind like a faulty neon sign.

"We’ll go," she said finally. "Obviously. With brownies. And a dish that says ‘home’."

"What dish is that, exactly?" Miles asked.

"I don’t know yet," she replied. "But it will involve butter."

She typed back with one paw, mug in the other.

> LILLIAN: We’ll be there. Prepare your intestinal tracts for glory.
>
> MILES: I’ll bring brownies.
>
> SARAH: I can bring… something yellow.
>
> RAMON: MY SUNSHINE CHILD! xx
>
> ROSALIA: Please not the egg thing you made last time.
>
> SARAH: It was experimental.
>
> ROSALIA: It was a crime.

The kettle clicked off. Somewhere in the garden, a train rumbled past on the distant line. For a moment, life slipped back into its familiar grooves.

But the words hung between them, turning the air slightly thicker.

*Not "our" for much longer.*

---

The day filled itself with ordinary tasks—the ones that were the bones of their life, invisible until you looked closely.

Miles fixed the under-sink leak he’d been pretending not to notice for a week. He wiped up the thin ring of damp that had been quietly staining the cupboard’s wooden floor. It felt good to set something right that had a clear before and after.

Lillian practised for her Camden set, low and careful, saving her voice. She ran through the garden song Sarah loved—a melody about roots and windows and the way light behaved in their kitchen—and then set the guitar aside to measure butter and sugar.

"If Ramon wants something that says ‘home’," she declared, "he’s getting a casserole that could legally qualify as a blanket."

"That sounds… heavy," Sarah said.

"If your friends aren’t in danger of slipping into a carbohydrate coma after your potluck, do you even love them?" Lillian asked.

She opened the spice cupboard and regarded the shelves with a general’s tactical gaze.

Sarah drifted between them, half in her own world. She sketched the way the steam curled from the pot, the angle of Miles’s shoulders as he bent under the sink, the crease between Lillian’s brows when she tasted the sauce and found it lacking in something intangible.

Her phone buzzed twice in her pocket.

She checked the first message: a meme from a college friend about art school tutors and the word "rigour." She smiled, thumbed back a laughing emoji.

The second was an email notification. The preview showed only a line.

*FOLLOW-UP: Regarding prior contact from your biological family…*

Her throat tightened. She locked the screen and shoved the phone back into her pocket, as if it had burned her.

"You okay, pup?" Lillian asked, padding closer to read over her shoulder.

"Fine," Sarah lied.

"You stirred the sauce like it insulted your ancestors," Lillian observed.

"It had lumps," Sarah countered.

"It has character," Lillian corrected. "It’s like us."

Sarah huffed a laugh despite herself.

"Help me grate cheese," Lillian commanded, mercifully changing the subject.

They fell into the easy choreography of working side by side. Cheese, sauce, layers of roasted vegetables, pasta sheets. A domestic architecture.

Miles, on his knees under the sink, checked the new seal one last time and sat back. Water ran where it should, stayed where it should, obeyed the laws he laid down for it.

If only job offers and neighbours and daughters and futures could be sorted with the right washer.

---

By early evening, the house smelled of baked cheese, chocolate, and a low thrum of nerves.

The lasagne-casserole hybrid—Lillian refused to call it "lasagnole" but the name lurked—rested on the counter, bubbling gently at the edges. A tray of brownies cooled beside it, glossy and cracked in all the right places.

"If these don’t secure us emotional stability, nothing will," Lillian said.

"That’s not how stability works," Miles replied, sliding the brownies into a tin.

"In this house, it is," she said.

Sarah hovered by the hallway mirror, fussing with a scarf she didn’t really need.

"It’s just next door," Miles said gently. "You look fine."

"I know," she said. "I just… want to look like I belong in their news."

He frowned. "What news?"

She hesitated, then shrugged it off. "Whatever they’re going to say."

He held her gaze a beat longer than usual, but let it go. The hallway clock chimed the quarter hour.

"Right," Lillian said. "Let’s go be diplomatically culinarily ambushed."

They stepped out into the soft, blue-tinted light of early evening. Next door’s windows glowed warm, silhouettes moving behind curtains. Laughter drifted through the thin summer air.

Lillian balanced the casserole dish like an offering. Miles carried the brownie tin and a bottle of red wine he’d been saving for "something." Sarah clutched a bowl of lemon-dressed potatoes with a concentration that suggested she was more afraid of dropping her contribution than of whatever conversation lay ahead.

"If anyone says the word ‘journey’ in an emotional context," Lillian muttered as they reached the doorstep, "I’m leaving."

"You can’t leave," Sarah whispered. "You’re bringing the main course."

"Fine. I’ll emotionally storm out to the kitchen and reorganise their cutlery drawer instead."

Miles rang the bell.

The door flew open before the last chime had faded.

"My favourite neighbours!" Ramon cried, throwing his arms wide.

"We’re your only neighbours on this side," Miles pointed out.

"Irrelevant," Ramon said, sweeping them into the hallway with a flourish that almost dislodged the casserole from Lillian’s paws.

The house smelled of garlic, roasted peppers, and expensive candle. The dining table was already laid, a chaotic collage of mismatched plates and carefully folded napkins. Several covered dishes sat in the middle, steaming gently.

"You outdid yourself," Lillian said.

"I am outdoing myself," Ramon corrected. "The night is young."

Rosalia appeared from the kitchen, wiping her hands on a tea towel.

"You came," she said simply, and for a moment the noise fell away. There was a softness in her eyes that made Lillian’s chest ache.

"You said ‘important’," Lillian replied. "We may be many things, but we are not emotionally illiterate."

"Speak for yourself," Ramon said. "I am fluent only in melodrama."

He helped them unload their offerings onto the table, narrating each dish like an MC.

"Miles’ Brownies of Moral Fortitude! Lillian’s… edible embrace! Sarah’s…"

He paused, lifting the lid off her bowl.

"Potatoes," Sarah supplied dryly.

"Potatoes," he repeated reverently. "Simple. Honest. Like the core of your soul."

"Stop flirting with our daughter," Miles said mildly.

"I flirt with concepts, not people," Ramon replied. "Potato is a concept."

They settled around the table. For a while, there was only the clatter and murmur of a normal evening—passing plates, trading recipes, small jokes. Lillian watched everyone with the practiced eye of a performer reading a crowd.

Ramon’s jokes were slightly louder than usual. Rosalia’s smile was a fraction too fixed. When conversation lulled, their gazes flicked toward each other, then away.

The announcement, when it finally came, arrived not with a toast but with a sigh.

"We have news," Rosalia said, as Miles was cutting into the first slice of lasagnole.

The knife stalled halfway.

"Here we go," Lillian murmured.

Ramon cleared his throat.

"We’ve been thinking," he began.

"Always dangerous," Lillian said softly, earning herself a small smile from Sarah.

"About change," he continued. "About… second acts."

Miles set the knife down carefully. "Is this about the podcast? If you’re moving it to video, I refuse to be in the background again."

"No, darling," Ramon said, a little sadly. "It’s about the house."

The house. Not *our* house. Not necessarily *this* house.

Rosalia folded her hands together on the table, knuckles pale.

"We’re selling," she said.

The word landed like a dropped plate.

For a moment, no one spoke. The clock in the hallway ticked too loudly.

"Selling?" Lillian echoed.

"You’re… moving?" Sarah asked.

"We’ve been offered a place closer to Rosalia’s parents," Ramon said quickly. "Near the coast. A little terrace with a garden that doesn’t flood every other winter. And—" He gestured helplessly. "Her mother is not… getting younger."

"None of us are," Lillian said, but there was no heat in it.

Rosalia’s mouth quirked. "You are ageless chaos. The rest of us are mortal."

"When?" Miles asked, voice quiet.

"We don’t have a date," Rosalia said. "We haven’t even put it on the market. We wanted to tell you before any ‘For Sale’ sign appeared like some… omen." She shuddered.

"We would never desert you without proper theatrical build-up," Ramon added.

"Comforting," Miles said.

Lillian stared at the tablecloth, tracing the printed pattern with a claw.

"So this is… a farewell dinner," she said.

"No!" Ramon said. "God, no. This is a… prelude. A prologue."

"Ramon wants a moving musical number," Rosalia explained. "We are still negotiating."
