"I will not be moving boxes without choreography," he insisted.

Sarah swallowed. "You’re really going," she said, more statement than question.

"We haven’t signed anything," Rosalia said.

"But you’ve decided," Miles said. He knew the sound. The Berlin offer echoed in his ears.

Ramon spread his hands. "We’ve… begun to decide. Which is sometimes the same thing."

Lillian’s chest hurt. She had known, in the abstract, that lives changed. People moved. Neighbours came and went, like train carriages rattling past a platform.

But this house next door had been a constant longer than most things in her life. It had been the backdrop to a hundred arguments, a thousand shared meals, a million in-jokes. Ramon and Rosalia’s windows were as much a part of her mental map of "home" as her own front door.

"I’m happy for you," she said, eventually.

"You don’t sound happy," Ramon observed gently.

"That’s because I am also selfish," she replied. "Both things can be true."

He reached across the table and squeezed her paw.

"I will miss you like oxygen," he said.

"We’re not dead," Rosalia interjected.

"Yet," Lillian added.

They all laughed, a small, wobbly sound that made the room feel less like an emptying train carriage.

"You’ll visit," Sarah said. It wasn’t quite a question.

"Of course," Rosalia said.

"You have not lived until you’ve seen me attempt paddle-boarding," Ramon added.

"I refuse," Miles said.

"You’ll all come to stay," Rosalia continued, as if listing normal future plans could make them so. "We’ll cook. You’ll laugh at our tiny kitchen. Ramon will injure himself on a folding chair. The usual."

Lillian inhaled slowly, exhaled more slowly still.

"Then we will eat this lasagne of feelings," she said, "and pretend we’re not already mourning the sound of your front door."

"Deal," Ramon said quietly.

---

After that, conversation moved in fits and starts. They talked about property prices (obscene), coastal weather (unreliable), and Rosalia’s mother’s latest medical drama (ongoing). They argued about what constituted "real" carbonara.

Every so often, the topic drifted back to the move. Each time, the room felt a little thinner around the edges.

Sarah found herself cataloguing tiny details: the chipped corner of the cabinet where she’d once hidden during a game of hide-and-seek; the faint water stain near the ceiling from the time the upstairs radiators had rebelled. Ramon’s wedding photo on the sideboard, slightly crooked as always.

She thought of the email sleeping in her pocket. *Prior contact from your biological family…*

Houses moved. People moved.

Maybe some things were meant to.

"You are very quiet," Rosalia observed at one point, when Ramon had gone to fetch dessert and Miles was in the kitchen helping.

Sarah flashed a small smile. "Just… processing."

"If you need somewhere to be angry about it that is not your kitchen, you know you are always welcome here," Rosalia said. "At least until we become coastal elite."

"You already are coastal elite," Sarah replied. "In your hearts."

Rosalia huffed a laugh.

"That is what I get for helping you with your English essays," she said.

---

Dessert was a collaborative chaos of brownies, a suspiciously glossy fruit tart Ramon insisted was "light" (it wasn’t), and ice cream that dripped faster than anyone could eat.

As plates emptied, Ramon clapped his hands for attention.

"One more thing," he said.

"If you pull a ring box out of your pocket, I am leaving," Lillian warned.

"Rosalia would leave first," he replied.

Rosalia rolled her eyes.

"No rings," he said. "But. We wanted to ask a favour."

He looked at Miles.

"Will you help us fix the bits of the house that need fixing before we sell?" he asked simply. "The estate agent says buyers like things that don’t fall down."

"They are so fussy," Rosalia muttered.

Miles blinked. "Of course," he said. The answer was automatic. "I’ll make a list."

Of course he would make a list. Of course he would patch their walls, tighten their taps, quiet their floorboards. Of course he would, in some quiet way, help erase their traces from this place so that strangers could move in.

The thought made his chest twist.

"And," Ramon added, looking at Sarah now, "we were thinking… there’s this wall in the stairwell. Very plain. Very boring. Our agent says neutral is good, but what do agents know? We thought… maybe you’d like to leave something. A small mural. Not for the buyers. For us."

Sarah’s eyes widened. "A mural?"

"Nothing big," Rosalia said quickly. "Something that can maybe be painted over one day. But not by us. Not while we’re there."

Sarah realised her tail was wagging.

"I… could," she said slowly. Ideas already sketched themselves across the blank wall in her mind. A tiny house between two bigger ones. A row of mugs. A cat and a fox and a badger and a shepherd and a girl with a sketchbook. Something small and secret.

"No pressure," Rosalia added.

"Of course there’s pressure," Ramon said. "Art is pressure."

"Ignore him," Rosalia advised. "Remember I taught you how to make bechamel properly. That is our real legacy."

Sarah smiled. "I’d like to," she said. "To… leave something."

Lillian watched her daughter’s face, the way it lit up. She swallowed the sting behind her eyes.

They were all leaving pieces of themselves everywhere, it seemed.

In garden sheds. In stairwells. In the inboxes of people they hadn’t met yet.

---

Later, when they finally tumbled back through their own front door, bellies full and emotions frayed at the edges, the house felt different.

Not worse. Not better.

Just… more itself. As if it knew it was about to become, by default, the last anchor on the street.

Lillian set the empty casserole dish in the sink and leaned against the counter.

"They’re really going," she said.

"Looks that way," Miles replied.

"Everyone’s moving," she muttered. "Sarah’s got Brighton in her back pocket, Ramon and Rosalia are running away to the seaside, even Penny’s threatening Manhattan brunches."

"Penny did move to Manhattan," Miles reminded her. "Threat successfully executed."

"And you—" She stopped.

The word *Berlin* sat unsaid between them, like a suitcase that had been packed but never zipped.

He dried his paws on a towel, not looking at her. "We haven’t decided anything," he said.

"That’s the problem," she replied softly. "Everyone else is deciding. We’re… marinating."

"Some dishes are better marinated," he said.

"Some go off," she countered.

They stood in the kitchen’s familiar light, the hum of the fridge and the far-off murmur of the radio filling the spaces between them.

"Do you want to go?" she asked quietly at last.

He opened his mouth. Closed it.

"I don’t know," he admitted. "I know I’m supposed to. That’s what people like me do, isn’t it? Big job, big move, big adventure."

"People like you," she repeated. "You mean men whose idea of a good time is labelling jars?"

"Engineers," he said, helpless.

She stepped closer.

"Miles," she said, and his name in her mouth was a kind of anchor. "You know you’re allowed to want the small life. The… deep one."

He looked at her then.

"You never did," he said.

"I never thought I deserved it," she corrected. "Different disease."

He exhaled, something in his shoulders loosening.

"I don’t want to be the only one still here when everyone else’s credits roll," he said.

"You won’t be," she replied. "I am extremely hard to get rid of."

He almost smiled.

"We’ll see," he said. It was not an answer. But it was closer than silence.

From the hallway, Sarah’s voice floated in, muffled by the stairs.

"Mum? Dad?" she called. "Is it okay if I go over tomorrow to look at their stairwell wall? I have… ideas."

Lillian closed her eyes briefly, then opened them, steady.

"Go," she called back. "Leave something beautiful where it’ll annoy the estate agent."

She turned back to Miles.

"You’ll help them fix their house," she said. "You always help. That’s who you are. But just so we’re clear—" Her voice softened. "You don’t owe Berlin anything. You don’t owe anyone a grand gesture. This—" She gestured around them. "This is already a life that would make half the world jealous."

He looked at the sink full of dishes, the worn patch on the floor where she always stood to stir sauces, the window that stuck when it rained.

"We could use some new grout," he said.

"Then start with that," she replied.

He nodded slowly.

Outside, next door’s windows glowed. For now, the house was still theirs.

For now, that was enough.
# Episode 6: Blueprints

The kitchen table had never looked so official.

It had hosted bills and birthday cakes and late-night confessionals, art projects and half-finished crosswords and one memorable attempt at building a scale model of the Millennium Bridge. But it had never, until this particular Tuesday, been covered edge to edge with paper.

Sheets overlapped in a loose mosaic—graph paper, tracing paper, a page torn from the back of Sarah’s sketchbook. Lines criss-crossed in pencil: walls, doorways, rectangles labelled with neat, blocky handwriting.

MILES: KITCHEN. LIVING. BEDROOM (S). BATH.

LILLIAN: WHERE THE TEA LIVES.

Sarah had added a tiny cartoon of Mozzarella in the corner, mid-yawn.

Miles stood leaning over the table like a general over a war map, paws braced on either side. His ears were angled forward, eyes narrowed in concentration. A pencil rested behind his ear; another one was already in his paw, tapping a slow, unconscious rhythm.

"You’re vibrating," Lillian observed.

She perched on the edge of the counter, one leg swinging lazily, a mug of tea balanced in her paw. The late-morning light slanted across her, picking out silver strands in her fur she refused to acknowledge.

"I’m thinking," he said.

"Same difference," she replied.

He exhaled through his nose and moved one of the papers half an inch.

"Look at this," he said. "The hallway’s a bottleneck. And the cupboard under the stairs is criminally under-utilised. If we knocked through here—" He drew a line with the pencil. "—we could open up the sightline from the front door to the garden. More light. Better flow."

"Ah yes," Lillian said. "What every home needs. More flow."

"You complain every time you bump your hip on that radiator," he pointed out.

"My hips are a public service," she said. "If the radiator can’t handle them, that’s on the radiator."

From the other side of the table, Sarah added a new rectangle to the living room sketch and labelled it *COUCH (MASSIVE)*.

"Accuracy is important," she murmured when Miles raised a brow.

"I’m not saying we actually do all this," he said. "It’s just… potential."

He spread his paws over the paper, as if he could feel the house through it. The lines, the angles, the way one room breathed into another.

"You’re redrawing our life," Lillian said lightly. "Should I be worried?"

He flinched the tiniest bit.

"It’s just plans," he said quickly. "Preliminary."

Sarah’s phone buzzed where it lay face-down on the table. She checked the caller ID, frowned, and let it ring out.

"Someone persistent," Lillian remarked.

"Probably spam," Sarah said.

The house gave one of its little shudders—the floorboard in the hallway clicking like a tongue, the pipes sighing. Mozzarella shifted under the radiator, rolling onto his back with an indignant sound.

"We don’t have to fix everything," Lillian said, more to the ceiling than to Miles.

"No," he agreed. "But if I’m going to leave a house behind, I’d like it to be in good order."

The words dropped into the room like a tool clattering onto concrete.

He froze. Lillian went very still.

Sarah’s pencil paused halfway through shading a doorway.

"If," Lillian repeated.

He swallowed.

"Figure of speech," he said.

"Uh-huh," she replied.

---

The problem with being an engineer, Miles thought, was that problems never arrived one at a time. They came in clusters. Systems. Feedback loops.

You started thinking about the way the kitchen door stuck in damp weather and suddenly you were redesigning the entire ground floor. You accepted a job offer email into your inbox and suddenly you were redrawing the map of your life.

He had not told them yet about the two-week deadline.

The Berlin email sat in his pocket even when the tablet was nowhere in sight. He could feel it like a weight, like an imbalance he was trying to compensate for by levelling everything else.

"You’re nesting," Penny had told him over video call the night before, when he’d mentioned the shed, the garden, the list of repairs. Her pixelated opossum face had smirked knowingly from a Manhattan high-rise.

"Nesting?" he’d repeated, offended.

"Yes," she’d said. "Classic pre-departure behaviour. Fixing everything so you feel okay leaving it."

"You moved three times in two years," he pointed out.

"Exactly," she’d said. "Trust me. I know an exit strategy when I see one."

Now he stared at the lines on the paper, willing them to tell him what to do.

"If we widen this doorway," he murmured, more to himself than anyone, "it’ll be easier to move furniture between rooms."

"Or to move suitcases out," Lillian said.

He winced.

"Can we not make every door about international relocation?" he asked.

"Can we not pretend you’re drawing these for fun?" she countered.

Sarah’s eyes flicked between them, tracking the invisible currents.

"Maybe," she said carefully, "we could mark what we *actually* want to change, and what’s just brainstorming. Different colours. So we don’t panic about imaginary walls coming down."

Miles seized the lifeline.

"Good idea," he said. "Blue for definite, green for maybe, red for ‘only if we win the lottery.’"

"And purple for ‘midlife crisis’," Lillian added.

"There is no purple," he said.

"There is always purple," she replied.

---

That afternoon, the house became a drafting board.

They moved from room to room with tape measures and notebooks. Miles took measurements with the solemnity of a priest performing a ritual. Sarah sketched quick elevations in her pad, annotating them with tiny notes.

*Hallway: weird echo. Good for singing. Bad for secrets.*

*Living room: light best at 4 p.m. Also dust.*

Lillian contributed commentary more than data.

"This corner is where Sarah first fell asleep on the sofa without flinching when I moved," she said, tapping the living room wall.

"That’s not very measurable," Miles murmured, though his voice had gone soft.

"Important load-bearing memory," she said.

In the hallway, he measured the width three times, writing the number down with increasing irritation.

"We could move this wall four centimetres," he said. "It would open it up."

"Four centimetres," Lillian echoed. "Will guests suddenly feel freer? Will their secrets glide in more easily?"

"It changes how people move through the space," he said. "Angles matter."

"Do angles matter more than people staying?" she asked quietly.

He didn’t answer.

---

In Sarah’s room, the tension briefly shifted.

"You want me to move the bed?" she asked, as Miles eyed the layout.

"The light’s better on that wall," he said. "For drawing. And you’d have more floor space."

"I’m leaving in a year," she reminded him gently. "Maybe."

"Exactly," he said. "Might as well make the most of the time you’ve got in here."

She watched him for a long moment.

"Is that what this is about?" she asked. "Making the most of the time?"

He busied himself with the tape measure.

"I like knowing where things stand," he said.

"And if they move?" she asked.

He reeled the tape back in with a snap.

"Then we draw new lines," he said.

She sat on the edge of her bed, fingers knotting in the bedspread.

"I got another email," she said suddenly. "From the social worker. About… them."

He looked up.

"Are you going to reply?" he asked.

"I don’t know," she admitted. "Part of me wants to. Part of me wants to delete it and pretend it bounced."

"That’s allowed," he said.

"Is it?" she demanded. "Everyone keeps telling me I’ll regret *not* meeting them. Or I’ll regret it if I do and it goes badly. It’s like I’m doomed to regret something either way."

He set the tape measure down and sat beside her, careful to leave just enough space that she could choose to close it.

"You’re not doomed to anything," he said. "You’re just… faced with two incomplete plans. Neither of them has all the information yet."

She huffed a watery laugh.

"Trust you to turn this into a flowchart," she said.

"It helps me think," he said. "But for what it’s worth… any plan that hurts you on purpose? We don’t choose that one."

"What if they don’t mean to?" she whispered. "What if they’re just… themselves. And that’s enough to make everything complicated."

He thought of Berlin. Of emails that did not intend harm but still carved fault lines through his days.

"Then we draw boundaries," he said softly. "Thick ones. In purple."

"You said there was no purple," she reminded him.

"I’ve been overruled," he said.

She leaned her shoulder against his.

"If I meet them," she said, "will you be mad?"

The question hit him like a stray nail.

"Mad?" he repeated. "No. Terrified, maybe. Protective. Confused. But not mad."

"Will Mum?" she asked.

He looked toward the half-open door, where a sliver of hallway showed the familiar, slightly crooked family photo.

"She might be," he said honestly. "At first. At the idea. At history. Not at you."

"She might think I’m leaving," Sarah said.

"Then we’ll have to draw that line very clearly," he replied. "Meeting isn’t leaving. Knowing isn’t replacing."

She breathed out, a long, shaky exhale.

"You’re really going to map this out, aren’t you?" she said.

"You’re my favourite project," he said. "Of course I am."

---

That evening, the table disappeared under paper entirely.

Lillian came down from a phone call with Frederick—something about set orders and lighting cues and a drummer with opinions—to find father and daughter bent over the plans, arguing quietly.

"You can’t just move the wall," Sarah was saying. "That’s where the Forbidden Shelf is."

"We’d move the shelf," he replied. "Carefully."

"Blasphemy," Lillian said.

They both started.

"We’re not *actually* demolishing anything," Miles said quickly.

"Tell that to these panicked little rectangles," she said, tapping the sketches.

She studied the plans more closely.

There were notes everywhere now. Some were practical.

*INSULATE. SKIRTING BOARD REPLACE. SOCKET HERE?*

Some were not.

*KEEP THIS DOORWAY. FIRST KISS HERE (AGAINST TERRIBLE LAMP).* 

That one was in her handwriting.

"You’re annotating emotional history now," Miles said.

"You started it," she replied. "With your sentimental load-bearing beams."

She traced a line from the front door to the kitchen.

"This," she said, "is the path Sarah took the first time she came in. Backpack, too-big jumper, eyes like saucers."

"We shouldn’t put a wall there, then," Miles said quietly.

"No," she agreed. "We shouldn’t."

"I wasn’t planning to," he said. "It’s just a draft."

"I know," she said. "I know drafts are your love language."

He looked up at her.

"I just—" he began. "If I go—"

"If," she said sharply.

"If," he amended. "If I go, I want to know I left you both with… a map."

"We have a map," she said. "It’s called memory."

"Memory doesn’t fix leaky roofs," he said.

"No," she said. "But it does make roofs worth fixing."

The room went quiet.

Sarah watched them, feeling like she was standing at a junction where three different sets of tracks met.

"Maybe," she said tentatively, "the house doesn’t need a new plan. Maybe we do. The us-plan."

"I thought that’s what Berlin was," Miles said.

"Berlin’s… a you-plan," she said. "This—" She gestured around. "This is the us-plan."

Lillian blinked.

"When did you get so wise?" she asked.

"Somewhere between art school critiques and your podcast appearances," Sarah replied.

---

Later, after Sarah had gone upstairs to answer—finally—the email about meeting her biological family, and the house had sunk into its evening hush, Lillian and Miles stayed at the table.

The papers lay between them like a thin, crinkling continent.

"What if we don’t change anything?" Miles said suddenly.

"To the house?" Lillian asked.

"To any of it," he said. "What if I say no? To Berlin. To… all of it. What if I just stay."

"You say ‘just’ as if it’s small," she said.

"It would be," he insisted. "Smaller than what they’re offering."

"Smaller than what who is offering?" she asked. "Men in suits? Emails?"

He hesitated.

"The world," he said.

She reached across the table and covered his paw with hers.

"The world is not outside this house, Miles," she said. "Not only. It’s in it. In here." She tapped the table. "On this stupid laminate. On our dented mugs. In Sarah’s terrible shoe pile."

He looked at their hands.

"I wanted that job once," he said quietly. "Before. Before you. Before her. Before… this."

"Then maybe the you who wanted it can be glad you were chosen," she said. "And the you who lives here now can be glad you’re allowed to say no."

"It sounds ungrateful," he muttered.

"It sounds like choosing," she said. "You’re allowed to choose the thing that looks smaller on paper and bigger in your chest."

He exhaled.

"What if I regret it?" he asked.

"Then we’ll build you a little regret shelf next to my Forbidden Shelf," she said. "We’ll put a tiny Berlin postcard on it and dust it twice a year."

He laughed, helplessly.

"You’re impossible," he said.

"And yet," she said, "here you are."

He turned one of the pages around, picking up his pencil.

With a few quick strokes, he drew an arrow from the little box labelled *SHED* to the one labelled *KITCHEN*.

"What’s that?" she asked.

"A path," he said. "The one I keep walking."

He added another arrow, from *KITCHEN* to *GARDEN*, and wrote in small letters: *HOME*.

"That’s not very technical," she said, but her voice was thick.

"No," he said. "But it’s accurate."

The phone buzzed on the counter. A notification flashed.

*Berlin HR: Reminder — decision deadline approaching.*

He looked at it.

Then he picked up the phone, tapped the screen, and opened a new email.

"What are you doing?" Lillian asked.

"Redrawing the plan," he said.

He typed slowly, carefully.

*Dear Dr. Weiss,

Thank you again for the offer. After careful consideration, I’ve decided not to relocate at this time.*

He paused.

"‘At this time’," he repeated under his breath.

"That leaves doors open," Lillian said.

"I like doors," he replied.

He finished the email, hands only shaking a little, and hit send.

The message whooshed away into whatever ether emails lived in.

For a long moment, they both stared at the phone as if waiting for lightning.

Nothing happened.

The fridge hummed. The house creaked. Someone down the street shouted about a football match.

"That’s it?" he said.

"That’s it," she replied.

He let out a breath he hadn’t realised he’d been holding for months.

"Now what?" he asked.

"Now," she said, gathering the papers into a slightly untidy stack, "we decide which walls we actually want to paint, and which ones we’re perfectly happy creaking as they are."

She slid the stack to one side and set her notebook down in front of her.

"I might write a song about this," she added.

"About blueprints?" he asked.

"About choosing the small, stubborn life," she said.

He considered this.

"Use a major key," he said. "It deserves one."

Upstairs, Sarah sat on her bed, an email draft open on her laptop.

*Dear Elaine,

Thank you for reaching out. I think I am ready to meet. But I’d like to start slowly.*

She stared at the words for a long time.

Then she hit send.

In the blueprint of their lives, new lines were being drawn. Not with rulers and pencils, but with choices.

Somewhere between the kitchen and the shed, the house settled into its foundations with a satisfied sigh.

# Episode 7: Tea with Penny

The invitation arrived in the most on-brand way possible: as a passive-aggressive flyer.

Lillian found it wedged in the letterbox between a takeaway menu and a glossy estate agent leaflet boasting about "discreet, professional handling of life transitions." The estate agent went straight into the recycling bin. The flyer she kept.

It was printed on pale pink cardstock, the kind that tried very hard to be gentle. Across the top, in looping faux-handwritten font, it read:

> *Mindful Mornings with Penny*  
> *Tea, Tarot, and Tactical Letting Go*  
> 
> *By appointment only. No shoes, no shouting, no sugar.*

At the bottom, in tiny print: *(Decaf available upon request. Emotional catharsis not guaranteed.)*

Lillian stared at it for a long moment, her tail tip twitching.

"Absolutely not," she muttered.

Behind her, the house hummed in its early-morning rhythm. Miles’s footsteps creaked their way down the stairs in familiar sequence: one, two, hesitate, three. Sarah’s kettle clicked on in her room upstairs; she’d taken to making her own tea first thing, a tiny ritual of independence.

Lillian set the flyer on the kitchen table as if it might scuttle away.

"What’s that?" Miles asked, appearing in the doorway with his usual mild dishevelment—shirt slightly skewed, fur at one ear insisting on pointing north.

"A cry for help," she said. "Possibly a curse."

He picked up the flyer and scanned it.

"Penny’s started a mindfulness… thing," he said slowly.

"A cult," Lillian corrected. "Next she’ll be selling crystals infused with the tears of disappointed opossums."

He suppressed a smile.

"She’s pivoting," he said. "People do that."

"Some of us pivot away from chaos, not into it," Lillian replied.

He raised a brow.

"You did a nude performance art piece in Shoreditch once," he reminded her.

"I was mostly covered in glitter and righteous fury," she said. "And that was before I had a mortgage."

He turned the flyer over. On the back, in neat, surprisingly restrained handwriting, was a short note.

> *Lillian,*  
> *I’m hosting small tarot/tea sessions from my flat while I’m back in London for a bit.*  
> *No pressure, but—you once said you’d come let me read your cards "when I was less of a menace."*  
> *I’m… trying.*  
> *If you ever feel like talking without shouting, you’d be welcome.*  
> 
> *—Penny*

Miles watched her face carefully.

"You did say that," he said quietly.

"I say many things," she replied. "Some of them to shut people up."

"She is… trying," he said.

"So am I," Lillian shot back. "You don’t see me printing flyers about it."

Sarah wandered in, hair still sleep-tousled, her mug clutched between her paws.

"What are we glaring at?" she asked.

"Penny’s marketing," Lillian said, shoving the flyer across the table.

Sarah read it, eyebrows lifting a little higher with each line.

"‘Tactical Letting Go,’" she read aloud. "That sounds like a yoga pose and a breakup strategy."

"Exactly," Lillian said.

Sarah flipped the card to read the note.

Her expression softened.

"You should go," she said.

"Traitor," Lillian replied.

"You’re the one who told me people are allowed to change," Sarah reminded her. "Also, I kind of want to know what she’d say about your future."

"Nothing good," Lillian muttered. "Too many carbs. Not enough Pilates."

"You could take Miles as a human shield," Sarah offered.

"I am not bringing my husband to a spiritual appraisal by his ex-girlfriend," Lillian said, scandalised. "That’s how horror films start."

Miles sipped his coffee.

"I could drop you off," he said mildly.

"I’m not a child going to a birthday party," she said.

"You’re a woman going to have tea with the opossum who once tried to ruin your life," he replied. "A lift is the least I can do."

She opened her mouth to retort.

Then she closed it again.

Because beneath the outrage and the jokes and the reflexive bristling, something else was humming.

Curiosity.

---

Penny’s temporary London flat was in a building that tried very hard not to admit it was old. The lobby had been freshly painted in a shade of grey that estate agents called "contemporary" and Lillian called "hangover." The lift smelled faintly of bleach and someone’s overly ambitious cologne.

"Last chance to feign food poisoning," Miles said, thumb hovering over the button for the fourth floor.

"I’m not afraid of her," Lillian said.

"You’re not afraid of anybody," he replied.

"Exactly," she said.

He pressed the button.

The lift jolted upward with a sigh.

Lillian watched their reflections in the mirrored panel—her broad badger frame in a dress that walked the line between casual and armour, his leaner shepherd build in his "neutral" shirt. They looked, she thought, like people auditioning for different plays.

"You sure you don’t want me to stay?" he asked.

"If you stay, she’ll think I need you as a buffer," she said. "I don’t."

He nodded, but his ears stayed tilted in concern.

"Text me if she starts burning sage," he said. "I’ll call the fire brigade."

She snorted.

"Go," she said when the doors opened on the fourth floor. "Before I ask you to hold my paw in front of my arch-nemesis."

"She’s not your arch-nemesis anymore," he said quietly.

"We’ll see," she replied.

The corridor carpet was the kind of pattern designed to hide stains and small crimes. A door at the far end stood slightly open, a line of warm light spilling into the hall.

"Come in if you’re Lillian!" called a familiar, nasal voice.

"And if I’m not?" Lillian called back.

"Then you’re very lost," Penny replied.

Lillian took a breath and stepped inside.

---

Penny’s flat did not look like the lair of an emotional saboteur.

It looked… nice.

Soft, even.

Someone had draped a thick woven throw over the back of the sofa. Plants clustered near the windows in a way that suggested successful survival rather than imminent doom. A low table held a teapot, three mismatched mugs, and a small ceramic bowl of sugar lumps defiantly labelled *NO GUILT* with a marker.

Penny herself stood in the tiny kitchen alcove, pouring hot water over a carefully arranged constellation of tea bags. Her fur was sleeker than Lillian remembered, streaks of grey at her muzzle worn like deliberate highlights rather than defeat. She wore a loose linen shirt and soft trousers—less armour, more… comfort.

Only her eyes were the same sharp, assessing brown, missing nothing.

"You came," she said.

"Don’t make a big thing of it," Lillian replied.

"It *is* a big thing," Penny said. "But I won’t say that yet. Sit."

Lillian eyed the seating options as if they were a test. The armchair looked safest; the sofa felt like an intimacy she wasn’t ready to concede. She chose the armchair.

Penny brought over the tray and set it down.

"Chamomile, peppermint, or something with turmeric and marketing hype?" she asked.

"Black," Lillian said. "With enough caffeine to dissolve regret."

"So you want the stuff I keep for emergencies," Penny said. "Noted."

She produced a small tin from the back of the tray as if from a magician’s sleeve.

"You look well," Penny said as the tea steeped.

"You look… like you own your own crystals," Lillian replied.

"I rent them," Penny said dryly. "Commitment issues."

They sat in a silence that wasn’t quite comfortable but wasn’t hostile either.

"So," Lillian said at last. "Cards. Enlightenment. My chakras. Let’s get it over with before I lose my nerve and start singing show tunes."

"Please don’t," Penny said. "The neighbours already think I’m running a commune."

She reached for a small tin box on the side table and opened it to reveal a well-worn tarot deck, corners softened by years of shuffling.

"Before you start," Lillian said, "you should know I don’t believe in any of this."

"You also don’t believe in underwired bras and yet you own three," Penny replied.

"That’s about structural integrity," Lillian protested.

"So is this," Penny said. "Of a different kind."

She began to shuffle the cards, movements smooth and practised.

"I don’t do fortune-telling," she added. "I do… pattern-looking. Story-shaping. It’s an excuse to talk about the thing people already brought with them."

"What if I didn’t bring anything?" Lillian asked.

Penny looked at her over the cards.

"You brought at least twelve unresolved conversations," she said. "And that’s just in your shoulders."

Lillian bristled.

"Don’t read my shoulders," she said.

"They’re very loud," Penny replied.

---

They started with three cards.

"Past, present, possible path," Penny said. "Not future. You can still throw that in the bin if you hate it."

"Good," Lillian muttered. "I’ve always wanted to heckle fate."

She placed her paws on the deck for a moment when Penny asked her to, feeling foolish and a little self-conscious.

"Ask a question," Penny said.

"Out loud?" Lillian asked.

"If you can," Penny said. "Saves me guessing."

Lillian stared at the table.

She wanted to ask *Is everyone going to leave?*  
She wanted to ask *Do I hold them too tight?*  
She wanted to ask *If Miles stays just for me, will he hate me quietly in ten years?*

What came out was:

"What happens if I stop… pushing?"

Penny nodded, as if that made sense.

"Cut the deck," she said.

The first card went down.

"The Chariot," Penny said. "Past. Movement. Drive. Control. You, basically, with better posture."

The illustration showed a figure in a chariot pulled by two animals straining in different directions.

"Subtle," Lillian said.

"You’ve always moved forward by grabbing the reins harder," Penny said. "Worked for you. Mostly."

"Mostly," Lillian echoed.

The second card.

"The Hanged Man," Penny said. "Present. Pause. Surrender. Not as bad as it sounds."

Lillian eyed the upside-down figure on the card, hanging serenely from a tree.

"He looks smug," she said.

"He’s trying not to fight gravity for once," Penny replied. "Seeing what happens if he stops kicking."

Lillian made a face.

"Being still is not my colour," she said.

"You’re still right now," Penny pointed out.

"That’s because any sudden movement might jostle my trauma," Lillian retorted.

Penny huffed a laugh despite herself.

She laid down the third card.

"The Ten of Cups," she said softly. "Well. That’s… on the nose."

The card showed a little painted family under a rainbow of cups, arms thrown wide in celebration, house in the background.

Lillian’s throat did something inconvenient.

"Ah," she said. "So the universe is a stereotype."

"Possible path," Penny reminded her. "Not guarantee. Not punishment. Just… a suggestion. Fulfilment in what you have instead of what you chase."

Lillian stared at the card.

"You stacked the deck," she said.

"I did not," Penny replied. "I don’t have time to rig emotional metaphors before breakfast."

"You really think I’m just… supposed to sit here while life happens around me?" Lillian asked. "Let Sarah go. Let Ramon and Rosalia move. Let Miles decide whether he wants Berlin or this kitchen without even… pushing?"

"I think," Penny said carefully, "you might be surprised what people choose when you’re not shoving them toward your preferred outcome."

"People need shoving," Lillian insisted.

"You needed shoving," Penny corrected. "Once. A long time ago. You’re still fighting people who aren’t in the room anymore."

Lillian’s jaw clenched.

"Like you?" she asked.

Penny didn’t flinch.

"Yes," she said. "Like me."

---

The tea cooled between them.

"Why are you doing this?" Lillian asked at last. "The blog, the mindfulness, the tarot."

Penny shrugged.

"Because New York is loud and lonely and I got tired of being the villain in every story I told myself," she said. "Because therapy is expensive and tarot cards are cheaper. Because—" She hesitated. "Because I realised the only person still punishing you for the things I did is you. And that’s… awkward, for both of us."

Lillian stared.

"That almost sounded like an apology," she said.

"It almost was," Penny replied.

Silence again, but different.

"I’m not… ready to be your friend," Lillian said.

"I’m not asking you to," Penny said. "Friends come with expectations. I’m offering… neutral ground. Occasional tea. Honest conversation, if you want it."

"Why me?" Lillian asked. "You could be reading cards for strangers on the internet."

"I do," Penny said. "But they don’t know the version of me who threw a drink at you in front of a pub full of people. You do. If I can sit here with you and not feel like I have to justify my existence every five minutes, then maybe I’m… not completely irredeemable."

Lillian blinked.

"That’s very… self-aware of you," she said.

"I am insufferably self-aware now," Penny replied. "It’s disgusting."

Lillian snorted.

"What did your cards say about you?" she asked.

"That I talk too much and should learn to listen," Penny said. "So I’m practising."

She leaned back, paws open.

"Tell me something true," she said. "Not about me. About you."

Lillian’s first instinct, always, was to perform.

To turn truth into a story. To gild it, twist it, throw glitter on it until it was easier for everyone to look at.

She watched her own reflection in Penny’s eyes and, for once, didn’t like the idea.

"I’m scared," she said quietly. "Of staying. Of everyone leaving. Of being… the loud one in an empty room."

Penny nodded as if this were the most normal confession in the world.

"And if you go?" she asked. "If you chase something. Tours. Records. Bigger stages. What then?"

"Then I’m scared of missing it," Lillian said. "Of missing *them*. Of coming home one day and finding Sarah’s drawings boxed up and Miles’s shed repurposed as a rental listing feature. ‘Charming outbuilding, suitable for emotionally avoidant engineers.’"

Penny’s mouth twitched.

"You’ve built a life where either choice means loss," she said. "Congratulations. You’re a human."

"Badger," Lillian corrected automatically.

"Same curse," Penny said.

---

They talked.

Not in the explosive, operatic way they once had—shouting across pubs, across stairwells, across whole years—but in fits and starts. About nothing and everything.

Penny confessed she hated New York bagels but ate them out of ritual. Lillian admitted she sometimes Googled herself and then regretted it.

They circled the big things—Sarah, Miles, the miscarriage they had never spoken about directly, the way Penny had once been a ghost in their hallway—like skittish animals around a pond.

"Do you… resent her?" Penny asked at one point, nodding toward a framed photo of Sarah on Lillian’s lock screen.

"For what?" Lillian asked, genuinely confused.

"For not being the child you lost," Penny said. "For being the one who lived here instead."

Lillian’s grip tightened on her phone.

"No," she said fiercely. "Never. She’s… the proof. That I wasn’t empty. That there was still room."

Penny looked at her for a long moment.

"Good," she said. "That’s… good."

"You?" Lillian asked. "Do you resent anyone for who you didn’t become?"

"All of Instagram," Penny said. "And my parents. And occasionally you. But mostly me."

"At least you’re efficient," Lillian said.

They both laughed.

---

When she finally left, the sky had shifted toward late afternoon. The light along the street had softened, turning brickwork to honey.

Miles waited in the car, book open on his lap. He looked up as she approached, scanning her face the way he scanned fault lines in walls.

"You’re not on fire," he observed.

"Give it time," she said, sliding into the passenger seat.

"How was it?" he asked.

She considered.

"We did not throw anything," she said. "We did not insult each other’s life choices. She did not spontaneously combust in a puff of patchouli."

"High bar," he said.

"She read my cards," Lillian added.

"Did they say you’re terrifying and wonderful?" he asked.

"They suggested I might try not controlling the trajectory of every person I love like a deranged air-traffic controller," she said.

He smiled, small and genuine.

"Revolutionary," he said.

"Shut up," she replied, but there was no heat in it.

As they pulled away from the curb, Lillian watched the city roll past.

The houses, the shops, the familiar bend in the road near the park where Sarah had once fallen off her bike and insisted on getting back on immediately. The small, ordinary markers of a life she loved more than she’d ever expected to.

"Do you ever wish," she said, "that we could see the blueprint? Of us. Of how it all turns out. So we could stop… fiddling with the walls and just live in it."

Miles considered this.

"If I’d seen the blueprint at twenty-five," he said, "I would’ve thought, ‘That’s too small. Not enough variables. Not enough risk.’"

"And now?" she asked.

"Now," he said, "it looks like the biggest thing I’ve ever built."

She reached across and laced her paw through his.

"Penny says I should stop pushing," she said.

He glanced at her.

"I’ve been saying that for years," he said.

"Yes, but she had props," Lillian replied.

They drove the rest of the way in companionable silence.

---

That night, Sarah sat at the kitchen table with her laptop open, the email from Elaine on the screen.

*I’d love to meet you,* it read. *But only when you’re ready. We can start small. A call. A coffee. You set the pace. There’s no rush. I’m not going anywhere.*

Sarah’s fingers hovered over the keys.

She thought of Penny talking about patterns. Of her mother’s face when she’d come back from tea—more unsettled, somehow, but also lighter at the edges.

She thought of Miles’s blueprints spread across this same table, imaginary walls moving, doorways widening.

Slowly, she began to type.

*Hi Elaine,*

*Thank you for being patient. I think… I’d like to talk. Maybe a call first. I live with my parents in Islington, and I’m not planning on going anywhere just yet either. But I’d like to know where I came from. At least a little.*

*I’m scared, but I’m trying.*

*Sarah*

She read it three times.

Then, heart pounding, she hit send.

From the doorway, Lillian watched, unnoticed.

She did not rush in. She did not offer five different opinions or twelve different snacks.

She stayed where she was, one paw on the doorframe, letting her daughter make her own blueprint.

Later, when Sarah turned and caught her there, their eyes met. Something soft and fierce passed between them.

"Tea was weird," Lillian said.

"Yeah?" Sarah asked.

"Yeah," Lillian replied. "But… maybe weird isn’t bad."

Sarah smiled, small and real.

"Weird got us here," she said.

And in the small, cluttered kitchen that had held so many plans and arguments and improvisations, the three of them moved a little closer to a life they hadn’t scripted—but were, slowly, learning to inhabit.
# Episode 8: The Berlin Dinner

The first sign that the dinner was a bad idea came when Lillian caught Miles rehearsing sentences in the hallway mirror.

He thought he was alone. The light was off, the only illumination coming from the sliver of late-afternoon sun that snuck in through the frosted glass of the front door. He stood there in his good shirt—the one without paint stains or mysterious workshop scuffs—practising the shapes his mouth would have to make.

"It was a good offer," he said to his own reflection. "But it wasn’t right for us. For me. For now."

He grimaced.

"I sound like a press release," he muttered.

From the living room doorway, Lillian watched him, unseen.

"Try less corporate weasel, more actual Miles," she suggested.

He jumped.

"You shouldn’t sneak up on people in their existential crisis," he said, hand to his chest.

"You shouldn’t have existential crises near mirrors," she replied. "It’s bad for your pores."

He huffed a laugh despite himself.

"Do I really have to go?" he asked.

"You said you’d meet them," she reminded him. "It’s food and polite conversation, not a referendum on your character."

"They flew in from Berlin," he said. "Just to wine and dine me. That makes it feel… weightier."

"Then eat something heavy beforehand so you’re harder to sway," she said. "I’ll make potatoes."

"Lillian," he said, helpless.

She softened.

"You already said no," she reminded him gently. "You sent the email. This is just… follow-up. Closure. Whatever management buzzword makes you feel least like you’re going to be sick."

He ran a paw over his snout.

"What if they convince me I’ve made a mistake?" he asked.

"Then we’ll have to talk about why you’re so easily seduced by canapés," she said.

"I’m serious," he replied.

"So am I," she said. "You’re allowed to be tempted. You’re allowed to miss the idea of it. You’re even allowed to sit there and think ‘God, I could have been that person.’" She stepped closer, resting a paw over his heart. "You just already decided you’d rather be this one."

His shoulders dropped a fraction.

"Come with me," he said.

"Absolutely not," she replied.

He blinked.

"I thought—" he began.

"If I go, I’ll spend the whole evening performing," she said. "I’ll make jokes. I’ll charm them. I’ll sell them on why you were right to say no, or why they were wrong to ask, or why our kitchen is better than their labs. I will do what I do, which is push."

She held his gaze.

"You need to know you can sit in a room with people who want something from you and not be carried off by their story," she said. "Without me as your stage manager."

"That sounds terrible," he said.

"It is," she replied. "That’s why you get dessert."

---

The restaurant was one of those glass-and-wood places in the city centre that tried very hard to look effortless. The host wore a discreet headset, the lighting was flattering but just shy of actually letting you read the menu, and the wine glasses were slightly too big, as if compensating for something.

Miles arrived seven minutes early, because being exactly on time felt suspicious.

He’d rehearsed a dozen ways to introduce himself—some humble, some professional, some that tried to split the difference. In the end, he simply gave his name to the host and let himself be led through a maze of tables to a corner booth near the window.

They were already there.

Dr. Weiss looked exactly like his emails—neat, precise, mid-fifties with a greying muzzle and an expression that suggested an internal spreadsheet of everyone’s usefulness. Beside him sat a fox in her thirties with sharp cheekbones and the kind of tailored jacket that cost more than Miles’s entire shed.

"Miles," Dr. Weiss said, standing partway, paw extended. "So good to finally meet in person."

"Likewise," Miles said, shaking.

The fox smiled warmly.

"Anna Weber," she said. "Operations. Thank you for coming."

They all sat.

For a moment, there was the polite, awkward choreography of menus and napkins and water glasses. Miles’s paws felt too big for the stemware. He placed his hands deliberately on either side of the plate, as if anchoring himself.

"We appreciate your time," Anna said. "Especially now that you’ve…" She gestured vaguely. "Made a decision."

"I appreciate the offer," Miles said. "Truly. I just—"

"Wanted to confirm you really meant it," Dr. Weiss finished for him.

Miles blinked.

"I beg your pardon?" he asked.

Weiss smiled, thin but not unkind.

"Bright people often say no to big things at first," he said. "Fear. Family worries. Imposter syndrome. Then they sit with it for a while and realise they’ve made an error. We’ve learned not to take the first refusal as final."

A small, stubborn part of Miles bristled.

"I don’t usually make important decisions lightly," he said.

"No, of course not," Anna said quickly. "We’re not questioning your judgement, only… offering conversation."

The waiter arrived to take drink orders. Miles ordered sparkling water on instinct; the idea of adding alcohol to this felt like stacking variables on a wobbly equation.

"I want to be clear," he said once the waiter had gone. "I’m here because I respect the work you do. And because Lillian said it would be cowardly to hide behind an email."

"Lillian?" Anna asked.

"My wife," he said.

"Ah," Weiss said. "The musician."

Miles tilted his head.

"You’ve looked me up," he said.

"Of course," Weiss replied. "We don’t invite people to uproot their lives without understanding as much as we can. Your publications, your patent work, your…" He glanced at his phone. "Community involvement."

"That sounds ominous," Miles said.

"We saw the story about the shed," Anna said.

Miles blinked. "The what?"

"Local blog piece," she clarified. "About repurposing domestic spaces for creative work. There was a photo of you in front of a very sturdy-looking outbuilding."

"Ramon," Miles muttered under his breath.

"We’re not here to ambush you," Anna said gently. "We genuinely want to understand what you need."

"I need," Miles said carefully, "to not move to Berlin."

There was a small pause.

"That seems quite… categorical," Weiss said.

"You asked," Miles replied.

Weiss steepled his paws.

"Perhaps we should talk about why," he said.

---

They talked about work first, because it was easiest.

About the project in Berlin—the renewable energy grid, the modular storage systems, the chance to lead a team instead of quietly patching everyone else’s solutions. Anna outlined the funding structure with an enthusiasm that almost made Miles’s paws itch for a whiteboard.

He asked questions. He couldn’t help it. About load balancing, about failure protocols, about the margin they were leaving for human error.

"You’d have scope to redesign most of this," Weiss said, eyes bright. "We don’t bring people in to hold screwdrivers. We bring them in to draw the plans."

Miles imagined it for a moment.

The lab. The hum. The sense of scale. Being in the room where decisions were made instead of reading about them later.

"It’s… tempting," he admitted.

Weiss smiled, like a man who’d just heard the first crack in a wall.

"Your email mentioned ‘family considerations’," Anna said. "You have a daughter?"

"Sarah," he said. "She’s… complicated. Brilliant. Just finished at the academy." His chest swelled a little despite himself.

"We support families," Anna said. "We have relocation specialists, school placement assistance—"

"She’s not a placement," Miles cut in, more sharply than he intended. "She has a life. A history. A therapist."

Weiss arched a brow.

"Berlin has therapists," he said dryly.

"Berlin doesn’t have the woman who stood in front of a judge and said ‘She can come with me,’" Miles replied.

The table went very still.

Anna set down her glass carefully.

"You’re talking about your wife," she said.

"I’m talking about my family," he said. "My wife. My daughter. The neighbours who show up with soup when the pipes burst. The badger who can tell what kind of day I’ve had from the way I shut a drawer."

He realised, belatedly, that his paws were gripping the edge of the table.

He forced them to relax.

"I don’t… do things halfway," he said, more quietly. "If I came, I’d want to do it properly. Learn the language, be there late, show up early. I know what that job would take."

"And you don’t think you could balance that with home?" Weiss asked.

"I barely balance home with home," Miles said. "Ask anyone who’s seen my calendar."

Anna smiled faintly.

"Work-life balance is hard anywhere," she said.

"Yes," he agreed. "But here, when I fail at it, at least I’m failing in a kitchen where the tea lives." 

He gestured vaguely, as if Lillian’s marginalia had made its way onto the world.

---

At some point, the food arrived. Miles barely tasted it. His fork performed its duties out of politeness while the rest of him navigated a different sort of table.

"May I ask," Anna said at one point, "if it’s… money? We can sometimes adjust packages."

"It’s not money," he said.

It would have been easier if it were. Money was numbers. Trade-offs. Columns you could balance.

"Is it fear?" Weiss asked, not unkindly. "You wouldn’t be the first. New city, new language, new expectations."

"Of course I’m afraid," Miles said. "I’d be an idiot not to be. But I’m more afraid of waking up in five years and realising I live in a beautiful flat in a city I only know through U-Bahn maps, while the people I love have quietly built a life without me somewhere else."

He thought of Sarah in Brighton, of Lillian in their creaky kitchen, of Ramon and Rosalia by the sea.

He thought of himself on a different platform, watching different trains.

"You think they wouldn’t come with you?" Anna asked.

"I think," he said slowly, "that hauling someone across a continent because you’re scared to be brave in the small way might not be love. It might just be cowardice in a better suit."

The words surprised even him.

Weiss studied him for a long moment.

"We don’t often meet people who turn down this kind of opportunity," he said. "When they’re perfectly qualified. Perfectly placed."

"I spent half my life chasing the bigger thing," Miles said. "It turns out small things take just as much work. They just don’t come with press releases."

"You’re sure," Weiss said.

Miles sat back.

He thought of the shed’s warm light. Of Lillian in the doorway, refusing to go to Berlin for him. Of Sarah’s tentative email, the lines drawn between past and present.

He thought of the email he’d already sent.

"I’m sure," he said.

Weiss nodded once, decisively, as if concluding a meeting.

"Then," he said, "thank you for your honesty."

Anna smiled, softer now.

"If you ever change your mind," she said, "we won’t hold this against you."

"If I ever change my mind," he replied, "I’ll have a very long conversation with my family before I send any emails."

"That seems… wise," she said.

---

They insisted on paying.

"You didn’t come to be recruited," Anna said when he protested. "You came to close a loop. We can at least feed you."

On the way out, she pressed a card into his paw.

"For future conferences," she said. "Or if you ever want to consult. You don’t have to uproot your life to be in the room sometimes."

He slipped it into his wallet without really looking.

The night air outside was cooler than he’d expected. The city lights shimmered on wet pavement; it must have rained while they were inside.

He pulled out his phone.

No missed calls. One text.

> LILLIAN: If they stole you, blink twice.

He snorted and typed back.

> MILES: Still mine. On my way home.

---

Back in Islington, the house waited with its familiar unevenness.

Lillian had waited up despite promising she’d go to bed at a sensible hour. She sat at the kitchen table in her dressing gown, a mug of tea between her paws, the radio playing soft jazz at a volume that suggested she’d forgotten to turn it off.

She looked up as he came in.

"Well?" she asked.

He hung up his coat, took a breath, and leaned in the doorway.

"I said no again," he said.

"Out loud? To their actual faces?" she pressed.

"Yes," he said.

"Were there violins?" she asked.

"Just cutlery," he replied.

She searched his face for cracks.

"Any regret?" she asked.

He considered.

"Yes," he said.

Her stomach dropped.

"Oh," she said.

"Not the kind you think," he added quickly. "Not ‘I should have gone.’ More…" He groped for the word. "Grief. For the version of me who would have."

She relaxed a fraction.

"You’re allowed to mourn the road you didn’t take," she said. "So long as you don’t spend the rest of our lives telling me about the scenery."

"I’ll keep the wistful monologues to a minimum," he promised.

She patted the chair beside her.

"Sit," she said. "Tell me everything. I want to know exactly what I’ve stolen you from."

He sat. He talked.

About the lab, the funding, the way Weiss’s eyes had lit up when they got into the weeds of design. About Anna’s sensible questions. About the moment he’d said "no" out loud and felt his lungs open and close properly for the first time in months.

"Do you feel smaller?" she asked when he finished.

He thought about it.

"No," he said. "I feel… located."

"Good," she said softly.

They fell into a comfortable quiet.

"How’s Sarah?" he asked.

"Asleep," Lillian said. "She had a call with Elaine."

His ears pricked.

"Already?" he asked.

"They just said hello," she replied. "Voices, not tales of trauma. Baby steps. I stood at the bottom of the stairs and eavesdropped like a responsible parent."

"How did it sound?" he asked.

"Like two people trying not to scare each other," she said. "It was… gentle, I think."

He exhaled.

"We’re all making choices," he said.

"Look at us," she replied. "A regular family of decision-makers."

He reached across and took her paw.

"Thank you," he said.

"For what?" she asked.

"Not coming tonight," he said. "Letting me do it."

"I wasn’t letting you do anything," she said. "I was protecting the poor Germans from my opinions."

"Still," he said.

She squeezed his paw.

"Next time you have to turn down a life-changing opportunity," she said, "I’m charging a consultancy fee."

He smiled.

"Deal," he said.

---

Later, in bed, with the light off and the familiar weight of the duvet anchoring them, Miles stared at the ceiling.

"Do you think," he said into the dark, "that saying no to something big means I have to say yes to something else? To justify it."

"No," Lillian said. "But you might." 

"What does that mean?" he asked.

"It means," she said, "that if you passed on Berlin to stay here and then just… emotionally moved into your shed for the next decade, I would feel somewhat cheated."

He huffed.

"So I have to be present," he said.

"Afraid so," she replied.

"Very demanding, this marriage," he said.

"You knew that going in," she replied.

He turned his head toward her in the dark.

"Do you ever wish you’d said yes to something else?" he asked. "Some big offer. Tour. Record deal."

She thought about smoky back rooms, near-misses, half-promises.

"All the time," she said. "And then I think about this bed. This house. The way our fridge makes that horrible noise at three a.m. And I think… well. At least the acoustics are good here."

He smiled into the pillow.

"We’re very bad at being ambitious," he said.

"On the contrary," she replied. "We’ve chosen the most ambitious project of all: staying."

He considered this.

"You should put that on a flyer," he said.

"I’ll get Penny to design it," she replied.

Down the hall, Sarah turned over in her sleep, dreams full of unfamiliar voices saying her name gently. In a flat elsewhere in the city, Penny shuffled her cards and smiled at a pattern she couldn’t quite name.

In Berlin, two scientists closed a file and opened another.

In Islington, a house creaked, settled, and held.
# Episode 9: The Silent Room

The first sign that something was wrong was the kettle.

Halfway through its usual shrill whistle, it choked on itself and cut out.

Lillian frowned at it.

“Don’t you dare,” she said.

The kitchen lights flickered in sympathy, then surrendered.

The house exhaled.

The hum of the fridge stopped. The low growl of the boiler died. Somewhere in the distance, a neighbour’s television gave up mid-sentence.

Darkness dropped over the room like a blanket.

“Oh,” Lillian said into it.

Then, because old habits died hard: “Miles?”

From somewhere above her head came a muted thud and a very dignified, “Ow.”

“You alive?” she called.

“Mostly,” he replied. “Stay where you are, I’ll—”

The sentence ended in another thud.

Lillian rolled her eyes and fumbled her way along the counter.

“Or,” she muttered, “the blind badger who has memorised this kitchen could light a candle.”

Her paw closed around the matchbox in its usual drawer. Two strikes later, a small orange flame bloomed.

She lit the candle that sat on the table for “ambience” (Rosalia’s word, not hers) and watched the soft light push the dark back a little.

The kitchen reshaped itself around that glow: table, chairs, the stack of unpaid bills in the corner, the Basil Plant of Perpetual Judgment on the sill.

The kettle sulked on its base.

Mozzarella padded in, nails clicking on the tiles, ears pricked.

“It’s fine,” Lillian told him. “The universe has just decided we need to sit in our feelings without Wi-Fi for a bit.”

He sat heavily at her feet as if bracing for impact.

Moments later, Miles appeared in the doorway, one paw pressed to his forehead.

“Fuse box?” Lillian asked.

“Probably,” he said, squinting. “Whole street looks out.”

He nodded toward the front. Through the thin curtains, the usual glow of other people’s bad lighting was conspicuously absent.

“Oh good,” Lillian said. “If we die, at least it’ll be communal.”

He came to the table and sat down opposite her.

The candle cast odd shadows on his face, making him look older and younger at the same time.

“I’ll go down and reset it in a minute,” he said.

“Or…” she said.

He arched a brow.

“Or,” she repeated, “we could sit. In the dark. Like Victorian ghosts. And—brace yourself—talk.”

He huffed a soft laugh.

“We do talk,” he said.

“We talk about bins and bus timetables,” she said. “We talk around things. We are Olympic-level avoiders.”

He looked down at his paws.

“Is this about the shed?” he asked.

“Everything’s about the shed,” she said. “The shed is a metaphor now. You’ve made it a metaphor by refusing to admit it’s a metaphor.”

He smiled, despite himself.

“You know I rebuilt it because it was rotting,” he said.

“And because you’re rotting,” she said.

There it was.

The too-sharp line.

He flinched.

Immediately, she regretted it.

“Sorry,” she said, softer. “That came out harsher than I meant.”

He swallowed.

“It’s fine,” he murmured.

It wasn’t.

They both knew it.

The candle flickered between them.

---

The power cut did not, of course, only affect them.

Within minutes, there was a knock on the back door.

Ramon’s silhouette loomed, holding aloft what looked suspiciously like a bottle.

“Apocalypse?” he called. “I brought wine.”

Lillian rose and opened the door.

“Come in,” she said. “We’re just embarking on an emotional excavation. You can be our foreman.”

Ramon’s eyes adjusted to the candlelit kitchen.

“Ooh,” he said. “Atmosphere. Very ‘intervention’.”

“Absolutely not,” Miles said.

Too quickly.

Ramon’s eyebrows did a little dance.

“I only came because Rosalia’s oven died mid-lasagne,” he said, setting the bottle on the table. “We need to borrow your gas hob.”

“Our hob runs on electricity,” Miles said.

“Then we are truly doomed,” Ramon said gravely.

Lillian lit another candle and pushed it toward him.

“Stay,” she said. “Commiserate. If we’re going to sit in a gothic tableau, we might as well have an audience.”

He sank into a chair.

“Why are you two looking like you’re about to confess to murder?” he asked.

“Because your friend here is hiding a body,” Lillian said.

“Lill,” Miles warned.

She met his eyes through the wavering light.

“I found your practice German app on the tablet,” she said. “Again.”

Ramon blinked.

“He’s learning German?” he said. “At his age?”

“Apparently,” Lillian said. “And he mysteriously vanishes into the garden every evening to commune with our new wooden therapist. And he says he’s ‘taking a look’ at things. Which is what he said when the dishwasher started making that noise just before it died.”

Miles took a slow breath.

“It’s a good language to know,” he said weakly.

Ramon looked between them.

“Okay,” he said. “Either you are moving to Berlin or you are planning to seduce a Teutonic plumber. Which is it?”

Lillian barked out a laugh that was only half real.

“That,” she said, “is the question.”

Miles’s shoulders tightened.

“This isn’t—” he began.

The fridge attempted to hum back to life, failed, and died again.

The candle sputtered but held.

“Do you want to tell him,” Lillian asked, voice suddenly flat, “or shall I?”

Ramon sat up a little straighter.

“Tell me what?”

Miles stared at the table.

“It’s nothing,” he said.

“It’s not nothing,” Lillian snapped. “Nothing doesn’t come with a salary and relocation package.”

Ramon went very still.

“Ah,” he said quietly.

---

The power cut, for all its drama, was not total.

After twenty minutes, a small, stubborn glow appeared in the corner.

The Wi-Fi router, inexplicably, had resurrected itself.

“Of course,” Lillian muttered. “The internet survives the apocalypse. It’ll be cockroaches and cat videos in the end.”

Her own phone buzzed on the table, screen lighting briefly with a notification.

The glow spilled across the wood, illuminating a scattering of mundane objects: a supermarket receipt, a half-finished crossword, a corner of paper with neat, small handwriting.

She frowned.

“What’s that?” she asked.

Miles followed her gaze.

His stomach dropped.

“Nothing,” he said, too fast.

She reached out.

He didn’t stop her.

Her paw closed on the edge of the paper.

Underneath the router’s little green lights, the letter’s header gleamed: a neat corporate logo, an address in Berlin.

Her name was not on it.

His was.

She picked it up.

“Dear Mr Bark,” she read aloud.

Ramon made a small, involuntary noise.

“Lillian,” Miles said. “Please.”

She ignored him.

“‘We were delighted to meet you at the conference last year,’” she went on, voice climbing. “‘And, following subsequent discussions, would like to formally offer you the position of—’”

She broke off.

The candle hissed.

“You printed it,” she said.

He opened his mouth.

Closed it.

“You printed it and left it under the router,” she said. “Like a little shrine to indecision.”

Ramon cleared his throat.

“I can… go,” he offered.

“No,” Lillian said sharply. “Stay. You’ve been through enough of our worst bits, you might as well see this one.”

“This isn’t a bit,” Miles said quietly.

“Isn’t it?” she shot back. “The supportive wife, the noble husband, the letter from a better life. It writes itself.”

The silence that landed then was different from the one the power cut had made.

This one had edges.

Mozzarella whimpered under the table.

Sarah wasn’t there.

She was at Clare’s that night, a deliberately scheduled sleepover that Lillian had agreed to with dignity and had then spent the afternoon resenting.

Now, a small, selfish part of her was glad her daughter was missing the show.

“How long?” she asked.

Miles stared at his paws.

“A few weeks,” he said.

“Weeks,” she repeated.

“They first emailed just after Sarah’s graduation,” he said. “Then again after…”

He gestured vaguely.

The shed. The potluck. The thousand tiny domestic scenes he’d been building around.

“And you didn’t tell me,” she said.

He winced.

“I wanted to be sure there was something to tell,” he said. “I didn’t want to worry you if it was nothing.”

“It’s not nothing,” she said, shaking the paper. “It’s literally an offer. That’s the opposite of nothing. That’s something.”

Ramon took a slow sip of wine, eyes wide.

“Do you… want it?” he asked tentatively.

Miles’s throat worked.

“I don’t know,” he said.

Lillian laughed.

“You know,” she said. “You just don’t want to say it in front of the matching spice rack.”

“Lill—”

“Do you want to go?” she asked, voice suddenly raw. “Yes or no.”

The candle flickered between them, casting his hesitation in slow motion.

“Yes,” he said.

The word dropped like a stone.

Then, before she could swallow it whole:

“And no.”

She frowned.

“That’s not how this works,” she said weakly.

He spread his paws.

“It is,” he said. “In my head. I want… the work. The challenge. The version of me who exists in that hospital, in that city. I wanted it for years. Before you. Before this.”

He nodded toward the house around them.

“And now?” she asked.

He looked at her.

At the candlelight catching the familiar lines of her face. At the stack of sketches on the counter. At the muddy pawprints on the floor.

“Now,” he said slowly, “I want… this. More. But I don’t… quite trust that wanting yet. It feels like…

He searched for the word.

“Like I’m failing some version of myself who didn’t know you,” he finished.

Lillian stared at him.

For a terrifying second, she had no joke.

“You think staying is failure,” she said.

“No,” he said quickly. “I think… I’ve been told my whole life that leaving is success. That moving is growth. That saying yes to the shiny thing is the brave choice. And now there’s this part of me that looks at the shed and the garden and… you, and thinks… maybe not. Maybe the brave thing is…

He gestured helplessly.

“Staying,” she supplied.

He nodded.

“But it’s harder to explain at parties,” he said.

Ramon made a choked noise that might have been a laugh.

“I would go to that party,” he said.

Lillian put the letter down very carefully.

“You should have told me,” she said.

He closed his eyes briefly.

“I know,” he said. “I was… cowardly.”

“You were scared,” Ramon interjected. “It’s not the same.”

“It is when you used to pride yourself on being the brave one,” Miles said quietly.

Lillian’s chest ached.

“You’re the steady one,” she said. “That’s not the same as brave.”

He looked at her.

“Isn’t it?”

She thought of all the nights he’d sat with her on this very floor while she panicked about gigs and bills and being too much.

Of the mornings he’d gotten up with a sick child while she slept off a show.

Of the way he’d walked two paces behind Sarah on her first day of secondary school, just far enough to let her feel independent, just close enough to catch her if she tripped.

“You idiot,” she said, very gently. “You’re the bravest person I know.”

The router blinked approvingly.

---

Outside, someone’s smoke alarm began to shriek in earnest.

“Rosalia’s lasagne,” Ramon said, leaping to his feet. “I must go and rescue it before she blames Britain.”

He hesitated, glancing between them.

“Do you need me to… stay?” he asked.

Lillian shook her head.

“Go save the béchamel,” she said. “We’ll… we’ll cope.”

He nodded, squeezed Miles’s shoulder, and slipped out the back door, taking the bottle with him.

They were alone.

Just them, the candle, and the letter.

The silence stretched.

Mozzarella sighed and flopped onto his side, tail thumping once.

“Do you want me to tell you not to go?” Lillian asked at last.

He considered.

“Part of me does,” he said. “Part of me wants you to forbid it. So I can resent you instead of myself.”

“Honesty,” she said. “Hot.”

He smiled weakly.

“But the rest of me,” he went on, “wants you to say… whatever you actually feel. Even if it’s… ‘go’.”

She snorted.

“You think I’m capable of saying ‘go’?” she asked. “To you? To my…

She flapped her paw uselessly.

“You,” she finished.

He held her gaze.

“I don’t know,” he said. “You told Sarah to.”

That landed.

She looked away.

“That was different,” she said.

“How?”

“She’s… young,” Lillian said. “She has to… go. Try. See her other life. So she can come back here on purpose, not by accident. You’re…

She waved at him.

“Old?” he supplied.

“Set,” she said. “Rooted. This is your…

She gestured around.

“This is where you built your life,” she said. “With me. With Sarah. With that bloody basil plant that refuses to die.”

He swallowed.

“So if I left,” he said slowly, “that would mean… what?”

She opened her mouth.

Closed it.

“That it wasn’t enough,” she said finally. “That I wasn’t.”

The words hung between them, heavy as wet laundry.

He flinched.

“Love,” he said. “No.”

“Then why is this even…” She tapped the letter. “Why is this still on the table?”

He reached across and covered her paw with his.

“Because I’m scared of being small,” he said. “And this—” he looked around “—is small. On paper. In other people’s eyes. ‘He stayed in a semi in Islington and fixed things.’ It doesn’t look… impressive.”

She stared at him.

“Do you hear yourself?” she asked.

He nodded.

“Unfortunately,” he said.

She squeezed his paw.

“Do you remember,” she said slowly, “when we first moved in, and my mother came to see the place and said, ‘It’s very… compact’?”

He smiled faintly.

“I do,” he said. “She meant ‘small’.”

“She meant ‘small’,” Lillian agreed. “And I stood in this kitchen and thought, ‘Yes. Good. Containable. I can fill this.’ For the first time in my life, I wasn’t trying to squeeze myself into something too big for me emotionally, or too small for me physically. It was… right.”

She looked at him hard.

“You did that,” she said. “You made it right. You built… us. Here. You think that’s small?”

He blinked fast.

“The world is big,” he said.

“So is this house when you’re the one doing the washing up,” she said. “Scale is relative.”

The candle had burned low, wax pooling at its base.

The router blinked, valiantly trying to reconnect with a world that, for the moment, did not matter.

“What do you want?” she asked.

He took a breath.

Let it out.

“I want…”

The word snagged.

He tried again.

“I want to want to stay,” he said. “Without feeling like I’ve failed some test.”

She let out a shaky laugh.

“You and Sarah,” she said. “With your bloody tests.”

He smiled, just a little.

“You told her she didn’t have to choose between us and Clare,” he said. “That she could be both. Here and there. Golden Retrievers are apparently excellent at dual citizenship.”

“I am a fountain of wisdom,” she said dryly.

“Maybe,” he went on, “I need to believe that about myself. That the version of me who goes to Berlin can exist in my head. In… emails. In what-ifs. Without… actually going.”

He tapped the letter.

“And that the version who stays,” he added, “isn’t… lesser.”

She watched him.

“I can’t make that choice for you,” she said.

“I know,” he said.

“I can only tell you,” she went on, voice unsteady, “that if you go, I will survive. I will cry and write twelve angry songs and probably move Penny into your side of the bed out of spite, but I will… survive. I’ve been alone before.”

He winced.

“If you stay,” she said, “I will still sing. I will still be annoying. I will still occasionally resent you for not being on some glamorous rooftop in Berlin. But I will also…

She swallowed.

“I will also think you’re the bravest man I know,” she finished.

Silence.

Mozzarella snored.

The faint beep-beep of life returning to a neighbour’s microwave drifted through the wall.

The house, old and nosy, listened.

Miles looked at the letter.

At her.

He reached out and folded the paper in half.

Then in half again.

He didn’t tear it.

Not yet.

He placed it in the middle of the table.

“When the lights come back on,” he said, “I’ll move this. Off the router. Off the table. Somewhere… less altar-like.”

She nodded slowly.

“Okay,” she said.

He squeezed her paw again.

“And I’ll tell you when I answer,” he added.

She met his gaze.

“Promise?”

“Promise,” he said.

---

The power returned with an almost sheepish flicker.

The kitchen lights blinked on. The fridge hummed back to life. The kettle, startled mid-sulk, began to heat again.

They both squinted.

The candle, reduced to a stub, guttered.

Lillian pinched the wick between two fingers, extinguishing it.

For a moment, the afterimage of its flame danced at the edges of her vision, a ghost of the small, contained world they’d occupied for the last hour.

Mozzarella stretched and wandered off in search of crumbs.

Miles stood.

He picked up the letter.

For a second, she thought he was going to tear it up after all.

Instead, he folded it one more time and slipped it into the drawer where they kept takeaway menus and spare batteries.

“Symbolic,” she said.

“Messy,” he corrected. “But… less shrine-y.”

She smiled faintly.

“Progress,” she said.

He reached for the kettle.

“Tea?”

“Always,” she said.

He filled the mugs.

The ordinary clink of ceramic on wood felt, bizarrely, like a relief.

“You know,” she said, as he stirred, “for a silent room, that was a lot of talking.”

He laughed softly.

“It was,” he agreed.

She looked around.

The same kitchen. The same table. The same slightly peeling paint.

And yet.

“Do you think,” she asked, “we’ll remember what we said when the lights were off?”

He set a mug in front of her.

“I will,” he said.

She believed him.

Outside, the street slowly woke back up: televisions, kettles, arguments about fuses.

Inside, for the first time since the Berlin offer had arrived, the house felt…

Not settled.

But less cracked.

The fault line had been named.

The room, briefly, had been truly silent.

Now it was noisy again.

Their kind of noisy.

Lillian lifted her mug.

“To small lives,” she said.

Miles clinked his against hers.

“To brave ones,” he said.

# Episode 10: Sarah's Train

The platform smelled like coffee and old announcements.

Not a bad smell, as train station smells went. No rancid chips. No suspicious puddles. Just the faint tang of brake dust and the ghosts of a thousand commute Mondays.

Lillian hated it.

She stood under the departures board, clutching a paper cup that had once contained tea and now contained only her own rapidly cooling fingers.

“It’s just Brighton,” Miles said quietly beside her.

“Nothing good has ever started with ‘it’s just’,” she said. “It’s just a haircut. It’s just a text. It’s just a small wolf with a knife.”

He blinked.

“We don’t live in that sort of fairy tale,” he said.

“We do now,” she said. “Our daughter is about to get on a train to meet the woman who literally grew her. That’s very ‘mysterious stranger appears at the edge of the forest’ behaviour.”

He looked out along the tracks.

The electronic sign above them read: *Brighton – On time – 09:42*.

Sarah sat on a metal bench a few feet away, hood up, rucksack at her feet. Her sketchbook lay open on her knees, pencil hovering. She wasn’t drawing.

Every few seconds, she glanced up at the clock, then down again, as if time might behave better if she pretended not to watch it.

Lillian watched her instead.
