Her girl. Their girl. The one who had arrived in their lives with a social worker and a battered suitcase full of clothes that didn’t quite fit.

“We could still push her onto a different train,” Lillian muttered. “Like, surprise, you’re going to Cleethorpes.”

“She would get off at the next stop and walk back,” Miles said.

“She’s very stubborn,” Lillian agreed.

He nudged her gently.

“Where do you think she got that from?”

“Ramon,” she said without missing a beat.

He laughed softly.

---

The email from Clare had been sitting in Sarah’s inbox for two weeks before she’d shown them.

“I didn’t want to… I don’t know,” she’d said at the kitchen table, staring at the laptop screen. “Make it real.”

The subject line had been simple: *Hello, Sarah.*

The body, somehow, simpler and more complicated at once.

I’ve thought about writing to you for years.

If you don’t want to hear from me, that’s okay. You don’t have to reply.

If you do, I would love to meet you. On your terms. Somewhere neutral. No pressure.

There had been a phone number. A few lines about Brighton. A mention of ceramics, which had made Sarah’s breath catch.

“You don’t owe her anything,” Lillian had said automatically, protective instincts flaring.

Sarah had nodded, eyes wet.

“I know,” she’d said. “That’s kind of the point. I want to talk to her when I don’t owe her. When I’m… me. Not a baby or a…

She’d gestured vaguely.

“A case,” Miles had supplied gently.

She’d nodded again.

“Yeah,” she’d said. “A me who chose.”

Now, at the station, the choice was a train away.

---

“Forty minutes,” Miles said, glancing at the timetable. “An hour, if the universe wants to be dramatic.”

“The universe is already being dramatic,” Lillian said. “Have you met it?”

He gave her the look that meant, *I am about to suggest breathing exercises and I know you will mock me but I’m going to do it anyway.*

“Do you want to sit?” he asked instead.

She shook her head.

“If I sit, I’ll have to get up again,” she said. “I prefer to maintain a constant state of readiness, like a fridge.”

“Fridges don’t—”

“Let me have this,” she said.

He let her.

Across the platform, a family with small children attempted to corral them into some semblance of order. A man in a suit scrolled through his phone with grim determination. A teenage fox in headphones swayed gently, oblivious to everything.

Life went on.

Their life had, for years, been stably chaotic: school runs, gigs, neighbour drama, dog hair.

Now, a new axis had appeared.

“You know we’re not being replaced, right?” Miles said quietly.

Lillian squinted at him.

“You know that,” he repeated.

She opened her mouth to say of course, obviously, don’t be ridiculous.

What came out was:

“What if she’s better at it?”

He blinked.

“At what?”

“At… being her mum,” Lillian said, the word dropping heavier than she meant. “What if she’s… calmer. Or less…

She waved a paw at herself.

“Loud?” he offered.

“Excessive,” she said.

He considered her for a long moment.

“Do you remember when Sarah was thirteen and you tried to help her with her science homework and ended up giving a twenty-minute lecture on the patriarchy instead?” he asked.

“The patriarchy is a key part of any curriculum,” she said.

“She handed it in as-is,” he went on. “The teacher wrote, ‘Interesting, but not technically about photosynthesis.’”

Lillian’s mouth twitched.

“Your point?”

“My point,” he said, “is that you have never been… generic. And she has never once wanted you to be. She doesn’t need another you. She might need… a Clare. For a bit. To see where her nose comes from. To hear a different story. But she chose you first. And she’s choosing you now.”

“She’s choosing a train,” Lillian muttered.

“With us on the platform,” he said.

She hated that he was right.

She loved that he was right.

---

“Platform 3 for the 09:42 service to Brighton,” the announcer said in that particular station voice that sounded like it had seen some things.

Sarah stood.

The motion was small, but it made Lillian’s heart trip.

“You don’t have to—” she began.

“I know,” Sarah said.

She slung her rucksack over one shoulder, sketchbook tucked inside. She’d agonised the night before about what to bring. In the end, she’d settled on that: pages and pages of the life she’d had, in case the life she might have had needed to see it.

Lillian and Miles moved to stand beside her.

“We’ll walk you to the carriage,” Miles said.

“Like you do for toddlers,” Lillian added.

Sarah’s mouth quirked.

“Please don’t lick my face,” she said.

“No promises,” Lillian replied.

They trailed down the platform together as the train slid in, long and silver and indifferent.

Doors hissed open.

People spilled out, others flowed in.

“Remember,” Lillian said, words tumbling over each other, “you don’t have to tell her anything you don’t want to. You can lie. I fully endorse lying in this context. Say we live on a boat. Say your dad is an astronaut. Say I am a quiet, demure hedgehog.”

Sarah laughed, sharp and bright.

“That last one might be a stretch,” she said.

“I’m very versatile,” Lillian said.

Miles reached out and squeezed Sarah’s shoulder.

“If at any point you want to come home,” he said, “text me ‘lasagne.’ I’ll call and say there’s been a family emergency.”

“Is the emergency that Mum has eaten all the lasagne?” Sarah asked.

“Possibly,” he said.

Her smile wobbled.

“Okay,” she said. “Thanks.”

They reached an empty set of seats near the middle of the train.

“This one?” Miles asked.

Sarah nodded.

She turned to them.

For a moment, no one spoke.

Then she stepped into Lillian’s arms.

The hug was not the frantic, clinging kind of years ago when nightmares had sent her scrabbling for safety.

It was firm. Grounded.

“I love you,” Sarah said into her fur.

Lillian’s lungs squeezed.

“I love you more,” she said, because it was legally required.

Sarah pulled back and hugged Miles too.

He held her with the same measured strength he used for heavy beams and fragile things.

“Have a good day,” he said.

She snorted.

“That’s a low bar,” she said.

“Okay,” he amended. “Have whatever day you need. And then come home.”

Her eyes shone.

“I will,” she said.

The guard blew the whistle.

She climbed on.

They stepped back to the platform.

Through the window, she found them again.

She placed a paw on the glass.

Lillian did the same, matching it.

Miles, feeling ridiculous and not caring, added his.

For a second, it looked as if they were trapped in a very niche art installation about intergenerational attachment.

Then the train lurched.

The platform began to slide away.

Lillian’s throat burned.

She kept her eyes on Sarah as long as she could, until the shape of her blurred into the shapes of other people, then into the dull smear of speed.

The train rounded the curve and was gone.

The platform hummed.

Someone’s suitcase creaked. A pigeon strutted importantly past a discarded crisp packet.

Lillian felt like someone had peeled off a layer of her and put it on rails.

---

They didn’t move.

For a long moment, they simply stood there, side by side, looking at the space the train had occupied.

“We could go home,” Miles said eventually.

“We’re at a train station,” Lillian said. “We’re supposed to sit in liminal agony until something arrives.”

He considered this.

“Tea?”

She nodded.

They found a bench halfway down the platform and sat.

The station café offered tea in flimsy cups and what it optimistically called pastries.

Miles returned with two cups and something that might once have been a croissant.

“This is a hate crime against France,” Lillian said, eyeing it.

“Symbolic,” he said. “Everything is flaky.”

“See, this is why I married you,” she said. “You understand metaphors.”

They sat in companionable misery, sipping tea that tasted faintly of cardboard.

Around them, the station continued its own stories.

An elderly badger argued with the ticket machine.

Two students lugged enormous backpacks and talked loudly about gap years.

A toddler yelled joyfully every time a train went past.

“Do you remember when we first brought her home?” Miles asked quietly.

“No,” Lillian said. “I blacked it out for legal reasons.”

He smiled.

“You paced up and down this platform for twenty minutes while we waited for the train from her foster carer’s town,” he said. “You wore a groove in the concrete.”

“I was auditioning for ‘Trainspotting: The Musical’,” she said.

“You were terrified,” he said.

“I am always terrified,” she said.

“You told me,” he went on, ignoring her, “that you didn’t know how to be someone’s mum. That you only knew how to be… you.”

She blew on her tea.

“That tracks,” she said.

“Then the train arrived,” he said, “and she got off holding that little suitcase, and you looked at her and said, ‘Well. That’s mine now.’”

Her mouth twitched.

“Possession is nine-tenths of the law,” she said.

“You didn’t ask yourself if you were enough,” he said. “You decided you would be.”

She stared at the opposite platform.

“I had no idea what I was doing,” she said.

“You still don’t,” he said.

“Rude,” she said.

“You’re still enough,” he added.

She swallowed.

“She’s going to look at this woman,” she said softly, “and see her own nose. Her own hands. Her own stupid cowlick in clay form. And then she’s going to come back and look at me and think, ‘Who is this loud stranger and why does she keep offering me soup?’”

“She already knows who you are,” he said. “You were the one who printed that email out and stuck it to the fridge.”

“To make sure we didn’t ignore it,” she said.

“To make sure she knew you weren’t afraid of it,” he corrected.

She snorted.

“I am afraid of everything,” she said.

“You are afraid and you still show up,” he said. “That’s… the definition of brave, isn’t it?”

She made a face.

“Stop auditioning for my song,” she said. “You’re going to make me write something sincere.”

He smiled.

“Can’t have that,” he said.

---

Time, that treacherous thing, moved.

Sarah’s train would be pulling into Brighton now. She’d step off, heart pounding, eyes scanning the crowd for a face she’d only seen in photos.

Lillian tried not to picture Clare.

She failed.

She imagined a taller version of Sarah. Or a shorter one. Similar ears, different laugh. A stranger with familiar eyes.

“Do you think she’ll like her?” she blurted.

“Clare?” Miles asked.

“Sarah,” Lillian said. “What if she doesn’t like her?”

He blinked.

“I can’t imagine anyone not liking Sarah,” he said.

“You didn’t like her at first,” she said.

He choked on his tea.

“I was terrified of breaking her,” he protested.

“Exactly,” she said. “What if Clare is terrified and mistakes it for dislike? What if Sarah is awkward and mistakes it for rejection? What if they both cry and I’m not there to misinterpret everything helpfully?”

He reached for her paw.

“If it goes badly,” he said, “she will come home and tell us. We will make tea. You will swear. I will suggest breathing exercises. She will roll her eyes at both of us. We will be here.”

“And if it goes well?” she asked.

He squeezed her paw.

“Then she will come home and tell us,” he said. “We will make tea. You will swear. I will suggest breathing exercises. She will roll her eyes at both of us. We will still be here.”

She stared at him.

“You’re very annoying when you’re right,” she said.

“I know,” he said.

---

After an hour, her phone buzzed.

She stared at the screen as if it were a live grenade.

Miles leaned over.

> Made it. She’s here. We’re having coffee. She brought a sketchbook. 
> 
>  Don’t panic. Love you. x

Attached was a photo, slightly blurry.

Sarah at a café table, mug in hand, eyes bright.

Beside her, a woman with similar eyes and different everything else.

They both looked… tentative. Curious.

Alive.

Lillian’s heart clenched.

“Well?” Miles asked.

She handed him the phone.

He studied the picture.

“They look… okay,” he said softly.

“They look like a before shot,” she said.

“Every moment is a before shot,” he said.

She rolled her eyes.

“I’m going to ban Penny from lending you her mindfulness books,” she said.

He smiled.

“Too late,” he said.

She typed back.

> Proud of you. Tell her you’re taken. See you at home. x

She hesitated.

Then added:

> PS: Remember the lasagne code.

Three dots appeared.

> lol. No lasagne needed yet. 
> 
>  She’s nervous too. That helps. x

Lillian exhaled.

“Nervous too,” she echoed.

“Level playing field,” Miles said.

They sat back.

The station announcements droned on.

Somewhere, a tannoy mispronounced “Letchworth.”

The world, in all its ridiculous detail, continued.

---

By the time Sarah’s return train pulled in that evening, the light had shifted.

The station looked the same and not.

Lillian and Miles stood in almost the exact same spot as that morning.

“Full circle,” Lillian murmured.

“Spiral,” Miles corrected. “Same place, different you.”

“You’ve been reading again,” she said.

The doors opened.

Sarah stepped off.

Her shoulders were hunched in that way she had when she was carrying more than her rucksack.

Lillian’s stomach flipped.

Then Sarah saw them and her face broke open.

“Hi,” she said, voice a little hoarse.

“Hi,” Lillian said back.

They didn’t pounce.

They let her come to them.

She did.

She hugged Miles first this time, burying her face in his chest for a moment.

“Okay?” he murmured.

She nodded into his fur.

Then she turned to Lillian.

“Well?” Lillian asked, because nuance was for people who hadn’t spent the day vibrating.

Sarah laughed, a little wild.

“It was…

She flapped her paws.

“A lot?” Miles suggested.

“Yeah,” she said. “Good a lot. Weird a lot. Fine a lot. She’s… kind. She’s not you, Mum—but she’s kind.”

Lillian’s vision blurred.

“That’s an acceptable review,” she said.

Sarah smiled.

“She makes mugs,” she added. “Like… proper ones. She showed me her studio. It’s small. Messy. Lovely. She said she always wondered if I liked making things. Like she does.”

“Genetics,” Lillian muttered. “Rude.”

“I told her I did,” Sarah went on. “And that I got to. Because of… you.”

Her eyes met Lillian’s.

“Both of you,” she corrected.

Lillian’s throat closed.

“Good answer,” she croaked.

“Ten out of ten diplomacy,” Miles agreed.

They started walking toward the exit.

“She gave me this,” Sarah said, fumbling in her bag.

She pulled out a small, slightly wonky mug.

It was glazed in a soft, sea-glass green.

On the bottom, in careful script, was written: *For Sarah. From Clare. For whatever you choose to drink.*

Lillian stared at it.

“I hate how tasteful that is,” she said.

Sarah laughed.

“We can put it on the shelf,” she said. “With the others.”

“It’ll throw off the symmetry,” Lillian said.

“Good,” Sarah said.

They stepped out into the evening air.

The route home was the same as it had always been: up the hill, past the newsagent, left at the corner where the paving stones wobbled.

But Sarah’s steps felt… different.

She walked a fraction ahead of them, then fell back, then matched their pace.

Orbiting.

At the front door, she paused.

“I’m glad I went,” she said.

“Me too,” Miles said.

“I’m glad you came back,” Lillian added.

Sarah rolled her eyes affectionately.

“You locked the door,” she said. “I had no choice.”

She pushed it open.

The familiar smell of their house — dog, garlic, paint, something sweet Lillian had forgotten she baked yesterday — washed over them.

Home.

“So,” Lillian said, as they stepped inside. “Tell us everything. Every awkward silence. Every badly timed toilet break. I want the director’s cut.”

Sarah smiled.

“Okay,” she said. “But you have to make proper tea. I’ve had train tea all day.”

Miles headed for the kettle.

Lillian reached for the new mug.

For a second, she hesitated.

Then she set it on the counter.

“Welcome to the collection,” she told it.

In the living room, Mozzarella barked once, announcing their return.

Upstairs, the house adjusted around the new information: there was now a woman in Brighton whose laugh sounded a bit like Sarah’s.

Downstairs, at the same old table, they sat.

Same mugs. New one.

Same stories. New chapter.

The train tracks that led away from them felt, for the first time, less like a threat and more like a line on a map they now had permission to redraw.

# Episode 11: Miles, Interrupted

The evening started with olives.

This, in itself, was not suspicious.

Olives featured heavily in any encounter with Ramon and Rosalia. They appeared at barbecues, at Tuesday-night debriefs, at impromptu “the council has annoyed us” summits.

But they did not usually arrive in a dish this large.

“That is a weapon, not a starter,” Lillian said, eyeing the bowl as Rosalia set it on their kitchen table.

“Food is never a weapon,” Rosalia said. “It is a tool.”

“For what?” Miles asked, slightly wary.

“For honesty,” Ramon said, breezing in behind his wife with two more plates. “And also for preventing you from running away mid-conversation.”

He set down a platter of croquetas with a flourish.

Miles blinked.

“Is something… wrong with the lasagne?” he asked.

“Nothing is wrong with anything,” Ramon said. “That is the problem.”

Lillian’s whiskers twitched.

“Oh God,” she said. “This is an intervention, isn’t it?”

Rosalia smiled pleasantly.

“We prefer the term ‘structured chat,’” she said.

“With tapas,” Ramon added.

Mozzarella, having caught the scent of fried things and emotional tension, stationed himself under the table like a furry understudy.

Miles looked from their neighbours to his wife.

“Did you… know about this?” he asked Lillian.

She lifted her paws.

“I knew there would be food,” she said. “I didn’t realise there would be…

She gestured vaguely.

“Feelings,” Ramon supplied.

“Those,” she said.

---

The table, once laid, was obscene.

Patatas bravas. Tortilla. Marinated peppers. Tiny toasts with things piled on them that Lillian refused to dignify by calling “canapés.”

“You know we’re only four people,” she said.

“Five,” Rosalia corrected, dropping a little dish of cheese cubes onto the floor for Mozzarella, who accepted the promotion with grace.

“We cook for the conversation we want,” Ramon said, uncorking a bottle of wine. “Tonight, we desire… frankness.”

Miles shifted in his chair.

“If this is about the time I used the wrong screws in the shelving unit—”

“It is not about the screws,” Ramon said.

“Nothing is ever about the screws,” Rosalia added.

Lillian made a face.

“Can we make it about the screws?” she asked. “I know what to do with those.”

Rosalia shook her head.

“No,” she said. “Tonight, we are talking about…

She glanced at Miles.

“The email,” she finished.

The Berlin email.

The letter had been moved off the table after the power cut, folded into the drawer. The knowledge of it had not gone anywhere.

Miles picked up his fork.

“I thought we covered this,” he said lightly. “There was a blackout, there was a confession, we all nearly cried. Very cathartic. Thank you for attending.”

Ramon raised a brow.

“You covered that it exists,” he said. “You did not cover what you are going to do about it.”

Miles stabbed a potato.

“I’m… thinking,” he said.

“He’s thinking very loudly,” Lillian said. “With wood. And lists. And slightly more aggressive weeding than usual.”

Rosalia leaned her elbows on the table.

“Mi amor,” she said. “We love you. We are not neutral parties. We want you to stay. But we also do not want you to wake up in twenty years and blame your choice on us. So we are going to ask you some questions.”

“This feels like therapy,” Miles muttered.

“You can pay us in leftover cake,” Ramon said. “Now. First question.”

He pointed his fork at Miles.

“What,” he asked, “would you miss most about your life here if you left?”

Miles blinked.

“This is very first-session stuff,” Lillian said. “Shouldn’t you ease him in with ‘tell me about your childhood’?”

“I am not interested in his childhood,” Ramon said. “I am interested in his present, which he is refusing to acknowledge.”

Miles looked at his plate.

He could have said the obvious: Lillian. Sarah. The house.

He didn’t.

“The… noise,” he said instead, surprising himself.

“Interesting,” Rosalia murmured.

Lillian scoffed.

“Lies,” she said. “You hate noise. You shush the kettle.”

“I hate uncontrolled noise,” he said. “Drills at 7am. Motorbikes. That blender the neighbour has that sounds like it’s chewing gravel. But this—” he gestured around “—this is… rhythm. I know what our house sounds like. I know which squeak on the stairs means Sarah is home. I know how your singing sounds through the shower door. I know the difference between the dog throwing up and the dog dreaming.”

“That last one is a terrifying skill,” Lillian said.

“In Berlin,” he went on quietly, “it would all be… new. For a while that would be exciting. Then it would be… loud. And I…

He trailed off.

Ramon topped up his wine.

“Good,” he said. “You would miss your domestic cacophony. Noted. Next question.”

Miles winced.

“How many are there?”

“As many as it takes,” Rosalia said.

“To achieve closure,” Ramon added.

“Or until we run out of olives,” Lillian said.

They all glanced at the bowl.

“We will be here all night,” Ramon decided.

---

“What scares you more,” Rosalia asked, “going or staying?”

Miles didn’t answer immediately.

He chewed. Swallowed. Took a sip of wine. Considered stalling.

“Staying,” he said at last.

Lillian’s ears flattened.

“Oh,” she said.

“Because it feels like giving up?” Ramon prompted.

Miles shook his head.

“Because it feels like… committing,” he said. “Like saying, ‘This is it. This is my life. No more contingency plans.’ I’ve always had…

He gestured vaguely.

“An escape hatch,” Lillian supplied.

“An alternate route,” he corrected. “A version of myself who got on that plane after uni and never looked back. He’s… neat. Easy to explain. He goes to conferences and has anecdotes about airports.”

“You could still go to conferences,” Lillian said. “You just wouldn’t live in one.”

He smiled.

“The version who stays,” he went on, “is… messier. He has opinions about paint colours and drainage. He knows which neighbour forgets bin day. He can’t blame his dissatisfactions on geography. If he’s unhappy, it’s because of… choices. His own.”

The table went quiet.

Ramon nodded slowly.

“So you are afraid,” he said, “that if you stay and you are not happy every second, you will only have yourself to blame.”

Miles winced.

“When you put it like that,” he said.

“Good,” Rosalia said. “We are getting somewhere.”

Lillian prodded a croqueta.

“Happiness isn’t… constant,” she said. “It’s not a…

She waved the fork.

“Tap you leave on,” she finished.

“That is the worst metaphor you have ever made,” Miles said.

“I’m under pressure,” she said.

He smiled, then sobered.

“I know,” he said. “Intellectually. I know that anywhere, even Berlin, would have bad days. Leaky roofs. Boring meetings. People who don’t stack dishwashers properly.”

“Monsters,” Ramon muttered.

“But this offer has been… held up as The Thing,” Miles said. “In my head. For years. ‘If they call, you go. Obviously.’ It’s like… a story I’ve told about myself. And now that it’s here, I’m realising the story might be… out of date.”

Lillian’s chest squeezed.

“Like skinny jeans,” she said.

“Exactly,” he said.

“So,” Rosalia said carefully, “if you said no… what story would you tell instead?”

He looked at her.

“I don’t know,” he said.

“Then that is the work,” she said simply.

---

They ate.

It helped.

Between questions, they veered off into other topics: Rosalia’s hunt for a decent flat near the sea, Ramon’s crusade against the new recycling schedule, Penny’s latest blog post about “micro-sanctuaries” that had somehow gone mildly viral.

Every so often, the conversation curled back to Berlin like steam.

“If you went,” Ramon said at one point, “what would you regret leaving behind?”

“You,” Lillian said immediately.

“The herb bed,” Miles said.

“Wow,” Lillian said.

He flushed.

“You are included in the herb bed,” he said. “You’re basil.”

“I am not basil,” she said. “I am… rosemary. Spiky, smells good when you roast things with me.”

“You’re both weeds,” Ramon muttered affectionately.

Miles smiled.

“I would regret,” he said more seriously, “not… seeing this through. The house. The shed. Sarah. We’re… in the middle of something. Several somethings. It feels… cowardly to walk away now.”

“Cowardly to stay. Cowardly to go,” Lillian said. “You’re very committed to being a coward.”

He laughed, surprised.

“Apparently,” he said.

Rosalia reached across the table and took his paw.

“Listen,” she said. “When we moved here, it was because Ramon’s job said go. So we went. We told ourselves we were brave. New country, new language, new everything.”

Ramon nodded.

“We were also running,” he said. “From our families. From expectations. From my mother’s lasagne.”

“No one runs from her lasagne,” Rosalia said. “They crawl toward it.”

“The point is,” he went on, “we thought movement was always courage. Only now, when we talk about moving again, to the sea, we are being very careful. Because this time, we are not running from. We are hopefully running toward. But also, maybe, just… sidestepping.”

“Side doors,” Lillian murmured.

Rosalia squeezed Miles’s paw.

“Sometimes,” she said, “the bravest thing is to admit you have already arrived.”

The room went quiet.

Even Mozzarella paused mid-chew.

Miles stared at their joined paws.

His whole life, big decisions had been framed as doors you either went through or didn’t. Opportunities you seized or missed.

No one had ever told him you were allowed to decide the room you were in was enough.

---

“Okay,” Lillian said briskly, stabbing another potato as if it had offended her. “New game. Everyone say one selfish reason they want Miles to stay. No noble crap. Pure selfishness.”

Ramon perked up.

“I like this,” he said.

“I’ll go first,” she said. “I don’t want to have to learn to use the boiler without you. I know I could. I don’t want to.”

Miles huffed.

“Noted,” he said.

“Me,” Ramon said. “I don’t want to do DIY alone. You are the only one who understands the correct length of a screw.”

“Sexiest thing anyone’s ever said to him,” Lillian stage-whispered.

Rosalia smiled.

“I don’t want to lose our wall-knocking code,” she said. “One knock: come for coffee. Two knocks: emergency. Three knocks: I have overcooked something and need you to lie.”

Miles laughed.

“I will miss the wall,” he admitted.

They all looked at him.

“Your turn,” Lillian said.

He hesitated.

“I don’t want to be the man who left his family for a job,” he said quietly. “Selfishly. Because I don’t want that to be the headline in my own head.”

Lillian’s eyes stung.

“That’s allowed,” she said.

Ramon nodded.

“Very on brand,” he said.

---

By the time the plates were mostly empty and the wine was half gone, something in Miles had shifted.

The indecision hadn’t evaporated. But its edges felt less sharp.

He could see, suddenly, that there were more than two options. Not just Berlin or failure. Not just leaving or cowardice.

There was also… here.

This messy, loud, olive-stained present.

“So,” Rosalia said, leaning back. “Homework.”

“There’s homework?” Miles asked.

“Of course,” she said. “Otherwise this is just dinner.”

“What do I have to do?”

“You have to write two letters,” she said. “One where you say yes. One where you say no. You do not send them. You read them aloud. To Lillian. To yourself. To the shed. I don’t care. But you put the words into the world. You see how they feel in your mouth.”

Lillian made a face.

“That sounds very…

She wiggled her paw.

“Therapeutic,” she finished.

“It is,” Rosalia said.

“And then?” Miles asked.

“Then,” Ramon said, “you delete the one that feels wrong.”

Miles laughed weakly.

“That simple,” he said.

“Simple is not the same as easy,” Rosalia replied.

He nodded.

“Okay,” he said.

“Okay?” Lillian echoed.

“Okay,” he repeated. “I’ll do it.”

She searched his face.

“Tonight?”

He hesitated.

“Soon,” he said.

She narrowed her eyes.

“Soon,” she repeated.

“We will check,” Ramon said cheerfully.

“I assumed,” Miles said.

---

Later, after the neighbours had waddled home with Tupperware full of leftovers and promises to knock on the wall the next day, the house was quiet again.

Lillian and Miles stood at the sink, doing the lazy dance of long-term couples: one washed, one dried, both moved around the other without really looking.

“How are you?” she asked, after a while.

He considered.

“Full,” he said.

“Emotionally or gastronomically?”

“Yes,” he said.

She snorted.

“You know they’re right,” she said.

“About the letters?”

“About you having already arrived,” she said.

He set a plate in the rack.

“Maybe,” he said.

She wiped her paws on a tea towel.

“Will you… let me be there?” she asked.

He blinked.

“For the letters,” she clarified quickly. “Not to dictate. Just to…

She flapped a paw.

“Witness?” he supplied.

“I was going to say ‘heckle,’” she said. “But sure, witness.”

He smiled.

“Yes,” he said. “I think… I’d like that.”

She exhaled.

“Okay,” she said.

He turned off the tap.

The house hummed.

Somewhere in the wall, a pipe clicked.

Through the plaster, faintly, came three quick knocks.

Ramon.

“See?” Lillian said. “The universe agrees.”

Miles rested his paw flat against the wall.

He could feel, in the vibration, not just his neighbour, but the whole network of connections he’d built without really noticing: knocks, shared meals, borrowed tools, late-night laughter.

He thought of Berlin.

He thought of two unsent letters.

He thought of the shed at the end of the garden, waiting.

“Soon,” he said again.

This time, it felt less like a delay and more like a promise.

# Episode 12: Roots and Wings

Sarah had always thought of phone calls as a kind of performance.

You had your opening line, your mid-episode ad break, your dramatic twist when the signal cut out and you both said "hello?" eight times in a row.

This one didn’t feel like a performance.

It felt like balancing on a tiny, wobbly bridge between two pieces of land that had only just been revealed to be the same country.

She sat on the back step with the phone pressed to her ear, bare paws on the cool stone, the garden smelling faintly of damp earth and last night’s rain.

Behind her, through the open kitchen door, she could hear the clink of crockery as Miles pretended to tidy and Lillian pretended not to hover.

In her ear, Clare laughed.

“Sorry,” Clare said. “I’m rambling.”

“You’re allowed,” Sarah said. “I asked.”

She meant it.

She had asked.

After the train. After the coffee. After the mug and the awkward hugs and the "so what did you listen to when you were fifteen?" game.

She had gone to Brighton, sat in that small, messy studio that smelled of clay and caffeine, and said the words out loud.

“Can we… keep talking?”

Clare had blinked quickly, then nodded.

“If you want to,” she’d said. “You set the pace.”

Now, a week later, they were pacing.

Gently.

On the phone.

“So,” Clare said now, on the other end of the line. “Your dad—that’s Miles, right? He seems… nice.”

Sarah smiled.

“Extremely,” she said. “Annoyingly. He apologises to chairs when he bumps into them.”

There was a soft huff of laughter.

“Good,” Clare said. “I like that.”

“And my mum,” Sarah added, pre-empting the question. “Is… a hurricane.”

“I got that impression from the emails,” Clare said dryly.

Sarah stared at the dandelions pushing up between the paving stones.

“She printed your first email out and put it on the fridge,” she said. “Like… art.”

“Did she?” Clare asked quietly.

“Yeah,” Sarah said. “So we wouldn’t pretend it wasn’t happening.”

There was a pause.

“I was… expecting to be the villain in her version,” Clare admitted.

Sarah snorted.

“Oh, you still are, sometimes,” she said. “But she’s very committed to nuance. It’s exhausting.”

Clare laughed again.

“I’d like to meet her,” she said, a little tentative.

Sarah picked at a chip in the step.

“Maybe,” she said. “Not yet. She’s…

She searched for a word that encompassed everything.

“…processing,” she finished.

“Fair,” Clare said.

---

Inside, Lillian pressed her back to the wall and mouthed, "What is she saying?" at Miles.

Miles, who had decided long ago that eavesdropping on your daughter’s deeply personal calls with her biological mother was a bad idea, shook his head.

“We agreed,” he said under his breath. “No listening.”

“I’m not listening,” she whispered back. “I’m… acoustically present.”

He gave her a look.

“Fine,” she sighed, retreating a few steps. “I’ll just… vibrate quietly over here like an anxious fridge.”

“You have never been quiet in your life,” he said.

She fidgeted with a tea towel.

“You think she’s okay?” she asked.

“Yes,” he said.

“How do you know?”

He considered.

“Her tail,” he said.

Lillian frowned.

“What about it?”

“It’s up,” he said simply.

She rolled her eyes.

“You can’t read everything off someone’s tail,” she said.

“Says the badger whose emotional state is directly correlated with whisker angle,” he replied.

She would have argued, but the back door creaked.

Sarah slipped inside, phone still in paw.

“Hey,” she said.

They both tried to look casual.

“So,” Lillian said in what she hoped was a breezy tone and was probably closer to strangled. “How’s… Brighton?”

“Still there,” Sarah said. “She’s fine. We talked about…

She waved the phone vaguely.

“Stuff,” she finished.

“Specific,” Lillian said.

Miles smiled.

“Do you want tea?” he asked.

“Yes,” Sarah said.

They moved into the familiar dance.

Kettle. Cups. Teabags.

The house, which had been holding its breath, exhaled.

---

“Have you talked about… why?” Lillian asked later, when it was just the two of them at the table.

Sarah picked at a biscuit.

“Why what?”

“Why she… you know.” Lillian rotated her paw uselessly.

“Let you go,” she finished.

Sarah’s jaw tightened.

“A bit,” she said. “Not like… a whole trauma TED Talk. She said she was… nineteen. And scared. And alone. And broke. And the social worker said…

She shrugged.

“‘This is the best thing for her,’” Lillian supplied quietly.

“Yeah,” Sarah said. “That line again.”

Lillian stared into her tea.

“I loathe that sentence,” she said.

“Sometimes it’s true,” Sarah said.

“Still loathe it,” Lillian said.

Sarah took a breath.

“She said she thought about me every day,” she went on. “At first I was like, ‘sure, okay,’ you know? But then she started listing stupid exact things.”

She smiled despite herself.

“Like how she couldn’t walk past the playground near her old flat for years,” she said. “Or how she couldn’t drink this one brand of instant coffee because she used to make it when she was pregnant and it made her sick.”

Lillian’s throat tightened.

“Details,” she said.

“Yeah,” Sarah said. “Details.”

“And how did that feel?” Lillian asked, very carefully.

Sarah stared at the table.

“Like someone had been secretly watching my origin story on DVD this whole time,” she said. “And suddenly decided to lend me the disc.”

Lillian nodded slowly.

“That’s a lot of metaphor for one cuppa,” she said.

Sarah huffed.

“You’re rubbing off on me,” she said.

“Good,” Lillian said. “You need more hyperbole.”

They sipped in silence for a moment.

“Do you… like her?” Lillian asked, in the tone of someone trying to sound neutral and failing.

Sarah thought.

“Yeah,” she said finally. “I think I do. She’s… kind. And awkward. And she keeps apologising for things I don’t need her to apologise for and not for the ones I do.”

“Classic,” Lillian muttered.

“But she listens,” Sarah added. “When I talk about you. Or about Dad. Or about… art. She doesn’t… flinch.”

Lillian blew out a breath she hadn’t realised she was holding.

“Okay,” she said.

“Okay?” Sarah echoed.

“Okay,” Lillian repeated. “I can work with ‘kind and awkward.’ I would have struggled more with ‘glamorous and judgmental.’”

Sarah smiled.

“She wears very comfortable jumpers,” she said.

“Oh, she’s fine then,” Lillian said. “Jumper people can be reasoned with.”

---

A few days later, the post arrived with an extra, unfamiliar envelope.

Miles picked it up, brow furrowing at the Brighton postmark.

“For you,” he said, handing it to Sarah.

Her ears flicked.

She slit it open with a claw.

Inside was a small card.

On the front, a simple drawing of a sprouting plant.

On the back, in neat handwriting:

> Dear Sarah,
> 
> 
> I didn’t want our last talk to be the only time I said this, so here it is in writing:
> 
> 
> Thank you for meeting me.
> 
> 
> You don’t owe me anything. You owe yourself everything.
> 
> 
> If staying in my life ever starts to feel like a duty instead of a choice, you have my full permission to take a break. Or stop. Or start again later.
> 
> 
> You are not responsible for fixing my past.
> 
> 
> Love,
> 
> Clare

There was a small doodle of a mug at the bottom.

Sarah read it twice.

Then a third time.

Then she handed it to Lillian without speaking.

Lillian read it.

Her eyes stung.

“Rude,” she muttered.

“Rude?” Miles asked.

“She’s… good at this,” Lillian said.

Sarah watched her.

“Are you… mad?” she asked.

Lillian blinked.

“At whom?”

“Her,” Sarah said. “You. The social worker. God. The concept of narrative irony.”

“Yes,” Lillian said. “All of the above.”

Sarah snorted.

“But mostly,” Lillian went on, “I am… grateful. That she is not trying to… claim you. Or… rewrite.

She waved the card.

“She’s… giving you permission to put yourself first,” she said.

Sarah shrugged.

“You did that too,” she said quietly. “When you told me I could go.”

Lillian looked away.

“Don’t bring my better moments into this,” she said gruffly.

Miles smiled.

Sarah slid the card back into its envelope.

“I want to keep seeing her,” she said. “Not every weekend. Not…

She searched for the shape of it.

“Not instead,” she finished. “Alongside. Like…

She gestured to the garden.

“Like when you plant something new in a bed that’s already got stuff growing,” she said. “You don’t dig everything up. You just… make space. Hope the roots don’t strangle each other.”

Miles’s chest ached.

“That’s… accurate,” he said softly.

“And if it doesn’t work?” Lillian asked, because she didn’t know how not to.

“Then we prune,” Sarah said simply.

---

That evening, Sarah sat at the kitchen table with her sketchbook open.

On one page, she drew the mug Clare had given her: the exact curve of its handle, the tiny imperfection in the glaze.

On the facing page, she drew one of Lillian’s favourite chipped mugs from the cupboard.

They didn’t match.

They didn’t have to.

Below them both, she wrote, in tiny letters: *and / and*.

Miles, passing through with a towel, paused.

“Nice,” he said.

“Practice,” she replied.

“For what?”

“For when I have to draw this in clay,” she said. “People’s faces are easy. It’s the spaces between them that are hard.”

He thought of his own in-between: the email in the drawer, the shed at the end of the garden, the life he was half-choosing and half-afraid of.

“Tell me about it,” he said.

---

Later that week, Penny came over with a stack of printed blog comments.

“People are obsessed with your little family saga,” she announced, dropping them on the table. “I posted about ‘holding two homes in one heart’ and half the internet showed up with tissues.”

Sarah eyed the pile.

“Do I want to read those?” she asked.

“Some of them,” Penny said. “Not the ones where people project their unresolved maternal issues onto the comments section. Those are mine.”

Lillian peered over.

“‘My adoptive mum and my bio mum both taught me how to make tea and now I cry whenever I boil water,’” she read aloud. “God, internet, calm down.”

She swiped at her eye.

Penny smirked.

“Point is,” she said, “you are not the only one doing this tightrope. It’s…

She shrugged.

“Common,” she finished.

Sarah nodded slowly.

“It feels very… specific,” she said.

“Specific is just common from very close up,” Penny said.

Miles looked at her.

“That’s actually quite wise,” he said.

“I know,” she said. “It’s disgusting.”

---

A few nights later, when the house was quiet and Mozzarella had finally given up lobbying for a second dinner, Sarah padded into the garden.

The shed glowed softly at the end, fairy lights twinkling.

The Den.

She opened the door.

Inside, the space smelled like wood and cables and faint, lingering chords.

Lillian sat on the stool with her guitar, notebook open on a crate. Miles hovered near the mixing desk, fiddling with levels just for something to do.

“Hey,” Sarah said.

“Hey,” Lillian said. “Come in. We’re… torturing a bridge.”

“It’s not torturing if it modulates,” Miles said.

Sarah smiled.

“Can I…

She gestured to the empty corner.

“Steal some floor?” she asked.

“Floor is free,” Lillian said. “Wall space, we charge for.”

Sarah sat cross-legged, sketchbook on her lap.

She watched them for a while: the tiny adjustments, the half-finished lyrics about kettles and courage.

“I’ve been thinking,” she said eventually.

“Oh no,” Lillian said.

Sarah rolled her eyes.

“I don’t want to move back to Brighton,” she said. “Not… as a big life move. I like visiting. I like Clare. I like the sea. But… my studio’s here. My classes are here. You’re here. I want…

She took a breath.

“I want to put my roots here,” she finished. “And my wings…

She waved vaguely toward the train tracks.

“…there,” she said.

Lillian stared at her.

“Did you steal that off a decorative cushion?” she asked.

“Maybe,” Sarah said.

Miles’s eyes shone.

“Good cushion,” he said.

“So,” Sarah went on, “I was thinking… I might talk to the community centre about running a regular workshop. And maybe… apply for that residency at the arts hub in town instead of the one in Brighton.”

Lillian blinked.

“I thought you wanted the Brighton one,” she said.

“I thought I had to want the Brighton one,” Sarah said. “Like it was the…

She curled her paw like Miles had at the table.

“Capital-O Opportunity,” she said. “But I realised… I can build something here that’s mine. Not just… borrow something someone else already built.”

Miles felt a strange, comforting echo in his chest.

“You can,” he said softly.

Sarah looked between them.

“Is that… okay?” she asked.

Lillian laughed, thickly.

“No,” she said. “It’s terrible. I’ll have to keep feeding you.”

Sarah smiled.

“Sorry,” she said, not sounding sorry at all.

Lillian put the guitar down and crossed the little room to hug her.

“Roots and wings,” she said into her fur. “Both. Greedy cow.”

Sarah hugged back.

“Takes one to know one,” she said.

Miles watched them, arms folded, heart stupidly full.

Outside, beyond their fence, a train rattled past in the distance.

Not an escape route.

Just a line.

One of many.

Inside, in the small, stubborn room they’d built, three lives adjusted around the new information.

Here, the roots deepened.

There, the wings stayed ready.

And somewhere in between, the space for both widened—just enough.
# Episode 13: The Garden Party

The invitation started as a distraction.

At least, that was how Lillian justified it to herself when she opened a fresh document and typed:

> NEIGHBOURHOOD GARDEN THING (NOT AT ALL A PARTY) – SAVE THE DATE

“Subtle,” Miles said, peering over her shoulder.

“It’s not a party,” she said. “Parties have expectations. This is… an outdoor coincidence.”

He smiled.

“An outdoor coincidence with fairy lights?”

“I’m not a monster,” she said.

Truthfully, she needed something to do with her paws that wasn’t refreshing her email or staring at Miles every time he walked past the cutlery drawer where the Berlin letter now lived.

Sarah had declared, after her second call with Clare, that she was “emotionally jetlagged” and had retreated into clay and naps.

Ramon and Rosalia were knee-deep in viewings and arguments about whether a balcony outweighed a gas hob.

Penny’s latest blog post had used the word “surrender” three times in a row.

The house hummed with unspoken conversations.

So: a garden thing.

“Why now?” Miles asked gently.

She shrugged.

“Because we haven’t had one since before Sarah went to Brighton,” she said. “Because everyone’s… in bits. Because if we don’t put all these people in one place soon, they’re going to drift off into their own storylines and we’ll have to watch via social media, and I refuse.”

He considered this.

“An ensemble episode,” he said.

“Exactly,” she said. “Bottle episode, but outdoors.”

He tilted his head.

“You know bottle episodes are indoors by definition,” he said.

“Don’t ruin this for me,” she said.

---

The flyers were pure Lillian.

Hand-lettered, a little chaotic, full of unnecessary punctuation.

> GARDEN THING!!!
> 
> 
> Food (bring some!)
> 
> Drinks (we have tea, obviously)
> 
> Music (no promises re quality)
> 
> Feelings (optional)
> 
> 
> Saturday, 3pm, our garden. Bring yourselves, your pets, your weird uncles.

“Do we want weird uncles?” Miles asked, reading.

“We already have Penny,” Lillian said. “How much worse can it get?”

They posted one through each letterbox on the street, plus a few to the immediate circle: Bea, a couple of Sarah’s college friends who still lived nearby, Penny (who didn’t need an invitation but got one anyway because it was shaped like a leaf).

“Do we invite Clare?” Sarah asked one evening, hovering in the kitchen doorway as Lillian assembled ginger biscuits with unnecessary aggression.

Lillian froze.

“Oh,” she said.

“She could come up,” Sarah went on. “Make a day of it. Or not. I don’t know. It might be…

She waved a paw.

“A lot,” Lillian supplied.

Sarah nodded.

“Yeah,” she said.

Lillian took a breath.

“Do you want her there?” she asked.

Sarah chewed her lip.

“Part of me does,” she admitted. “Part of me wants to see what it looks like. You, her, the house. In the same frame.”

Lillian’s stomach lurched.

“And the other part?”

“The other part wants to… have a day where I’m not thinking about who I belong to,” Sarah said. “Just… who I’m eating crisps with.”

Lillian nodded slowly.

“Then maybe this one’s…

She searched for the word.

“A Bark production only,” she said.

Sarah smiled faintly.

“Okay,” she said.

---

Saturday arrived with the kind of tentative sunshine that meant well.

Miles had mowed the lawn within an inch of its life. Sarah had strung fairy lights back and forth with the precision of someone hanging out her own nervous system.

Lillian had cooked.

And cooked.

The table inside sagged under bowls and trays.

“Is this enough?” she asked for the tenth time.

Miles looked at the spread: three salads, five dips, two quiches, a suspiciously large pile of sausage rolls, a cake that might have been lemon or might have been experimental.

“Yes,” he said.

“You didn’t even look properly,” she said.

“I did,” he said. “I looked with my soul.”

She huffed.

Penny arrived first, of course.

She swept into the kitchen carrying a basket of what she announced were “mindful crisps.”

“They’re just crisps,” Lillian said, peering in.

“Yes,” Penny said. “But we will eat them mindfully.”

“You’re banned from naming snacks,” Lillian said.

Ramon and Rosalia appeared next, bearing wine and a Tupperware the size of a small moon.

“Insurance,” Rosalia said, patting it.

“Against what?” Miles asked.

“British party food,” she replied.

Bea turned up with a tray of slightly uneven mini tarts.

“They’re… interpretive,” she said.

“Perfect,” Lillian said, meaning it.

Neighbours trickled in: the elderly badger from number twelve, the young fox couple from the corner house, the family with the eternally sticky twins.

By four o’clock, the garden was a patchwork of rugs, fold-out chairs, plates balancing on knees.

Music drifted from a speaker in the shed—soft, instrumental versions of Lillian’s songs that Sarah had declared “ambient enough.”

“It’s like living in my own Netflix montage,” Lillian muttered.

“Enjoy it,” Miles said. “You worked hard for this licensing deal.”

---

For a while, it was exactly what she’d wanted.

People talked.

About bins and buses and the scandalous price of courgettes.

About Rosalia’s upcoming move and whether Hastings had decent coffee.

About Sarah’s classes at the community centre and Bea’s “clay cult,” as Lillian insisted on calling it.

Kids darted between adults, sticky and loud.

Mozzarella made the rounds, gathering crumbs and compliments.

Every now and then, Lillian caught a glimpse of Miles leaning against the fence, laughing at something Ramon had said, or passing someone a drink, or quietly adjusting a chair so it didn’t wobble.

He looked… right.

Not like a man half-gone.

“See?” she muttered under her breath, as if the universe were listening. “He fits here.”

The universe, unbothered, continued rotating.

---

It wasn’t until nearly everyone had arrived that Lillian realised two people were missing.

“Where’s Penny’s cat?” she asked, scanning the garden.

“At home,” Penny said. “She doesn’t like crowds.”

“And… where’s your existential crisis?” Rosalia added lightly, nodding toward the house.

Lillian frowned.

“Miles?” she called.

He wasn’t on the patio.

He wasn’t by the grill (Ramon and Bea had somehow taken charge of that between them, an unlikely but effective combo).

He wasn’t in The Den.

She found him in the kitchen, standing very still in front of the sink, staring at the cutlery drawer.

The drawer where the letter lived.

“Miles?” she said, softer.

He jumped.

“Hi,” he said, too quickly.

“Hey,” she said. “The party—which is not a party, legally speaking—is out there.”

“I know,” he said.

“Are you… hiding?” she asked.

He opened his mouth.

Closed it.

“Maybe,” he admitted.

She came to stand beside him.

“Too many people?” she asked.

“Too many… futures,” he said.

She frowned.

He gestured vaguely toward the garden.

“Everywhere I look,” he said, “I see… what-ifs. Ramon and Rosalia leaving. Sarah… half here, half there. You…

He trailed off.

“Me what?” she pressed.

“You,” he said quietly, “building… this. This life. This community. This…

He waved toward the window.

“Table,” he finished lamely.

She followed his gaze.

Out there, under the bunting Sarah had made from scraps of old gig posters, their neighbours mingled.

Ramon teaching someone how to cut bread "properly". Penny holding court about meditation between bites of crisps. Sarah laughing with Bea, gesturing animatedly with clay-stained paws.

It was a small world.

It was also, undeniably, a whole one.

“You think that’s mine?” she asked softly. “That I built it?”

He shrugged.

“You started it,” he said. “With lasagne and loud opinions. I…

He looked down at his hands.

“I just… keep it from falling over,” he said.

She bumped her shoulder against his.

“You make it possible,” she said. “I make it loud.”

He smiled, a little.

She took a breath.

“Do you want to talk about it?” she asked. “The letters. The…

She gestured at the drawer.

“Or do you want to…

She nodded toward the sound of distant laughter.

“…avoid it for three more hours by feeding people?”

He considered.

“Both,” he said.

“Good,” she said. “We’re on the same page.”

---

As the afternoon stretched, the garden party (not a party) turned into what it was always going to be: a confessional with snacks.

The elderly badger from number twelve cornered Penny near the hydrangeas to talk about grief.

The young fox couple announced, to general delight, that they were expecting.

The sticky twins confessed to Sarah that they’d been “borrowing” her chalk for months.

“You can have it,” she said. “Leave me your parents’ email addresses as payment.”

Ramon, three glasses of wine in, declared loudly near the fence:

“We are moving to the coast and I am terrified!”

There was a ripple of laughter.

Rosalia squeezed his hand without looking.

“You love the sea,” she said.

“I love the sea when it is not full of my second thoughts,” he replied.

“Very on-brand content,” Penny muttered, fishing for a pen.

Miles watched it all.

He saw the web Lillian had woven almost accidentally over the years: favours, shared babysitting, borrowed ladders, swapped recipes.

He saw himself in it—threads that ran under the surface. The fixed gates, the shelves he’d put up in other people’s kitchens, the times he’d checked someone’s smoke alarm "just in case." 

He realised, with a jolt, that staying wasn’t just about his own four walls.

It was about this whole, ridiculous, interdependent ecosystem.

“Stop thinking,” Lillian murmured at his elbow, passing him a plate.

“I’m not,” he lied.

She laughed.

“You are,” she said. “I can see it in your ears.”

He looked at her.

“Are you… happy?” he asked abruptly.

She blinked.

“Now?”

“In general,” he said.

She considered.

“If I say yes, will you accuse me of settling?” she asked.

“No,” he said.

“Then… yeah,” she said slowly. “I am. Not all the time. Sometimes I want to set the council tax bill on fire and move to a cave. Sometimes I want to go on tour with a band half my age and make questionable life choices. But… mostly? Here. With you. With her. With…

She nodded toward the garden.

“…this,” she finished.

He swallowed.

“And if I said no to Berlin,” he asked, “would you ever… resent me?”

She barked out a laugh.

“Of course,” she said.

He froze.

She rolled her eyes.

“Not in a… capital-R way,” she said. “In a… normal way. The way I resent you now for not putting lids back on jars properly. I would occasionally look at you when you were being annoying and think, ‘You gave up a fancy hospital abroad to do this.’”

“Reassuring,” he said.

“But mostly,” she went on, “I would look at you making Sarah tea or fixing a neighbour’s fence or sitting on this patio with me for the thousandth time and think, ‘God, I’m glad he’s here.’”

He exhaled.

“Okay,” he said.

“Okay?”

“Okay,” he repeated. “That’s… clear.”

---

As dusk fell and the fairy lights took over, the party (still not a party) shifted into its softer phase.

Someone produced marshmallows.

Someone else produced stories.

On the grass near the back fence, Sarah lay on her back, Bea beside her, both staring up at the darkening sky.

“Do you feel different?” Bea asked.

“Since… Brighton?”

“Since all of it,” Bea said. “The emails. The visits. The…

She drew a vague circle in the air.

“Yeah,” Sarah said.

“How?”

Sarah thought.

“Less… hollow in one corner,” she said. “But not… less here.”

She tapped her chest.

“It’s like someone opened a window in another room of my brain,” she said. “But this room’s still the one where I keep my stuff.”

Bea nodded slowly.

“That’s…

She flapped her hand.

“Very poetic,” she finished.

Sarah snorted.

“You live with me,” she said. “It’s contagious.”

Across the lawn, Penny cornered Miles with a notebook.

“You’re staying,” she said, as if reporting the weather.

He started.

“I haven’t decided,” he said.

She tilted her head.

“You’re staying,” she repeated. “I can hear it in the way you talk about the compost.”

He frowned.

“That’s not a thing,” he said.

She shrugged.

“I’m very intuitive,” she said. “Also, you’ve stopped saying ‘if we’re still here next year’ and started saying ‘when we redo the bathroom.’ That’s the language of a man nesting.”

He huffed.

“Maybe I’m just…

He searched for a deflection.

“Pro-bathroom,” he finished.

“Sure,” she said.

He looked out at the garden.

At Lillian laughing with Rosalia over something burnt. At Sarah and Bea tracing constellations on nothing. At Ramon telling a story with his whole body.

He imagined a party like this in Berlin.

He couldn’t.

Or rather, he could, but it was faint. Sketchy.

This one was in full colour.

“Soon,” he said under his breath, thinking of the letters.

Penny pretended not to hear him.

But she smiled.

---

Later, when the neighbours had drifted away, leaving behind a respectable amount of debris and one mysteriously unclaimed Tupperware, the three of them sat alone under the fairy lights.

The garden looked bigger in the half-dark.

“So,” Lillian said, leaning back in her chair. “Garden thing: success?”

“Successful distraction,” Miles said.

“Distraction from what?” Sarah asked, head tilted.

“Existential dread,” Lillian said. “Council tax. The inevitability of decay.”

“Very chill,” Sarah said.

She looked around.

“I liked it,” she added. “Everyone in one place. All the… different bits of my life. Overlapping.”

She gestured at the house, the shed, the fence.

“It made everything feel…

She flapped her paw.

“Connected?” Miles suggested.

“Yeah,” she said.

Lillian watched them.

Her husband. Her daughter. The two points around which her own life orbited, sometimes neatly, sometimes chaotically.

She thought of roots. Of wings. Of doors.

“Okay,” she said suddenly.

They both looked at her.

“I’m done pretending we’re not all thinking about the same thing,” she said. “Tomorrow. The letters. We’re doing it.”

Miles swallowed.

“Tomorrow,” he repeated.

“Here,” she said, tapping the table. “At this very glamorous venue. We will each have one (1) beverage and one (1) emotional breakdown. Then you will read your stupid letters out loud and we will deal.”

Sarah’s eyes widened.

“Can I… be there?” she asked quietly.

Miles blinked.

“You… want to?”

She nodded.

“It’s my life too,” she said. “If you go. If you stay. Either way. I don’t…

She shrugged.

“I don’t want it to be something that happened behind a closed door I was sitting against,” she said.

Lillian’s chest hurt.

“Okay,” Miles said, voice rough. “You can be there.”

He looked at them both.

His family.

His garden.

His shed.

His neighbourhood, still echoing faintly with the day’s laughter.

“Tomorrow,” he said again.

The fairy lights buzzed softly above them.

The house, old and patient, listened.

The party was over.

The real conversation, finally, was about to begin.
# Episode 14: The Shed Sessions

The shed had better acoustics than any venue Lillian had played in the last five years, and she refused to forgive it for that.

It wasn’t fair.

Venues were supposed to have sticky floors and questionable lighting and at least one malfunctioning speaker. They were supposed to make you work for every note, to turn your voice into something you had to wrestle into shape.

The shed, by contrast, simply… held her.

"Again," Penny said, from her dubious throne on the stool by the workbench.

Lillian glared.

"My lungs are unionised," she said. "They require breaks. And biscuits."

"You had a biscuit," Penny pointed out. "You ate it resentfully, but you ate it. Again. From the middle section."

Behind them, Miles adjusted the small microphone stand he’d rigged up from a clamp and some spare tubing.

"Levels are good," he said. "If you sing at a normal volume."

"I have never sung at a normal volume," Lillian replied.

"Then they’re good for you," he said.

Sarah sat cross-legged on the floor, sketchbook balanced on her knees, capturing the tangle of cables and limbs. She’d already drawn three versions of the same scene: Lillian mid-phrase, Penny mid-eye-roll, Miles mid-fiddle-with-some-knob.

"This is so weird," Sarah said happily.

"This is a rehearsal," Lillian corrected. "In a structurally overqualified garden outbuilding."

"It’s a pilot episode," Penny said. "For the Shed Sessions. Working title."

"We are not calling it that," Lillian said.

"We are absolutely calling it that," Penny said.

Miles, traitor that he was, said nothing.

---

The idea had begun, like so many things lately, as a joke in the kitchen.

"If we can’t go out," Lillian had said one rainy evening, "we’ll just have to bring the world in. Or at least the neighbours."

"You want to start an underground folk club in our garden?" Miles had asked.

"Not underground," she’d replied. "The shed leaks less."

"The council will have opinions," he’d warned.

"The council always has opinions," she’d said. "They can buy tickets."

Then Penny, of course, had taken it and run.

"You have a ready-made third space," she’d said over tea. "A liminal zone. Not home, not stage. Perfect for experiments."

"It’s a shed," Lillian had protested.

"It’s a metaphor," Penny had replied.

Sarah had looked up from her sketches.

"We could film it," she’d said quietly. "Low-key. Just a camera in the corner. You and guest musicians. Stories. Songs. People need small, honest things right now."

Penny had pointed at her.

"Listen to the child," she’d said. "She has the internet brain."

Miles had sipped his tea.

"We’d need to soundproof that one wall," he’d murmured.

And just like that, a shed became a stage.

---

Now, on a blustery Saturday afternoon, they were beta-testing the idea.

"No livestream yet," Lillian had insisted. "We start with a room of people who already love us enough to pretend it isn’t terrible."

"So Penny," Sarah had said.

"And Ramon," Miles had added. "And Rosalia, if she’s not in mortal combat with an estate agent."

"And maybe Elaine," Sarah had said, almost too quietly to hear.

Lillian had pretended she hadn’t heard. Not because she didn’t care, but because she cared too much and was still working out how to breathe around it.

"Soft launch," Penny had decreed.

"Soft launch implies organisation," Lillian had said. "This is more… controlled chaos."

Even so, Miles had drawn up a list.

• Check cables  
• Test levels  
• Chairs (6)  
• Kettle  
• Extension lead (label!)

Lillian had drawn up her own list.

• Songs that don’t make me cry (too much)  
• Stories that won’t embarrass Sarah (too much)  
• Jokes that won’t make Penny leave (too much)

Between them, they had something like a plan.

---

"Again," Penny said.

Lillian sighed, shook out her paws, and began.

The song was new. Half-formed. It had started in the kitchen weeks ago, fragments of melody threading themselves between the sound of the washing machine and the radio. She’d hummed it under her breath while making tea, while waiting at traffic lights, while watching Miles measure yet another wall.

Now, in the shed, it had words.

*You keep drawing new lines on the same old floor,*  
*Saying we could move the window, we could widen this door…*

Her voice filled the small space, brushing up against the timber, curling around the jars of screws.

*But the light still finds us where it always did,*  
*In the crack by the kettle, in the room where we hid…*

She sang about blueprints and bad decisions, about saying no to big lives and yes to small, stubborn ones. About a shed that was also a sanctuary.

When she finished, the last note hanging in the warm air, nobody spoke for a moment.

"Again," Penny said, but more softly.

"It’ll get stuck in my head," Miles warned.

"That’s the point," Lillian replied.

Sarah’s pencil hadn’t moved for the last verse. She’d simply watched.

"You’re going to do that one," she said. "For the first session."

"We haven’t even booked a guest yet," Lillian said. "It’s just us and the spiders."

"Ramon will come," Penny said. "He’d appear for the opening of a crisps packet if there was applause."

"I want someone who isn’t family-adjacent," Lillian protested. "A real guest."

"You *are* a real guest," Penny said. "In your own life. You just forget sometimes."

---

In the end, the first Shed Session audience consisted of precisely five people and one cat.

Ramon and Rosalia arrived first, bearing a bottle of wine and a potted herb named Clive.

"For the vibe," Ramon said, setting Clive on a shelf. "Every good venue has foliage."

"He’ll die in here," Miles warned.

"Then we will write a heartbreaking ballad about him," Ramon replied.

Elaine came next, clutching a Tupperware of something that smelled like cinnamon and nerves.

"I brought…" She held out the container. "Comfort?"

Sarah took it, eyes bright.

"Snacks are an acceptable emotional language," she said.

Elaine relaxed a fraction.

"This is cute," she said, looking around the shed. "Like a doll’s house and a church had a baby."

"We’re still deciding on the exact ratio of sacrilege," Lillian said.

Mozzarella slunk in last, tail flicking, and immediately claimed the warm patch by the small heater as his domain.

"Right," Lillian said, clapping her paws once. "Ground rules. This is an experiment. If it goes badly, we blame the acoustics. If it goes well, we pretend we knew what we were doing all along."

"That’s my entire career," Penny murmured from the corner.

Miles adjusted the little camera he’d set up high on a shelf, angled so it saw the whole tiny room.

"Rolling," he said. "When you’re ready."

Lillian took a breath.

She looked around.

At Sarah, sitting on a cushion on the floor between Elaine and Rosalia. At Ramon, perched on the stool, already dabbing at non-existent tears with a handkerchief. At Penny, leaning against the workbench, arms folded, eyes sharp and soft at once. At Miles, behind the makeshift sound desk, one paw on a fader and the other steadying the leg of the table.

"Welcome to the Shed," she said. "Population: us."

They chuckled.

"Tonight’s theme," she continued, "is staying. Not in a stuck way. In a… chosen way."

She glanced at Miles.

"Sometimes," she said, "you say no to something big and shiny. And you think that means you’ve chosen small. But small doesn’t mean less. It just… means close."

She strummed a chord.

"This first song is about that," she said. "About blueprints and other lies we tell ourselves about control."

She began to play.

---

Later, when the last chord had faded and the last biscuit had been eaten, when Ramon had declared it a triumph and Rosalia had declared it "not unpromising" (which from her was nearly rapture), they watched the footage back on Miles’s laptop at the kitchen table.

"I look enormous," Lillian complained.

"You look like a woman who fills a room," Penny said.

"The sound is good," Miles said, turning up the volume a notch.

On screen, past-Lillian laughed at one of her own jokes. The room laughed with her. Sarah’s sketching hand moved in the corner of the frame. Elaine’s face, uncertain at first, relaxed by degrees.

"We could post clips," Sarah said. "Not the whole thing. Just… moments."

"The internet will be cruel to my hair," Lillian said.

"The internet is cruel to everything," Penny replied. "But it’s also full of people who need to see other people staying."

"We’re not trying to be influencers," Lillian protested.

"No," Sarah said. "We’re trying to be… visible. To whoever needs it."

Lillian stared at the screen.

At the way the light in the shed warmed the edges of everyone’s fur. At the way Miles glanced up every time she hit a particular note, like a man checking a well-loved machine for a familiar sound.

"Fine," she said. "But if anyone in the comments calls me ‘mum rock,’ I’m shutting it down."

"No one under thirty says ‘mum rock’," Penny said.

"You did yesterday," Sarah pointed out.

"I contain multitudes," Penny replied.

---

In the weeks that followed, the Shed Sessions became a quiet ritual.

Once a fortnight, they cleared the workbench, stacked spare lumber in the corner, and strung a couple of fairy lights Ramon had smuggled in despite Miles’s safety concerns. A handful of people squeezed in—neighbours, friends, the odd brave stranger who’d seen a clip online and sent a tentative email.

They listened to songs and stories. Sometimes Lillian was centre stage; sometimes she ceded it to younger musicians who shook as they played, seeing their own fear reflected and softened in her gaze.

Miles tweaked the setup, episode by episode. Better mic placement. Shorter cables. A second camera angle that caught Sarah’s drawings evolving in real time.

Sarah’s mural took shape in parallel on Ramon and Rosalia’s stairwell: a slow-growing, secret procession of tiny houses, plants, and figures, each one a little echo of someone she loved.

Elaine came back, again and again, always with something baked, always leaving just before the evening tipped into afterparty, as if careful not to overstay in a world that wasn’t quite hers yet.

"You’re building a bridge," Penny observed one night as they picked crumbs out of the rug.

"Between what and what?" Lillian asked.

"Between staying and going," Penny said. "Between here and there. Between who you were on stage and who you are when you forget you’re being watched."

Lillian sat cross-legged on the floor, back against the shed wall.

"Sometimes I miss the roar," she admitted. "The lights. The moment when you step out and there’s just… noise."

"Sometimes I miss the adrenaline too," Penny said. "It’s easier than sitting quietly with yourself."

Lillian tilted her head.

"You’re doing all right at that these days," she said.

"I have tarot cards and chamomile," Penny replied. "Cheating, really."

---

One evening, after everyone had gone and the shed was mostly put back into its usual configuration, Miles lingered.

"Leave the lights on a minute," he said.

Lillian paused, hand on the switch.

"Why?" she asked.

"I want to see it," he said.

She turned back.

The shed glowed, empty of people but full of residue. Laughter, chords, secrets that had landed in the wood and stayed.

"I thought this would be for you," he said. "A way to keep performing without… leaving."

"It is," she said.

"It’s also for me," he added. "Apparently."

She studied him.

"You miss the bigger lab," she said.

"Yes," he admitted. "But this—" He gestured around. "This is a different kind of system. Still moving parts. Still variables. Still things to tune."

"You’re comparing my emotional catharsis to an engine," she said.

"I’m comparing it to a living thing," he replied. "That’s a compliment."

She crossed the space between them in three strides and kissed him, tasting tea and sawdust and something like contentment.

"We’re very lucky," she said against his snout.

"We’re very tired," he corrected.

"That too," she said.

Outside, the garden rustled. Inside, the shed exhaled.

The sessions would not change the world. They would not pay for new roofs or impress Berlin. They might, if they were very lucky, mean that a handful of people walked home on certain nights feeling a little less alone.

For now, that was enough.

"Lights," Miles said softly.

She flicked the switch.

Darkness settled, warm and familiar.

The shed, once again, became what it had always been: a small, stubborn space where things were made and mended, with more care than anyone looking in from the outside might guess.

# Episode 15: The Wall

The coffee shop was too new for Lillian to trust it.

It smelled right—espresso and baked sugar, with a faint top note of hopeful freelancers—but the furniture was suspiciously unscarred. The baristas wore matching aprons. The plants in the window boxes were alive.

"It’s like a set for a show about people with healthy boundaries," she muttered.

Sarah, across the small table, hid a smile behind her mug.

"It’s just a café," she said.

"There’s no such thing," Lillian replied. "There’s home, there’s work, and there’s the place where you go to pretend to be a different version of yourself while you nurse a beverage that costs more than rent used to."

"That’s the definition of a third place," Sarah said. "You’re basically reciting a sociology paper."

"I contain multitudes," Lillian said.

The café in question—"Hearth" in tasteful lettering—had opened on the corner two streets over from their house, in a space that had previously been a newsagent, then a vape shop, then an empty promise. Its windows were large enough that, on clear days, the light poured through in generous slabs.

Today was not clear.

Rain tapped against the glass in a fine, steady curtain. Outside, commuters hurried under umbrellas, heads ducked. Inside, people lingered over laptops and notebooks and conversations that hovered just loud enough to be reassuring.

It felt, Lillian thought begrudgingly, like a good place to practice being brave.

"She’s late," Sarah said, checking her phone.

"Late is genetic," Lillian said. "She’s just establishing brand continuity."

Sarah made a face.

"Don’t call her a brand," she said.

"Sorry," Lillian said. "Too much time with Penny."

She watched her daughter’s paws, wrapped tight around the mug. Sarah had chosen something with oat milk and an earnest description on the chalkboard. Lillian had ordered tea and a slice of lemon drizzle large enough to be a structural element.

"You can still leave," Lillian said.

Sarah exhaled.

"I know," she said. "I don’t want to. I just… want to not want to and also not do it and also already have done it and have it have gone well."

"Ah," Lillian said. "You want the impossible. Excellent taste."

"Do you remember your first gig?" Sarah asked suddenly.

Lillian snorted.

"Which one?" she asked. "The one where I forgot my own lyrics, or the one where the amp exploded, or the one where the promoter paid me in crisps?"

"The first *real* one," Sarah said. "The one that felt… important."

Lillian thought.

"There was a pub in Camden," she said slowly. "Sticky floor, terrible lighting, in-house amp that hummed like a fridge with anxiety. I nearly didn’t go. Sat on the edge of my bed for half an hour thinking if I stayed still long enough the universe would reschedule for me."

"What made you go?" Sarah asked.

"A very rude voice in my head," Lillian said. "It said, ‘Fine. Stay home. Be comfortable. Watch telly. Live a nice, quiet, unremarkable life and then die having never found out if anyone clapped for you.’"

"That voice sounds like you," Sarah observed.

"It *was* me," Lillian said. "Turns out the inside of my head is a harsh but effective coach."

Sarah toyed with her spoon.

"The voice in my head is quieter," she said. "More like… ‘You could go. Or not. Either way you’ll probably mess something up and also you should have washed your hair.’"

"That voice is rude," Lillian said. "We should drown it in cake."

Sarah smiled faintly.

"Do you think she’ll like me?" she asked, so softly Lillian almost missed it under the café murmur.

"Elaine already likes you," Lillian said. "She brings you cinnamon things."

"Not Elaine," Sarah said. "Her. Elaine’s sister. My…"

She couldn’t quite finish the word.

Mother.

Lillian’s heart twisted.

"She’d be a fool not to," Lillian said.

"She might be a fool," Sarah said.

"Then we’ll take you home and add her to the list of people we feel vaguely superior to," Lillian replied. "But you won’t be any less you."

Sarah inhaled, exhaled.

"I told her we could meet somewhere neutral," she said. "Not your kitchen. Not… hers."

"Hence the overpriced third place," Lillian said.

"Hence the overpriced third place," Sarah echoed.

The bell above the door chimed.

Both of them looked up.

---

Elaine came first.

She shrugged off a raincoat, shook droplets from her ears, and spotted them with visible relief.

"Hi," she said, sliding into the booth beside Lillian. "Traffic’s awful. Are you okay?"

The question was aimed at Sarah, but Elaine’s eyes flicked briefly to Lillian, seeking, perhaps, some shared adult conspiracy.

"I’m here," Sarah said.

"That counts as okay," Lillian added.

Elaine smiled.

"She texted," Elaine said. "She’s on her way."

"She?" Sarah repeated, as if the pronoun might wriggle away.

"Clare," Elaine said. "She prefers Clare now."

Sarah nodded.

"Right," she said. "Clare."

Lillian stirred sugar into her tea with unnecessary force.

"How are *you*?" Elaine asked her quietly.

"Decorative," Lillian said. "Here to provide ambience and occasional sarcasm."

"You don’t have to stay," Elaine said.

"I’m leaving as soon as she sits down," Lillian replied. "Scout’s honour."

"You were never a scout," Sarah murmured.

"Technicalities," Lillian said.

The bell chimed again.

This time, it was her.

Clare.

She was smaller than Sarah had imagined, and larger. Younger and older. Familiar and utterly strange.

Her fur was paler, streaked with grey at the temples. Her coat was practical, the sort of thing you bought for walking, not for being seen. Her eyes—Sarah’s eyes, in a different frame—scanned the room once, fast.

When they landed on the table, she stopped.

Elaine lifted a paw.

"Here," she said.

Clare approached slowly, as if crossing a river on stones that might move.

Up close, Sarah could see the tiny details—chewed claws, the faint line of an old scar near one ear, the way her mouth tried and failed to decide whether to smile.

"Hi," Clare said.

"Hi," Sarah echoed.

Lillian stood.

"I’m going to get more cake," she announced to no one in particular.

"You haven’t finished that slice," Elaine pointed out.

"I am emotionally pre-empting," Lillian said.

She squeezed Sarah’s shoulder once. It was quick, but it landed with the weight of a thousand smaller touches.

"Text me if you want me back," she murmured.

"Okay," Sarah said.

Then Lillian moved away, to the counter, to the land of pastries and blessed distance.

The booth felt suddenly smaller.

Clare slid into the seat opposite Sarah. Elaine stayed where she was, forming a lopsided triangle.

"You look…" Clare began, then stopped.

"Like me," Sarah supplied.

Clare huffed a laugh that was half-sob.

"Yes," she said. "Like you."

---
