At the counter, Lillian pretended to debate between carrot cake and brownie while keeping half an eye on the booth.

She couldn’t hear their words over the café murmur, but she could see the shapes.

Clare leaning forward, elbows on the table, both paws wrapped around her coffee cup.

Sarah’s shoulders inching up, then down, then up again.

Elaine’s hands, always moving—offering napkins, gesturing, smoothing nothing.

"Everything okay?" the barista asked, misinterpreting her fixed stare.

"Just wondering if your cinnamon rolls are ethically sourced," Lillian said.

"They’re from a bakery in Hackney," the barista replied.

"Ah," Lillian said. "So ethically ambiguous. I’ll take two."

She carried the plate back like a peace offering and placed it on the table edge.

"Carbohydrates," she said. "In case of emotional emergency. I’m going to sit over there and pretend to read."

"You don’t have to—" Clare began.

"Yes, I do," Lillian said, more gently than it sounded. "This is your third place. I’m just the mascot."

She retreated to a nearby armchair, close enough to be called upon, far enough to be plausibly uninvolved.

She opened a book she’d brought and proceeded not to read a single word.

---

At the booth, words began to find their way into the open.

"I don’t know where to start," Clare said.

"Chronological order is overrated," Sarah replied.

Elaine smiled faintly.

"Tell her about the dog," she suggested.

"The dog?" Sarah echoed.

"We got a dog when you were born," Clare said. "I thought… I thought I could practice. On something I couldn’t break."

Sarah blinked.

"Did it help?" she asked.

"No," Clare said. "He ate the sofa and then he bit the health visitor and then I sat on the kitchen floor and cried so hard I scared myself."

"We rehomed him with a very patient couple in Kent," Elaine added.

"The dog, not the health visitor," Clare clarified.

Sarah laughed, unexpectedly.

"What was his name?" she asked.

"Biscuit," Clare said.

"That’s a terrible name for a dog," Sarah said.

"I was twenty," Clare replied. "Everything I ate was carbs and fear."

They smiled at each other, tentative.

"I don’t remember him," Sarah said.

"You were very small," Elaine said. "And then…"

She trailed off.

"And then I wasn’t there," Clare finished.

There it was.

The absence, spoken.

Sarah wrapped her paws around her mug, anchoring herself in the heat.

"Why?" she asked.

Clare closed her eyes briefly.

"Because I was young and scared and sick," she said. "And because no one had taught me how to do any of it. And because I made a choice that felt like saving you and also felt like cutting off my own arm."

She opened her eyes.

"None of those are excuses," she added quickly. "Just… context."

"Elaine said you were… not well," Sarah said.

"Depression," Clare replied. "And other things. They don’t put ‘chronic self-loathing’ in your notes, but it’s there."

"They should," Elaine muttered.

"By the time I realised I wanted you back," Clare continued, "you had a *life*. People who loved you. People who didn’t make that love conditional on you keeping them alive. And I…"

She swallowed.

"I couldn’t bear the idea of barging in and demanding a title I hadn’t earned."

"So you just… stayed away," Sarah said.

"So I just stayed away," Clare echoed.

"That hurt too," Sarah said.

Clare nodded.

"I know," she said. "I imagined it. I lived entire parallel lives in my head where I turned up at your school play or your graduation or your first exhibition and you looked at me like I was something that had crawled in under the door."

"I probably would have," Sarah admitted.

"Exactly," Clare said.

They sat with that for a moment.

"So why now?" Sarah asked.

Clare glanced at Elaine.

"Because Elaine yelled at me," she said.

"I did not *yell*," Elaine protested.

"She raised her voice in a firm and loving way," Clare amended. "She said, ‘You’re not protecting her, you’re protecting yourself from finding out whether she’s stronger than you were.’"

Elaine looked down at her hands.

"I may have been… blunt," she said.

"You were right," Clare said.

She turned back to Sarah.

"And then your email came," she said. "And I thought, ‘She is literally asking. The least I can do is show up and let her decide what I am to her. Not the other way around.’"

Sarah stared at the condensation on her glass.

"I don’t know what you are to me," she said. "Yet."

"You don’t have to know," Clare said quickly. "You don’t have to call me anything. You don’t owe me—"

"Stop," Elaine said gently. "Let her talk."

Sarah took a breath.

"I don’t remember you," she said. "Not really. I remember smells. Sounds. A fight in a kitchen. Slammed doors. Social workers’ shoes. Then… Lillian. Miles. Mozzarella eventually." She smiled faintly. "They’re… my parents. That’s not negotiable."

"I know," Clare said, voice thin.

"But also," Sarah continued, surprising herself, "I have your nose. And your stupid anxiety about whether my hair looks clean. And I have this… curiosity. This empty room in my head where a story should be."

She looked up.

"I don’t know if I want you to live in that room," she said. "But I’m… willing to open the door and see if you fit."

Clare’s eyes filled.

"That’s more than I deserve," she said.

"We are not doing the ‘deserve’ dance," Elaine cut in. "We are doing the ‘are you both willing to try being specific humans to each other’ dance."

"You sound like Penny," Sarah said.

"God forbid," Elaine replied.

They all laughed, shakily.

---

Across the room, Lillian watched Sarah laugh with the woman who had once been a headline in a case file.

Her own tea had gone cold.

She thought of all the nights she had lain awake, furious at a faceless stranger for not being there. For abandoning a tiny, brittle girl to the mercy of systems and statistics.

She thought of all the mornings she had stood at the foot of the stairs and listened for the sound of Sarah’s footsteps, still half-afraid they might simply… stop.

She thought of the first time Sarah had called her "Mum" and the way it had landed like a gift she was always terrified someone might try to take back.

Now, here, in this aggressively neutral space with its reclaimed wood and ethically ambiguous cinnamon rolls, she watched that someone sit across from her daughter and look, not like a villain, but like a person who had once been very young and very tired and very unprepared.

She didn’t forgive her.

Not yet. Maybe not ever, in the simple, storybook way.

But something in her chest loosened.

"You okay?" a voice asked.

She looked up. The barista.

"Fine," she said. "Just realising the universe occasionally does character development."

"We have a loyalty card scheme for that," he said.

"Of course you do," she replied.

---

When it was over—or rather, when this first, strange chapter had reached whatever counted as an ending—they stepped out into the rain together.

Elaine hugged Sarah first.

"Proud of you," she whispered.

"For what?" Sarah asked.

"For being here," Elaine said. "For setting terms. For not pretending it’s less complicated than it is."

Then Clare stood in front of her.

"Thank you for the coffee," Sarah said, because when in doubt, manners.

"Thank you for… existing," Clare said, and then winced. "Sorry, that sounded weird."

"It is weird," Sarah said. "All of it. That’s okay."

They looked at each other for a long moment.

"Can I—" Clare began. "May I hug you?"

Sarah’s throat went tight.

She thought of being held by Lillian, of the weight and warmth and smell of bergamot and guitar polish.

She thought of being held by no one, in different beds, in different houses, hands-off foster homes where touch meant obligation.

She nodded, once.

The hug was brief and careful, more a promise than a claim.

"We’ll see each other again," Clare said, pulling back. It was a question.

"We’ll… text," Sarah said. "And call. And maybe…" She glanced at Lillian, who pretended to be very interested in a bus timetable. "We’ll see."

It was more than a no. Less than a yes. Exactly honest.

Clare nodded.

"Okay," she said. "I’ll… follow your lead."

She and Elaine walked away together, two silhouettes under one umbrella.

Lillian and Sarah stood under the café awning, rain misting around them.

"Well," Lillian said at last. "That was… a lot."

"Yeah," Sarah said.

"Do you hate me for staying?" Lillian asked suddenly.

Sarah turned, startled.

"What?" she said.

"I could have left," Lillian said. "Given you privacy. Let you have it without me lurking in the background like an anxious potted plant."

"I wanted you there," Sarah said simply. "She’s… a new room. You’re… the house."

Lillian’s eyes stung.

"The house needs new wallpaper," she said thickly.

"We can pick some," Sarah replied.

They started walking home, side by side, their reflections blurring in the wet pavement.

Between work and home, between past and present, between leaving and staying, the coffee shop glowed behind them—a third place where something fragile had been cautiously, tentatively begun.

# Episode 16: Letters from Berlin

The email arrived at 06:17 on a Wednesday.

Miles knew this not because he compulsively checked his phone in the night—he did, but he was trying to do it less—but because he’d been awake anyway, watching the faint light creep around the edges of the curtains.

When the notification pinged, he didn’t move.

Beside him, Lillian snored softly, one paw flung over her head like a tragic heroine.

Downstairs, the house was still.

He told himself he could ignore it until morning.

He lasted eight minutes.

Sliding carefully out of bed, he padded into the hallway and down the stairs, phone in paw, like a teenager sneaking a forbidden text.

In the kitchen’s blue-grey half-light, he opened his inbox.

*Subject: Re: Offer – Decision Timeline*

His stomach tightened.

He tapped.

> Dear Miles,
> 
> 
> I hope this finds you and your family well.
> 
> 
> As we are finalising staffing for next year, I wanted to check in regarding your decision. We completely understand that this is a big choice and that you need time. That said, we do need a firm answer by the end of the month in order to proceed.
> 
> 
> Please let me know if there is anything you’d like to discuss.
> 
> 
> Best,
> 
> Dr. Keller

End of the month.

Three weeks.

The words sat on the screen, polite and reasonable and entirely out of proportion to his heart rate.

He closed the email.

He opened the notes app instead.

Two titles stared back at him:

*Letter – Yes*

*Letter – No*

Rosalia’s homework.

He had created the files two days ago, in a fit of compliance, then filled each with a single line:

> Dear Dr. Keller,

And stopped.

Now, in the quiet kitchen, with the deadline humming at the edge of his awareness, he tapped *Letter – Yes*.

The cursor blinked, patient.

He started to type.

> Thank you again for the opportunity.

That was safe. Neutral.

He hesitated.

> After a lot of thought, I’ve decided to accept your offer.

The words looked alien in his handwriting.

He kept going.

> I’m excited about the work and the chance to return to Berlin.

He paused, feeling for truth.

He *was* excited about the work, in a vacuum. The research, the team, the chance to use parts of his brain that currently came out mostly for shed reinforcement and complex lasagne logistics.

> My family is of course a big consideration, but we’ve discussed it and—

He stopped.

Had they?

They had cried in candlelight. They had prodded him around tapas. They had circled the decision like cats around a new piece of furniture.

He could not, in good conscience, write *we’ve decided this together*.

He deleted the sentence.

The cursor blinked accusingly.

He backed out and opened *Letter – No* instead.

> Thank you again for the opportunity.

Some sentences were universal.

> After a lot of thought, I’ve decided to decline your offer.

His chest loosened, fractionally, just reading it.

> This has not been an easy decision. The role and the team are exactly the kind of work I once imagined for myself.

He stopped.

Once.

Past tense, honest.

> However, my life has taken a different shape than I expected, and right now my priorities are here.

Here.

He looked around the kitchen as he typed it.

At the chipped tiles, the basil, the mug someone had left on the counter last night.

> I hope you understand, and I’m grateful for your confidence in me.

He read the paragraph back.

His shoulders dropped another millimetre.

He could say this.

He could *mean* this.

He just wasn’t sure he could live with it when the glow of bravely-declined opportunity wore off and the boiler broke again.

“You’re thinking very loudly,” a voice said from the doorway.

He jumped.

Lillian leaned on the frame, dressing gown askew, eyes still half-closed.

“Sorry,” he said automatically.

“Don’t apologise,” she yawned. “Just share.”

She padded in, glanced at his phone.

“Homework?” she asked.

He nodded.

“Letter version,” he said.

“Solo performance?”

He hesitated.

“Draft,” he said. “Dress rehearsal later.”

She made a face.

“You’re not allowed to cancel opening night,” she said. “We’ve printed programmes.”

He smiled, then sobered.

“Keller emailed,” he said quietly. “They need an answer by the end of the month.”

Lillian’s eyebrows rose.

“Time has entered the chat,” she said.

He sighed.

“Apparently,” he said.

She came to sit opposite him at the table.

“Read me one,” she said.

“They’re not finished,” he said.

“That’s never stopped you making me listen to half-written songs,” she replied.

He huffed.

She waited.

He opened *Letter – Yes* again.

He cleared his throat.

“Dear Dr. Keller,” he began. “Thank you again for the opportunity. After a lot of thought, I’ve decided to accept your offer. I’m excited about the work and the chance to return to Berlin.”

His own voice sounded wrong in his ears.

“Stop,” Lillian said.

He did.

“How do you feel when you say that?” she asked.

“Like I’m reading lines,” he said.

“Bad lines?”

“Corporate lines,” he said. “True in isolation. Not… in context.”

She nodded.

“Again,” she said. “The other one.”

He swallowed and opened *Letter – No*.

“Dear Dr. Keller,” he read. “Thank you again for the opportunity. After a lot of thought, I’ve decided to decline your offer. This has not been an easy decision. The role and the team are exactly the kind of work I once imagined for myself. However, my life has taken a different shape than I expected, and right now my priorities are here.”

His throat tightened on the last word.

He forced himself to finish.

“I hope you understand, and I’m grateful for your confidence in me.”

Silence.

Lillian’s eyes were bright.

“Well,” she said. “That one has better dialogue.”

He laughed, helplessly.

“You’re biased,” he said.

“Obviously,” she said. “I am invested in your continued access to our kettle.”

He looked down at the screen.

“But?” he prompted.

She softened.

“But also,” she said, “that sounded like you. The other one sounded like your CV wrote it.”

He breathed out slowly.

“So that’s it,” he said. “I should just… send this one.”

Lillian tilted her head.

“Do you want to?” she asked.

He thought.

Really thought.

Of the teenaged version of himself who had looked at glossy photos of Berlin labs and thought, *Yes, that. Out of here.*

Of the young man who had gone for a semester, loved the trains and the order and the way the city hummed, then come home for a funeral and found reasons not to go back.

Of the man he was now, who knew the sound of his daughter’s key in the door and his wife’s laugh in the kitchen and the exact spot on the garden wall that needed repointing.

“Yes,” he said slowly. “I think… I want to want to. And I… do. Mostly.”

“Mostly is enough,” she said.

“Is it?”

“Nothing is ever a hundred percent,” she said. “Except my hatred of the council. And my love of you. Everything else is a messy graph.”

He smiled.

“You make a compelling case,” he said.

“It’s the eyeliner,” she said.

“You’re not wearing eyeliner,” he pointed out.

“Spiritually, I am,” she said.

---

They didn’t send anything that morning.

The day had other demands: bins, emails, Sarah’s lesson plans, a leaking tap that chose that exact moment to assert itself.

But the letters existed now, in more than just his head.

That night, after dinner, they sat down again at the table.

Sarah joined them this time, as promised.

She curled up with her knees to her chest, a mug between her paws, watching her parents like they were a particularly tense box set.

“Previously, on Miles’s Existential Crisis,” she murmured.

“Hush,” Lillian said. “This is prestige television.”

Miles took a breath.

“I wrote them,” he said.

“Plural?” Sarah asked.

He nodded.

“We heard the ‘yes’ this morning,” Lillian said. “It got a bad review.”

“Two stars,” she added. “Would not renew.”

Sarah smiled.

“Can I… hear the ‘no’?” she asked.

He hesitated.

Then he handed her the phone.

She read in silence, lips moving slightly.

When she finished, she put the phone down carefully.

“That one feels… right,” she said.

“You’re both very quick to cancel my German spin-off,” he said.

“You can still go to Berlin on holiday,” Lillian said. “We’ll even let you bore us with photos of infrastructure.”

He huffed.

Sarah leaned forward.

“Dad,” she said. “If you said yes, I’d… deal. We’d… adjust. I’d make new art about trains. But I’d always have this little…

She tapped her chest.

“…thing,” she said. “This little ‘oh’ that went, ‘He chose there over here.’ Even if that wasn’t fair. Even if it wasn’t true. It would just… be there.”

He swallowed.

“And if I say no?” he asked.

She thought.

“Then sometimes,” she said, “when you’re grumpy about work or the boiler or the council, I’ll have a little ‘oh’ that goes, ‘He could have left. He didn’t.’ And I’ll be… grateful. And maybe a bit annoyed on your behalf. But I won’t feel like you…

She searched.

“Abandoned us,” she finished.

The word landed between them with a dull thud.

Lillian winced.

“Baby,” she said.

“I know it’s not that simple,” Sarah said quickly. “That’s just… how my brain labels things. It’s very binary. I’m working on it.”

Miles reached for her paw.

“Thank you for saying it out loud,” he said.

She squeezed his fingers.

“Thanks for letting me,” she replied.

---

After they’d gone to bed—Lillian on purpose, Sarah eventually—Miles found himself back in the kitchen.

The house glowed softly around him.

He opened the drawer.

The printed letter sat on top of takeaway menus and a rogue screwdriver.

He unfolded it.

Read it one more time.

He thought of Dr Keller, of the other consultants, of the version of himself who might have fitted neatly into that life.

He thought of Ramon’s knock through the wall, of Rosalia’s lasagne, of Penny’s infuriating wisdom.

He thought of Sarah, standing on a platform with a mug from Brighton in one paw and a sketchbook in the other, choosing to plant herself here.

He thought of Lillian, humming in the kitchen, eyes alight when a line landed.

He pulled out his phone.

He opened *Letter – No*.

His thumb hovered over the share icon.

For a second, fear reared up: fear of later regret, of imagined colleagues shaking their heads at his "smallness."

Then another feeling, quieter but steadier, stepped in front of it.

Care.

For them.

For himself.

For the life he had built plank by plank without quite realising it was a structure, not just scaffolding.

He forwarded the text into a new email.

Filled in Dr Keller’s address.

Added a subject line:

*Re: Offer – Decision*

He read it through, once, aloud.

His voice didn’t crack.

He hit send.

The whoosh was absurdly quiet.

The sky did not fall.

The boiler did not immediately explode in protest.

The house hummed as it always had.

He put the printed letter back in the drawer.

Not as an altar this time.

As a souvenir.

When he turned around, Lillian stood in the doorway.

Her eyes were wide, her dressing gown ridiculous.

“Did you—” she began.

“Yes,” he said.

She crossed the room in three strides and threw her arms around him.

“Idiot,” she whispered into his fur.

“Probably,” he said.

“Brave idiot,” she amended.

He laughed, breathless.

“Now what?” he asked, hand still shaking slightly.

She pulled back enough to look at him.

“Now,” she said, “we fix the wall.”

He blinked.

“The garden wall,” she clarified. “It’s been bothering you for weeks. You keep glaring at it like it insulted your mother. You’ve committed to this house now. You might as well make sure it doesn’t fall over.”

Relief, absurd and practical, washed through him.

“Okay,” he said. “Tomorrow.”

“Tomorrow,” she agreed.

They turned off the lights and went upstairs.

Outside, the email sped across cables and under seas, carrying his no to Berlin.

Inside, the house settled a little more firmly on its foundations.

# Episode 17: Ramon's Open House

The estate agent had brought a measuring tape and a smile that didn’t reach her eyes.

“Light-filled,” she said, gesturing at Ramon and Rosalia’s living room as if they’d never seen it before. “Characterful. Close to amenities.”

“She means the kebab shop,” Lillian whispered to Miles from their perch on the sofa.

Miles’s ears twitched.

“And the bus stop,” he murmured back.

“The bus stop is a character,” Lillian said.

Across the room, Rosalia was doing her best to look like someone whose house was not currently being judged.

Her usual warmth was dialled down, nervous energy buzzing beneath the surface.

Ramon, on the other hand, was at twelve.

“This,” he announced to the young couple being shepherded through the tour, “is the wall. It is structurally sound, emotionally resonant, and has hosted more arguments about damp than some entire marriages.”

He patted the plaster fondly.

The couple—a fox and a rabbit, both in their late twenties and radiating the particular panic of first-time buyers—offered tight smiles.

“And through there,” the agent said smoothly, opening the door to the kitchen, “we have a recently refurbished—”

“By me,” Ramon interjected.

“—kitchen,” she finished. “Very sociable space.”

“We have fed entire nations in there,” Rosalia added. “Well. One street.”

Lillian swallowed around the lump in her throat.

“This is horrible,” she muttered.

“They asked us to be here,” Miles reminded her. “Moral support.”

“Moral support does not usually involve watching strangers open my friend’s cupboards,” she said. “I feel like I should apologise to the plates.”

He squeezed her paw.

“At least this way we know who might end up next door,” he said.

“True,” she said darkly. “We can veto anyone who doesn’t laugh at Ramon’s jokes.”

---

The “Open House”—Ramon’s term, enthusiastically adopted—was both a practical necessity and, inevitably, an excuse for a gathering.

“If strangers are going to traipse through my home, I at least want people there who know which side of the sofa is mine,” Rosalia had said.

So the Bark family had been invited as emotional ballast.

Penny had invited herself and was currently perched in the corner taking notes “for a piece on transitions.”

Bea had brought a plant “as a housewarming gift for whoever lives here next, including if that’s still you.”

Sarah hovered between the kitchen and the garden, somewhere between documenting and distracting.

The agent ushered the couple upstairs.

“Bedrooms up here,” she called.

“And ghosts of dinner parties past,” Ramon added.

Rosalia shot him a look.

“Stop scaring them,” she hissed.

“If they are frightened by ghosts, they do not deserve this house,” he replied.

Lillian stood and stretched.

“I need air,” she announced. “And by ‘air’ I mean ‘to go and check our own house is still standing.’”

“Coward,” Rosalia said affectionately.

“Absolutely,” Lillian agreed.

Miles followed her out onto the landing.

“Are you okay?” he asked quietly.

She looked at the familiar hallway.

Same scuffed skirting boards. Same slightly crooked picture frame Sarah had drawn years ago.

“I hate change,” she said. “Unless I cause it. Then it’s art.”

He smiled.

“You’re allowed to hate this,” he said.

“I know,” she said. “I also know… they’re excited. In their weird, self-sabotaging way. It’s like watching someone you love get on a train backwards.”

He considered that.

“You know they can’t go if they don’t get an offer,” he said.

She brightened, momentarily.

“We could sabotage the viewing,” she whispered.

“Lill,” he warned.

“I’m just saying,” she went on. “We could release Mozzarella into the cupboard where you keep the weird spices. One sniff of your asafoetida and they’ll run screaming.”

He laughed.

“We promised we’d be good,” he reminded her.

“I promised to show up,” she said. “I did not promise to behave.”

---

Downstairs again, the second wave of viewers arrived.

This time, a single woman with a clipboard and an air of decisive competence.

“She looks like she files things,” Lillian whispered. “I don’t trust people who have their documents in order.”

“That’s very rich, coming from you,” Penny said without looking up from her notes.

The woman introduced herself, shook hands, and proceeded to inspect the property with brisk thoroughness.

“Solid,” she said, rapping on a piece of skirting.

“I installed that,” Ramon said proudly.

“Mm,” she said.

“‘Mm’ is not enough,” he muttered as she moved on.

Sarah trailed her at a polite distance, sketchbook in hand.

Later, there would be quick studies: the way the woman’s eyes darted to the storage space, the way her shoulders relaxed in the kitchen.

At one point, the woman paused in front of the wall between their house and the Barks’.

She tilted her head.

“Is that knocking… normal?” she asked.

On cue, three quick raps sounded through the plaster.

Ramon beamed.

“That is the sound of community,” he said.

The woman blinked.

“Do you… mind the noise?” she asked.

“We love the noise,” Rosalia said firmly.

“It’s like living next to a sitcom,” Sarah added.

“With better food,” Penny put in.

The woman’s mouth twitched.

“I work from home,” she said. “I like… knowing there are people around. But I also like…

She gestured at the wall.

“Boundaries,” she finished.

“We have those,” Ramon said. “Mostly.”

---

Between viewings, the house exhaled.

In the brief lulls, it almost felt normal.

Rosalia fussed over cushions. Ramon rearranged herbs on the windowsill. Lillian topped up everyone’s tea whether they wanted it or not.

At one point, Miles disappeared into their garden and came back with a tape measure.

“What are you doing?” Lillian asked.

“Checking the wall,” he said.

“Our wall?”

“The wall,” he corrected. “If someone new moves in, they’re going to lean shelves against this. I want to make sure it’s ready.”

She watched him measure, murmur, nod.

“You’re ridiculous,” she said softly.

“I’m practical,” he said.

“Same thing,” she replied.

---

By late afternoon, the Open House was over.

The estate agent had left with promises of follow-up calls. The viewers had drifted away to compare notes and mortgage calculators.

The remaining core—Ramon, Rosalia, the Barks, Penny, Bea—collapsed around the table.

Rosalia dropped her head into her hands.

“I hate this,” she said.

“You’re the one who wanted to move,” Ramon reminded her, patting her back.

“I want the *result*,” she said. “I do not enjoy the process where strangers look at my spice rack and decide if it is worth half a million pounds.”

“It’s worth at least three-quarters,” Lillian said.

“Hush,” Rosalia said, but she smiled.

“Do you think they liked it?” Bea asked.

“They’d be fools not to,” Penny said. “But fools buy houses every day. Have you seen Location, Location, Location?”

Rosalia groaned.

Ramon looked at Miles.

“What do you think?” he asked.

Miles considered.

“I think,” he said slowly, “that anyone who moves in here is going to inherit more than bricks. And I hope they understand that.”

Ramon nodded.

“We will vet them,” he said.

“You cannot vet buyers,” Rosalia said.

“Watch me,” he replied.

Lillian cleared her throat.

“For what it’s worth,” she said, “if Competent Clipboard Lady moves in, I will allow it. She seemed like she might file complaints *and* attend the garden thing. Balanced.”

Sarah snorted.

“I liked the fox and rabbit,” she said. “They held hands when they looked at the kitchen. That’s a good sign.”

“They also asked about council tax before storage,” Penny noted. “Deeply suspicious priorities.”

“Look at us,” Rosalia said. “Arguing about hypothetical neighbours while the actual ones are right here.”

She reached across and squeezed Lillian’s paw.

“Whatever happens,” she said, “you are stuck with us. Even with a train in between.”

Lillian’s eyes stung.

“Good,” she said.

---

That night, after everyone had gone home and the Open House had reverted to just “house,” Ramon and Rosalia stood in the doorway, looking at their living room.

The cushions were a little too artfully arranged. The surfaces a little too bare. The smell of estate agent’s air freshener lingered under the usual garlic and coffee.

“Do you ever regret it?” Rosalia asked quietly.

“Regret what?” Ramon said.

“Moving here,” she said. “Meeting them. Building… this. Knowing that now we have to… untangle it.”

Ramon thought of the first time he’d heard Lillian singing through the wall. The first emergency knock at midnight. The first lasagne eaten around that overburdened table.

“Never,” he said.

She nodded.

“Me neither,” she said.

“We’re not untangling,” he added. “We’re… stretching.”

She huffed a laugh.

“Listen to you,” she said. “Mr. Flexible Boundaries.”

He bowed.

Outside, in their own kitchen, Miles and Lillian stood at their side of the wall.

“Do you think they’ll get it?” Lillian asked.

“Yes,” Miles said.

“Do you think we’ll survive it?”

He rested his paw flat against the plaster.

“We’ve survived worse,” he said.

On the other side, as if on cue, Ramon knocked three times.

Miles knocked back.

The wall, for now, held.

# Episode 18: Reconstruction

The morning after Miles sent the email, the world did not end.

It rained a little.

The bin lorry came late.

Mozzarella threw up some unidentifiable grass on the rug.

Life, rudely, continued.

“I feel like there should have been a thunderclap,” Lillian said, standing at the back door with her mug. “Or at least a dramatic shaft of light.”

“We live in Islington, not a prestige drama,” Sarah said, tying her hair back. “The most we get is a fox knocking over the recycling.”

As if summoned, a fox trotted along the top of the fence, glanced at them with mild disdain, and kept going.

“See?” she said.

Miles stepped out into the garden, hands in his pockets.

The shed—The Den—waited at the far end.

It was almost finished.

The structure was sound. The roof didn’t leak. The walls were painted that soft, kitchen-echoing green.

Inside, there was still work to do.

Shelves to mount. Cables to wrangle. Acoustic panels to fudge out of old blankets.

“Today,” he said, more to himself than to them.

“Today what?” Lillian asked.

“We finish it,” he said.

“Very assertive,” she said. “I like it.”

Sarah glanced at him.

“You sure you don’t want to, I don’t know, stare at your sent items for six more hours?” she asked.

He smiled.

“Tempting,” he said. “But no. If I’m going to choose this life, I might as well… build it properly.”

Lillian’s heart clenched.

“Right,” she said. “Tools. I’ll fetch snacks. Sarah, fetch… artistic opinions.”

“On it,” Sarah said.

---

The Den, mid-reconstruction, looked like the inside of Miles’s brain.

Neat piles of planks. Boxes of neatly labelled screws. A whiteboard with measurements that meant something to him and very little to anyone else.

“It’s very… linear,” Sarah said, surveying it.

“Thank you,” he said.

“That wasn’t entirely a compliment,” she replied.

He chuckled.

“We can make it messier,” he said. “That’s where you come in.”

They started with the shelves.

“We need somewhere for your sketchbooks,” he said, holding a bracket in place.

“And your terrifying jars of screws,” she said.

“That too,” he admitted.

As they worked, she watched him.

He moved with a kind of calm precision that soothed her nerves.

“Do you regret it?” she asked suddenly.

He didn’t ask what.

“Not yet,” he said.

“You might?”

“Probably,” he said. “On bad days. When the boiler leaks or the council sends another letter or someone at a party says, ‘Oh, you could have been in Berlin.’ I’ll have little flickers of… ‘what if.’”

She nodded.

“Do you… resent me?” she asked.

He almost dropped the bracket.

“What?”

“For being part of the reason,” she said. “For being… here. For making ‘priorities’ plural.”

He set the drill down and turned to face her fully.

“Sarah,” he said. “You are not a reason I *didn’t* go. You are one of the reasons I stayed.”

She squinted.

“That’s some semantic gymnastics,” she said.

“It’s the difference between a chain and a root,” he said.

She softened.

“Penny’s been getting to you,” she said.

He smiled.

“Maybe,” he said.

---

By midday, the shelves were up.

Lillian arrived with a tray of sandwiches and a clipboard.

“Bread and bureaucracy,” she announced. “Pick your poison.”

“What’s the clipboard for?” Miles asked warily.

“Acoustics,” she said.

He blinked.

“You care about acoustics now?”

“I have always cared about how things sound,” she said. “I just never had a room that listened.”

He handed her the tape measure.

“Show me your vision, O Producer,” he said.

She beamed.

“I knew you’d crack eventually,” she said.

Together, they plotted out where the microphone would go, where the small fold-out sofa could live, how many people could feasibly cram in for a “Shed Session” without violating health and safety.

“Four,” Miles said.

“Ten,” Lillian countered.

They compromised on six.

Sarah sketched as they moved things around.

Later, the drawing would show the three of them from above: Miles a neat rectangle of movement, Lillian a series of looping lines, Sarah a small, steady circle.

In the margins, she wrote: *Reconstruction = not just wood.*

---

In the afternoon, Penny arrived uninvited, as usual.

She stood in the doorway of The Den, hands on hips.

“Ah,” she said. “The micro-sanctuary takes shape.”

“If you call it that, we have to charge entry,” Lillian said.

“You should charge entry anyway,” Penny replied. “People get weird about free things.”

She stepped inside, looking around.

“How do you feel in here now?” she asked Miles.

He considered.

“Less… temporary,” he said.

“Good,” she said. “You committed. The nervous system will catch up.”

“Are you talking about his or the house’s?” Lillian asked.

“Both,” Penny said.

---

They hung the fairy lights last.

It felt ceremonial, somehow.

“Up a bit,” Sarah said, standing back.

“Down a bit,” Lillian countered.

Miles adjusted, the three of them negotiating brightness like other families negotiated thermostats.

When they finally stepped back, the effect was…

Nice.

Not magical. Not transformative. Just… warm.

“It looks smaller,” Sarah said.

“Good,” Miles said.

She glanced at him.

“You’re into small now?”

He smiled.

“Turns out,” he said, “I am.”

---

As evening fell, they carried in the last few things that would make The Den itself: Lillian’s guitar stand, Sarah’s crate of sketchbooks, a small lamp that had once lived in the living room and had now been demoted/promoted.

Miles placed his toolbox in the corner.

“You’re keeping tools in here?” Lillian asked.

“It’s still technically a shed,” he said.

She made a face.

“It’s a studio,” she said.

“It’s both,” he replied.

Sarah grinned.

“And/and,” she said.

They stood in the centre, the three of them.

“We should christen it,” Lillian said.

“With what?” Miles asked.

“Song?” Sarah suggested.

“Tea,” Penny said from the doorway, brandishing a flask.

They settled on tea.

In mismatched mugs, of course.

“To reconstruction,” Lillian said, raising hers.

“To foundations,” Miles added.

“To finally having somewhere to put all my clay that isn’t the bath,” Sarah said.

They clinked.

The tea tasted like ordinary tea.

The moment tasted like something else.

---

Later, after Sarah had gone inside to text Bea photos and Penny had drifted off to write about “spaces that remember us,” Lillian and Miles sat alone on the little sofa.

Outside, the garden was a dark suggestion.

Inside, The Den glowed.

“How does it feel?” she asked.

He looked around.

“Like a decision I can see,” he said.

She nodded.

“Do you miss it?” she asked.

“Berlin?”

“Yeah.”

He thought.

“I miss… the idea of it,” he said. “The version of me who lived there. Slept badly. Ate more takeaway. Had very impressive lanyards.”

She snorted.

“You and your lanyards,” she said.

“This version,” he went on, gesturing around, “gets to… build things you can’t quantify on a CV.”

“Like emotional resilience and adequate shelving,” she said.

“Exactly,” he said.

She leaned her head on his shoulder.

“I’m proud of you,” she said.

He swallowed.

“For what?”

“For changing your mind,” she said. “Not from yes to no. From ‘success looks like that’ to ‘success looks like this.’ That’s hard.”

He let the words settle.

“You helped,” he said.

“Obviously,” she said. “I’m very wise.”

He laughed.

They sat in comfortable quiet for a while, listening to the faint hum of the house through the wall.

“You know what the best part is?” Lillian said eventually.

“What?”

“If everything goes wrong,” she said. “If the shed floods and the dog eats my notebook and Sarah moves to Mars and Ramon and Rosalia forget to invite us to Christmas—”

“Unlikely,” he said.

“If,” she repeated, “we can still leave. One day. If we want. Somewhere new. We just… won’t be running. We’ll be walking. Together.”

He smiled.

“The long way,” he said.

“The only way,” she replied.

He reached for her paw.

In the soft light of The Den, surrounded by wood and wires and the ghosts of songs not yet written, they sat.

Rebuilt.

Not perfect.

But theirs.

# Episode 19: The Ground Beneath

The email sat in his sent folder like a stepped-off platform.

For days after Miles sent it, he resisted opening it.

He knew what it said. He’d written it, after all. He didn’t need to reread his own refusal.

What he needed, apparently, was to stare at the garden.

He did a lot of that.

From the kitchen window. From the back step. From inside The Den, which now bore the satisfying clutter of a space in use.

“You’re going to wear a hole in the grass with your eyes,” Lillian said one morning, leaning beside him at the sink.

“The drainage could be better,” he muttered.

She grinned.

“There he is,” she said. “My deeply unsexy soil nerd.”

“Soil is very sexy,” he said automatically.

“You need to get out more,” she replied.

He shook his head.

“I need to get *in* more,” he said. “Into this.”

He nodded at the garden.

She watched him.

“You still waiting for an explosion?” she asked.

He considered.

“Part of me is waiting for Keller to email back and say, ‘Oh, we didn’t mean it, come anyway,’” he admitted.

“And if she does?”

He shrugged.

“Then I’ll say no again,” he said.

She smiled.

“Good,” she said. “Practice is important.”

---

On a Thursday, a parcel arrived from Berlin.

Just a slim, stiff envelope with his name on it and a return address he knew by heart.

Lillian found it on the mat.

“Post!” she called.

He took one look at the logo and felt his stomach swoop.

“I can bin it,” she offered.

He shook his head.

“No,” he said. “I should… see.”

He opened it at the table, carefully.

Inside was a printed letter and a small, glossy brochure for the hospital’s new wing.

> Dear Miles,
> 
> 
> Thank you for your thoughtful response. While we’re disappointed not to have you join us, we understand and respect your decision.
> 
> 
> Should circumstances change in future, please don’t hesitate to get in touch.
> 
> 
> Wishing you and your family all the best,
> 
> 
> Sincerely,
> 
> 
> Dr. Keller

That was it.

No guilt. No editorial.

Just a polite full stop.

He exhaled.

The brochure showed bright corridors, shiny equipment, a rooftop garden he had once stood in, flattening his fur against the wind.

He closed it.

“Well?” Lillian asked.

“They… accepted it,” he said.

“Of course they did,” she said. “They’re German. They love clear answers.”

He smiled.

Then, without overthinking it, he walked to the bin.

He dropped the brochure in.

He hesitated with the letter.

Then he folded it and slid it into the drawer with the first one.

Past and present, stacked.

“Souvenir?” Lillian asked.

“Receipt,” he said.

---

In the afternoon, he went to the garden centre.

It felt, in an odd way, like visiting a church.

Rows of plants. Smells of damp compost and fertiliser. Quiet conversations drifting between aisles.

He walked past the annuals, the perennials, the herbs.

He paused at the roses.

They were ridiculous plants, really. Fussy. Prickly. Overused as metaphors.

He picked out three.

One deep red. One soft pink. One white with a blush of yellow at its centre.

At the till, the clerk—a mole with soil permanently embedded under his nails—rang them up without comment.

“You’ll want good drainage,” he said as Miles fumbled for his card.

“I know,” Miles said.

“And patience,” the mole added.

Miles smiled faintly.

“That too,” he said.

---

Back home, Sarah watched him carry the plants through the kitchen.

“We getting fancy?” she asked.

“Reinforcing the theme,” he said.

“Subtle,” she replied.

He stepped out into the garden and surveyed the bed along the back fence.

It was already home to a couple of scruffy shrubs and some lavender that had seen better days.

He knelt and started to dig.

The soil was stubborn but not unyielding.

He worked methodically, loosening clumps, pulling out small stones.

The rhythm soothed him.

At some point, he realised he was humming.

One of Lillian’s songs. A quiet one. The chorus about “the sound my life makes when I ask for no more.”

He didn’t stop.

Lillian appeared at the back door, tea in hand.

She watched him for a moment.

“You look like a very handsome mole,” she said.

He glanced up.

“High praise,” he said.

She came to perch on the low wall.

“What are their names?” she asked, nodding at the roses.

“They’re plants, not pets,” he said.

“Everything has a name,” she replied.

He thought.

“This one’s Berlin,” he said, patting the red one.

Her ears twitched.

“Too on the nose,” she said.

“Exactly,” he said.

“And the others?”

“This is Here,” he said, nodding to the soft pink. “And this is… Maybe.”

He tapped the white one.

“Maybe?” she echoed.

“For whatever comes next,” he said. “Trips. Projects. Unexpected opportunities that don’t require leaving the people I love for years.”

Her throat tightened.

“You’re getting very good at this symbolism thing,” she said.

“I live with you and our daughter,” he said. “It was inevitable.”

---

Over the next few days, he fussed over the roses like first-time parents fuss over sleep schedules.

He checked the soil. Adjusted the supports. Consulted three different gardening websites about mildew.

“Is this your replacement obsession?” Penny asked, watching him from the patio one afternoon. “Now that you’re not refreshing your inbox every five minutes?”

“You say ‘replacement’ as if it’s a bad thing,” he said.

“I say ‘replacement’ as if it’s… redirected energy,” she replied.

He glanced at her.

“Is that therapy speak for ‘coping’?”

“Yes,” she said. “And I approve.”

He smiled.

When he straightened up, his back twinged.

He put a hand on the small of it and groaned quietly.

Lillian, deadheading some marigolds nearby, snorted.

“Welcome to middle age,” she said.

“Rude,” he replied.

“Accurate,” she said.

He looked at the garden.

It wasn’t perfect.

There were bald patches in the grass, weeds in the beds, a patch of damp on the side of the shed he needed to deal with.

But the roses, ridiculous and hopeful, stood like small declarations.

We intend to be here.

So do we.

---

One evening, as the light slanted low, Sarah joined him by the fence.

She held a sketchbook and a pencil, as usual.

“They’re very on-the-nose,” she said, eyeing the roses.

“I’ve been told,” he said.

“I like them,” she added.

“Thank you,” he said.

She knelt and started to draw.

Not the flowers themselves, but the way they sat against the fence. The tilt of their stems. The soil around them.

“You know what I keep thinking about?” she said, pencil moving.

“What?”

“That day in the station,” she said. “When we were waiting for my train. You and Mum looked…

She searched for the word.

“Untethered,” she said. “Like someone had unplugged you.”

He winced.

“Sorry,” he said.

“Don’t be,” she said. “It was… honest. I liked seeing that. I like seeing this.”

She nodded to the roses.

“You’re… plugging back in,” she said.

He smiled faintly.

“Trying,” he said.

“You don’t have to try,” she said. “You already did the hard bit.”

He frowned.

“Sending an email is not—”

“Not the email,” she interrupted. “The bit where you believed us when we said staying was… enough.”

He swallowed.

She shrugged, scratched in a shadow.

“The rest is just… maintenance,” she said.

He looked at the roses, then at her.

“You sound like me,” he said.

“Genetics,” she replied.

They both laughed.

---

On Sunday, he finally opened his sent folder.

The Berlin email was there, nestled between an order confirmation for screws and a reminder from the dentist.

He read it through.

Not to torment himself.

To mark it.

“Do you ever think about… the other you?” Lillian asked that night as they lay in bed.

“Which one?”

“The one in Berlin,” she said.

He thought.

“Sometimes,” he admitted. “I imagine him getting on a tram. Going to work. Coming home to… a flat. Nice. Quiet. Less dog hair.”

“Rude,” she said.

“He probably sleeps more,” Miles went on. “He has fewer people knocking on his wall. He has no idea what our council tax is. He might be… content.”

She was quiet a moment.

“Do you envy him?” she asked.

He considered.

“Sometimes,” he said. “On days when the house feels too small or the news feels too big.”

He turned to look at her.

“Do you?”

She snorted.

“I envy anyone who can wear skinny jeans comfortably,” she said.

He huffed.

“But no,” she added. “I don’t envy that Miles. He doesn’t get to hear you singing in the garden when you think no one’s listening.”

“I don’t—”

“You do,” she said.

He sighed.

“I envy *this* Miles sometimes,” she said softly. “From Season 1. When we didn’t know how scary everything could get. When the biggest drama was whether Sarah’s school would allow non-regulation socks.”

He smiled.

“We were very outraged,” he said.

“We’re still here,” she said. “Different. But here.”

He reached for her hand under the covers.

“The ground beneath us held,” he said.

“Mostly because you keep fixing it,” she replied.

He laughed into the dark.

Outside, the roses rustled in the breeze.

Their roots, ridiculous and earnest, burrowed a little deeper.

Inside, the foundations of their small, stubborn life settled.

Not immovable.

Not perfect.

Enough.

# Episode 20: Empty Nest?

The first sign that the house had changed was the shoes.

There were fewer of them.

Once upon a time, the mat by the front door had been a hazard zone of rubber and laces and inexplicable mud. Lillian’s stage boots with their glitter-scuffed heels, Miles’s sensible trainers, Sarah’s paint-flecked high-tops, Ramon’s immaculate Italian loafers that he took off even if he was only “popping in for five minutes,” Rosalia’s ballet flats with the worn-out toes from dancing while she cooked.

Now, on a soft September afternoon when the light came in low and golden, Lillian stood in the hallway and counted.

Her boots. Miles’s trainers. One pair of Sarah’s sandals. A lone flip-flop whose partner had gone to whatever single-sock heaven awaited lost footwear.

“That’s not right,” Lillian said, out loud, to the umbrella stand.

From the living room came the soft, lazy shuffle of pages turning. “What’s not right?” Miles called.

“The shoe ratio,” Lillian said. “It’s… anemic.”

Miles appeared in the doorway, glasses askew, a gardening book half-open in his hand. He took in the sight of her, arms folded, staring accusingly at the mat as if it had personally offended her.

“Love,” he said gently, “we did just do a clear-out.”

“That was a sock clear-out,” she said. “A humane culling of orphans. This is different. This is… this is absence.”

She nudged the mat with one paw. It didn’t fight back.

Miles stepped over to her, following her gaze. “Sarah took a few things over this morning,” he reminded her. “Just a bag or two.”

“Bag or three,” Lillian muttered.

He smiled, the corner of his mouth quirking in that way that meant he was trying to be supportive and amused at the same time. “Bag or three,” he amended. “She said she wanted to see what it felt like to wake up somewhere that was… hers.”

Lillian made a face, which in her case involved several expressive rearrangements of badger features and an extra flare of whiskers.

“Somewhere that’s hers is here,” she said. “We have literally painted ‘Sarah’s room’ on the door. In three colours. One of which glows in the dark and terrifies me at two in the morning.”

“Yes,” Miles said. “And also, she is twenty.”

Lillian narrowed her eyes. “How dare you.”

He laughed softly, then slipped an arm around her shoulders. She leaned in on reflex, the familiar weight of him slotting into the side of her like he’d been designed for that exact job.

The house hummed quietly around them: the tick of the old radiator, the distant buzz of someone’s lawnmower down the street, the faint creak of the upstairs floorboards as Mozzarella, having finished his afternoon patrol of the garden, flopped dramatically onto Sarah’s rug.

“She’s not moving out,” Lillian said, softer now. “She’s just… testing you.”

“Testing me?”

“The universe,” she clarified. “Gravity. Distance. Whether I can survive if I cannot shout up the stairs for her to bring down my phone charger.”

“You can shout,” Miles said. “She just won’t hear you from Dalston.”

“Miles.”

He squeezed her shoulder. “She’s not going to Dalston.”

“She could. At any moment. With her… her hand-thrown mugs and her ethical socks and her sketchbooks full of feelings.”

He pressed a kiss to the top of her head. “At the moment, she is going to the high street, three streets away, to a flat that smells faintly of new paint and someone else’s mistakes. And she’s coming back for dinner.”

Lillian exhaled. The breath left her like a deflating balloon, but quieter.

“Dinner,” she repeated.

“Dinner,” Miles said. “At which point you can quiz her mercilessly and decide whether you approve of the water pressure.”

“Obviously I don’t approve of the water pressure,” she said. “Our shower is the only correct pressure in London. It has ruined me for all other showers. This is our legacy.”

He chuckled. “I’ll put that on the family crest.”

---

Sarah’s “maybe-flat” was the top floor of a narrow terraced house ten minutes’ walk away. It had two bedrooms, sloping ceilings, and a skylight that faced just the right way to get late afternoon sun in September and almost none in November.

“It’s short-term,” Sarah had said at the kitchen table a week earlier, fanning out the letting agent’s printouts like tarot cards. “Three months. Just through the autumn. I keep my stuff here. My address is still here. It’s just… a test run.”

“For what?” Lillian had asked, wary.

“For being… not entirely in your bed,” Sarah had replied, deadpan.

Lillian had tried to scowl and ended up snorting into her tea.

Now, Lillian stood in the doorway of the maybe-flat’s living room, which at present contained: one second-hand sofa, an IKEA coffee table that had seen things, a stack of boxes, and a potted plant that looked like it was regretting its life choices.

Sarah was in the middle of the room, arms outstretched, turning slowly like a sun-obsessed satellite.

“Look,” she said, as the sunlight caught the cheap blinds and cut across her fur in bright stripes. “Golden hour lives here. At least until winter.”

Lillian watched her. Their daughter. Their girl who had gone to Brighton and come back with clay under her nails and new lines of thought in her sketchbooks, who had met her biological mother in a coffee shop and somehow come home more rooted, not less.

“You know golden hour also lives in our kitchen,” Lillian said. “Through the back window. Over the sink. On the pile of plates you promised to do.”

Sarah grinned. “Yes, but here it hits my face just so, and I can sit on the floor and pretend I’m in a film about a struggling artist, instead of a sitcom about a badger who can’t let go.”

Lillian’s mouth did that thing where it tried to be outraged and failed.

“I can let go,” she said.

Sarah padded over, bare-pawed, and wrapped her arms around Lillian’s middle, leaning into the familiar softness.

“I know,” Sarah said. “That’s the point, isn’t it? You let go, and I still… orbit.”

Lillian snorted again, but this time her eyes were wet.

“Who gave you permission to become emotionally literate?” she demanded.

“You,” Sarah said simply. “Several years ago. In an argument about my curfew.”

Miles, who had been quietly measuring the windows with a tape measure he had “just happened to bring,” cleared his throat.

“So,” he said. “Curtains?”

Sarah released Lillian and turned to him, face bright. “Yes! But like… nice ones. Not landlord beige. I want colour. Maybe that deep green from the kitchen walls? To match-but-not-match.”

Miles’s ears twitched. “We can absolutely do that,” he said. “We might even have some spare fabric. And if we don’t, we… know a woman who can bargain like a pirate in the fabric district.”

Lillian dabbed at the corner of her eye with the back of her paw. “I’ll have you know I am a *polite* pirate,” she said. “I say please before I emotionally pressure them into knocking off ten percent.”

Sarah beamed. “See? How could I possibly move to Dalston when I have access to this level of resource?”

“Exactly,” Lillian said.

She looked around again. At the sloping ceiling. At the undecided boxes. At the scuffed skirting boards that Miles’s fingers were already itching to sand.

“So,” she said. “Tell us. What are the terms of this… separation?”

Sarah bit her lip in thought. “I was thinking,” she said slowly, “three nights a week here. Four at home. But not fixed which ones. Just… when it makes sense. When I’ve got late classes, or an early workshop, or when I need to stay up and sculpt things that look like they’ve crawled out of my subconscious.”

“And when you want your mum to make you soup,” Lillian added.

“And when I want my mum to make me soup,” Sarah agreed.

Miles nodded. “And rent?”

Sarah pulled a face. “Oh no, is this the bit where you make me pay in vegetables?”

“Tempting,” he said. “But no. We talked about this. You’re teaching now. You’re earning. You can contribute something. Even if it’s just as a line in your own head. ‘I pay for this.’ It feels different.”

Sarah’s shoulders hitched in a little half-shrug. “Yeah,” she said. “I… I want that, actually. I like the idea that I can pay my own bills. And also turn up at your house and empty your fridge.”

“Now you’re getting it,” Lillian said.

---

That evening, the house did feel different.

It was not quieter, exactly. The television still murmured in the corner, some comfortingly terrible renovation show where people argued about taps. The kettle still whistled. Mozzarella still huffed dramatic sighs in doorways.

But there was a small, Sarah-shaped gap.

Her bedroom door was open. The fairy lights were off. A pile of sketchbooks sat in a neat stack on the desk, three of them missing — gone to the flat.

Lillian stood in the doorway, paws on the frame.

“She left the plant,” she said.

Miles came to stand beside her. “She didn’t want to traumatise it with two moves,” he said.

“She trusts us with its emotional wellbeing,” Lillian said. “That’s a lot of responsibility.”

He leaned his shoulder against hers. “We kept her alive, love. I think we can manage a fern.”

She elbowed him lightly. “She arrived pre-alive,” she said. “We just… upgraded her.”

They fell quiet.

From downstairs came the ping of Lillian’s phone. She ignored it. Then the buzz. Then the third, more insistent vibration.

“Someone’s keen,” Miles murmured.

“If it’s a spam text about my car insurance, I will burn the system down,” she said, but she padded down the stairs anyway.

It wasn’t spam.

It was a photo.

Sarah, in the maybe-flat living room, sitting cross-legged on the floor in that shaft of late afternoon light. Her sketchbook open. A mug of tea balanced precariously on the edge of the IKEA table. Behind her, the skylight framed a sliver of pinking sky.

Underneath, a message:

> Test flight commencing. Not leaving the planet. Love you. x

Lillian stared at the screen. Something inside her did the complicated thing again: the ache and the swell, the pinch and the pride.

Miles came to stand at her shoulder.

“She looks happy,” he said.

“She looks smug,” Lillian corrected. “Which is my favourite flavour of her.”

She typed back, paws surprisingly steady.

> Don’t forget, even astronauts come home to do laundry. Soup on Thursdays. Bring your own bowls, we’re going minimalist.

A reply came almost instantly.

> Lol. Tell Dad I found a patch of wall that desperately needs his level. It’s driving me mad.

Miles’s tail flicked. “Already on it,” he said.

---

Later, when the dishes were done and the evening had landed properly, they sat together on the sofa. The lamp cast a warm pool of light. The window showed their own reflection back at them: a badger and a German Shepherd, shoulder to shoulder, in a house that had grown and shrunk and stretched around them for years.

Lillian shifted closer, tucking her feet up, her head finding its way to Miles’s shoulder without needing directions.

“Do you miss her?” she asked.

He took a breath. Held it. Let it go.

“Yes,” he said. “And also… no.”

She tilted her head to look up at him. “Rude.”

He smiled. “I miss the version where she is always under this roof. Where I can hear her clattering around in the kitchen at midnight, and I know she’s here, even if I don’t see her for a day. But I don’t miss… watching her feel too big for the walls. You saw it too.”

Lillian swallowed. She had seen it. The way Sarah had started pacing the kitchen while talking about her workshops. The way her eyes had gone distant when she described the studios in Brighton, the feeling of waking up in a place that was hers to mess up.

“I like that she chose a place that’s… parallel,” Lillian said slowly. “Not an escape hatch. A… side door.”

Miles nodded. “We didn’t build this house to trap her,” he said. “We built it so she’d have somewhere to come back to. Even if she lives ten minutes or ten hours away.”

She nudged him again. “We didn’t build this house,” she said. “Some terrible landlord did. We just emotionally renovated it.”

He laughed softly.

They sat in that quiet for a while. The television hosts argued amiably about tiles. Somewhere outside, a scooter buzzed down the road, someone shouted a name, a dog answered.

“You know what the worst part is?” Lillian said eventually.

“Hm?”

“I was absolutely unbearable at nineteen,” she said. “If I had moved out then, my mother would have sent a fruit basket to whichever poor sod took me in, and then thrown a party.”

Miles considered this. “Your mother adored you,” he said.

“Yes,” Lillian said. “But she also adored silence.”

He looked around the living room. At the not-quite-empty house. At the pile of Sarah’s sketchbooks still in the corner, the dent her body had made over years in the sofa cushion.

“It’s not empty,” he said.

“I know,” she said. “It just… echoes differently.”

---

A few days later, the house woke up to the sound of the front door banging open.

“Emergency!” Sarah shouted.

Lillian sat up in bed, fur on end. Miles already had one paw on the floor.

“What?” he called. “What’s wrong?”

Sarah burst into their bedroom, wild-eyed, clutching a crumpled paper bag to her chest.

“The hob doesn’t work,” she said.

Miles blinked. “The… hob?”

“It’s possessed,” Sarah said. “It clicks but it doesn’t light and I Googled it and then I panicked and now I’m here.”

Lillian stared at her. Then she started to laugh.

“You sprinted across Islington in your pyjamas because your hob is having an existential crisis?” she asked.

Sarah looked down at herself. Flannel trousers with cartoon moons, oversized hoodie, mismatched socks.

“Yes,” she said. “And because I knew you’d laugh at me, and then Dad would fix it.”

Miles swung his legs properly out of bed.

“Right,” he said, already reaching for his toolbox. “Let’s go exorcise a stove.”

Lillian flopped back onto the pillows, grinning up at the ceiling.

“Empty nest, my arse,” she muttered.

The house, old and creaky and full of ghosts of dinners past, seemed to agree.

---

That evening, as the repaired hob clicked obediently to life in the maybe-flat and Sarah stirred her own soup for the first time, Lillian stood in their own kitchen, watching the light slant through the window.

The golden hour here was the same as it had always been: soft and generous, turning the chipped mugs into treasures and the washing-up into art.

She hummed under her breath, a melody she’d been working on. Something about spinning and staying, about orbits and anchors. About how you could watch someone walk ten minutes down the road and feel both hollowed out and full to the brim.

Miles came up behind her, slipping his arms around her waist, his chin finding its place on her shoulder.

“Thinking?” he asked.

“Always,” she said. “Currently about birds.”

“Birds,” he repeated.

“Specifically the ones that leave the nest and then come back because their landlord’s boiler is rubbish,” she said.

He laughed into her fur.

“She’s going to be fine,” he murmured. “We all are.”

Lillian looked at the back door. At the line of shoes by the mat. At the extra pair that had returned that afternoon, scuffed and paint-flecked, kicked off in a hurry.

“Yeah,” she said. “I think we are.”

Outside, the garden glowed in the low light. The shed — The Den, as it was now properly called — stood solid and inviting, fairy lights winking on its eaves. Somewhere inside, the faint echo of Lillian’s own recorded voice drifted from the speakers, singing about staying put while the world spun.

The nest wasn’t empty. It had just grown another branch.

And for the first time in weeks, the echo in the house sounded like room — space made on purpose — rather than loss.
# Episode 21: Rosalia's Last Supper

The smell hit them halfway down the street.

Garlic, tomatoes, and something else — something deep and slow and patient, like a Sunday afternoon had been simmering on the stove since sunrise.

“Uh oh,” Lillian said, sniffing.

Miles glanced sideways at her as they walked. “Uh oh?”

“That’s not weeknight food,” she said. “That’s farewell food. That’s ‘I used every pan I own and three I borrowed from next door’ food.”

Ahead of them, the lights in Ramon and Rosalia’s front windows glowed warmer than usual. The curtains were drawn back. Shadows moved inside, elongated and theatrical.

Lillian felt something twist under her ribs. The same something that had been twisting a lot lately: at estate agent signs, at cardboard boxes, at the way Rosalia had started sentences with “When we’re in Hastings…” instead of “If.”

“It’s just dinner,” Miles said gently.

“Nothing good has ever started with ‘just dinner,’” Lillian said. “Ask anyone who’s been dumped in a restaurant.”

“We’re not being dumped,” he said. “They’re moving to the seaside, not ghosting us.”

She snorted. “Tell that to my attachment issues.”

At their feet, Mozzarella trotted along with great purpose, tail high, nose twitching. He had a keen sense of occasion and an even keener sense of marinara.

By the time they reached the front door, the smells were so strong they felt like a physical presence, wrapping around them like a shawl.

Before they could knock, the door flew open.

“Mi famiglia!” Rosalia cried, flinging her arms wide.

She wore an apron that had definitely seen things. Her dark hair was piled up in a hasty knot, a wooden spoon tucked into it like a hairpin. There was a streak of something red on her cheek.

Lillian made a noise that was half laughter, half sob.

“Don’t you start,” Rosalia said, pointing the spoon at her. “You cry, I cry, the tiramisu curdles. Inside. Shoes off. Except for the dog. He is an honoured guest.”

Mozzarella swanned past her into the hallway with the confidence of a creature who had never once paid rent.

Miles smiled, stepping inside and bending to untie his laces. “It smells incredible,” he said.

“Of course it does,” Ramon’s voice called from the kitchen. “My wife is a genius and I am a humble chopping assistant.”

“Liar,” Rosalia muttered fondly. “He thinks he is head of herbs. Come in, come in. Sarah is already here.”

Lillian perked up slightly at that. “She is?”

“In the kitchen,” Rosalia said, shooing them along. “Trying to steal my secrets. I tell her there are no secrets, there is only butter. She does not believe me.”

---

The kitchen looked like a festival had exploded.

Every surface was occupied. Pots bubbled on the stove. Trays waited on the counter. A mountain of chopped vegetables sat in a bowl the size of a bassinette. The air was thick with steam and laughter.

Sarah stood at the island, sleeves rolled up, apron on, wielding a wooden spoon with surprising authority.

“You’re late,” she said, as they walked in.

“You stole our child,” Lillian said. “We had to fill out a missing person’s report.”

“I texted you,” Sarah pointed out. “Three times. And sent a picture of the sauce.”

Lillian could not argue with this. She had indeed received a close-up photo of a pan of tomatoes, captioned: *Come hungry.*

Ramon emerged from behind the fridge door, carrying yet another bottle of wine.

“There they are,” he said, beaming. “The victims.”

“Victims?” Miles repeated.

“Of a culinary assault,” Ramon said. “We are going to feed you until you beg for mercy. Or Tupperware. Whichever comes first.”

Lillian eyed the crowded counters. “Honestly, I’m more concerned about the structural integrity of the table,” she said. “Has anyone done a load-bearing assessment recently?”

“Your husband can hold it up,” Rosalia said. “He is strong, like garlic.”

“That’s not… never mind,” Miles said.

He accepted a glass of red wine from Ramon and took a careful sip. It was rich and heavy and tasted like it had opinions.

“So,” he said. “What’s on the menu?”

Rosalia lifted the lids of various pans with a flourish.

“Lasagne, of course,” she said. “You think I can leave this street without one more lasagne? Impossible. Then there is aubergine parmigiana. And roasted peppers. And a salad, which I include so you feel morally superior despite what you are doing to your arteries.”

“And dessert?” Lillian asked, already bracing herself emotionally.

Rosalia smiled wickedly. “You will see.”

Sarah leaned over to Lillian, lowering her voice.

“It’s tiramisu,” she whispered. “But with hazelnut. I told her it was illegal. She said that’s why it’s good.”

Lillian clutched her heart.

“I’m not ready,” she said.

“You have approximately forty-five minutes to prepare,” Sarah said. “Chop something. It will help.”

---

They ate in stages, because there was no other way.

First came bruschetta: thick slices of toasted bread rubbed with garlic and topped with chopped tomatoes and basil. Lillian tried to maintain some semblance of dignity and failed after the second piece.

“This bread is a crime,” she said around a mouthful.

“I will bake for you in prison,” Rosalia said.

Then the lasagne arrived, steaming and golden, the top just the right level of crispy.

Ramon made a production out of cutting it, wielding the knife like a conductor’s baton.

“Ladies and gentlemen, and assorted woodland creatures,” he said. “We are gathered here today to honour a union. Between pasta and cheese.”

“Stop talking and serve,” Sarah said, poking him in the side.

The plates filled. The conversation swelled. Stories flowed as easily as the wine.

“Remember the first time we had dinner here?” Lillian said at one point, gesturing with her fork.

Miles smiled. “You mean when you over-seasoned the potatoes to impress them and nearly killed Ramon?”

“I did not nearly kill him,” Lillian protested. “His eyebrows grew back.”

Ramon stroked his thick, dark brows theatrically. “They came back stronger,” he said. “Like our love.”

Rosalia rolled her eyes. “You were both disasters,” she said. “Burnt everything. No salt. Too much salt. Smoke alarm concerto. But you were trying. That is what counts.”

“And now look at us,” Lillian said. “You’re the ones abandoning us for fresher fish and lower rent.”

“Abandoning,” Rosalia repeated, arching a brow.

Lillian faltered. The word had come out sharper than she meant.

“I mean,” she said, softer, “leaving.”

A small silence settled over the table, like a napkin being laid down.

Ramon reached across and squeezed her paw.

“We are not abandoning you,” he said. “We are… expanding the map.”

Rosalia nodded, her eyes warm.

“We are going to the coast,” she said. “Not to Mars.”

“Speak for yourself,” Ramon murmured. “Have you seen British seaside fashion? It is extraterrestrial.”

Lillian huffed a laugh.

“I know,” she said. “It’s just… you’ve been here. Right there.” She pointed vaguely toward the wall that separated their semis. “For so long. I knock, you appear with wine. I cry, you appear with food. Miles breaks something, you appear with judgement.”

“I never judge,” Ramon said, offended. “I offer constructive criticism.”

“Last week you told me my choice of screws was a ‘metaphor for my fear of commitment,’” Miles pointed out.

“And were they?” Sarah asked, interested.

Miles considered. “They were too short,” he admitted.

Ramon spread his hands. “See?”

Rosalia leaned forward, resting her chin on her hand.

“Lillian,” she said. “When we moved into this street, I was so scared. New country, new language, new everything. And then one morning I look out the window and there is a badger in a dressing gown arguing with a pigeon.”

Sarah choked on her wine.

“In my defence,” Lillian said, “that pigeon started it.”

Rosalia smiled. “I thought, ‘Okay. I will be fine. The universe has put me next to chaos and song.’”

Her voice wobbled on the last word. She cleared her throat.

“We are not going to a place with no phones,” she said. “We are not disappearing. You have a garden shed with internet now. We have a spare room with a sea view and no tastefully chosen furniture yet. You will come. We will feed you. You will complain about the pebbles.”

“Absolutely,” Lillian said. “Sand or nothing.”

Sarah, who had been watching all this with her artist’s eyes, lifted her glass.

“To expanded maps,” she said.

They clinked.

---

By the time dessert arrived, the table was a battlefield of crumbs and sauce streaks. The lasagne dish sat scraped almost clean. Someone had drawn a smiley face in the remaining béchamel.

Rosalia vanished into the kitchen and returned carrying a large glass dish like an offering.

“Tiramisu,” she announced. “The last one made in this house. Do not make that face, Lillian. There will be more. But this one is special.”

Lillian’s throat tightened.

“Is it infused with our tears?” she asked.

“Only a little,” Rosalia said.

She set it down on the table. The top was dusted with cocoa and chopped hazelnuts, a constellation of chocolate.

Sarah made an appreciative noise.

“It’s beautiful,” she said.

“It’s obscene,” Lillian said. “Cut me the biggest piece.”

As the first spoonful hit her tongue, something unlocked in Lillian’s chest.

It wasn’t just the coffee and cream and crunch. It was every late-night debrief at this table after gigs gone wrong. Every impromptu homecoming party when Sarah arrived back from Brighton. Every ordinary Tuesday when nothing special had happened except that they had all been together and there had been enough food and enough laughter.

She closed her eyes.

“You okay?” Miles murmured.

“I am having a religious experience,” she said.

“Good,” Rosalia said briskly. “Now, before anyone becomes too full to move, I have something to say.”

Ramon groaned theatrically. “Here it comes,” he said. “The speech.”

Rosalia shot him a look and then turned back to their friends.

“When we told you we were thinking about moving,” she began, “you did not say, ‘How dare you.’”

“I did,” Lillian said.

“Not out loud,” Rosalia replied.

Lillian conceded this with a tilt of her paw.

“You said, ‘Tell us how we can help,’” Rosalia continued. “You offered to come to viewings. To look after our plants. To feed our sourdough starter which, frankly, scares me a little. You did not make this about you. Even though I know it… is a little bit about you.”

She smiled sadly.

“You have been our family here,” she said. “More than neighbours. When my mother calls and asks, ‘Have you found your people?’ I say, ‘Yes. They live through the wall. They are loud and ridiculous and they love us very much.’”

Lillian’s eyes flooded.

“Oh, for God’s sake,” she muttered, swiping at them.

Sarah reached over and took her paw under the table.

Rosalia took a breath.

“So,” she said. “In honour of that, we have prepared… gifts.”

Ramon perked up. “Yes! My favourite part.”

He darted out of the room and returned with a small stack of wrapped packages, unashamedly reused gift bags crinkling.

“First,” he said, handing one to Miles, “for you.”

Miles accepted it, puzzled, and peeled back the tissue paper.

Inside was a notebook. The cover was embossed with tiny tools: hammers, spanners, saws. The title read: *Projects That Can’t Be Done Over Zoom.*

He laughed.

“You know me too well,” he said.

“We expect you to fill it with lists,” Ramon said. “And then bring it to Hastings so you can judge our DIY.”

Next, Rosalia passed a flatter parcel to Sarah.

She opened it to reveal a sketchbook, heavier than her usual ones, the paper thick and creamy.

On the first page, in Rosalia’s looping script, were the words: *Rooms Yet to Be Drawn.*

“In case you get bored of our faces and need to draw new ones,” Rosalia said.

Sarah’s smile trembled. “Never,” she said. “But… thank you.”

Finally, there was a slightly lumpy package for Lillian.

Lillian held it as if it might explode.

“If this is a symbolic representation of my abandonment issues,” she said, “I’m sending it back.”

“Just open it,” Rosalia said.

Inside was a tea towel.

It was white, or had been at some point, now printed with a pattern of tiny cartoon vegetables dancing with kitchen utensils. In the middle, in bold, hand-embroidered letters, were the words: *You Always Have a Plate Here.*

Lillian stared.

“Oh,” she said, very small.

Her paw traced the stitching. The slightly wonky *A* where Rosalia had clearly broken a needle and sworn in two languages.

“You see?” Rosalia said softly. “We are not closing the door. We are just… opening another one. With a worse train connection.”

Lillian stood up, abruptly.

The chair scraped back. Everyone looked at her.

She held the tea towel in one paw and her wine glass in the other.

“I would like to make a toast,” she said, her voice steadier than she felt. “Before I become a puddle on your nice floor.”

Ramon brightened. “At last, a speech from the badger.”

Lillian took a breath.

“When we moved into our house,” she said, “I thought I had finally done it. Me. House. Husband. Child. Oven that works ninety percent of the time. I thought, ‘Okay. This is the picture. This is the thing I’m supposed to hang on the wall.’”

She gestured vaguely with her glass.

“And then you two turned up,” she went on. “With your wine and your weird salad bowls and your habit of turning up whenever the smoke alarm went off. And I realised the picture wasn’t just the three of us at a table. It was this.”

She swept her arm to encompass the cramped dining room: the overcrowded table, the crowded walls, the crowded hearts.

“It was kitchens that merge through thin walls,” she said. “It was arguments about whose turn it is to buy bin bags. It was having someone to text when the boiler makes a funny noise at midnight and your husband is asleep and you don’t want to wake him but you also don’t want to die of carbon monoxide poisoning.”

Miles opened his mouth. Closed it. Decided this was neither the time nor the place to correct her understanding of carbon monoxide.

“You have been…” She swallowed, wiped at her eyes again. “You have been proof that family doesn’t stop at the edge of the mortgage. It leaks. In the best way. Like sauce onto the nice tablecloth.”

Rosalia laughed wetly.

“So,” Lillian finished, raising her glass, “to leaked family. To expanded maps. To the fact that even if you live in a place with too many seagulls, we will still come and eat your lasagne. To the last supper… in this house.”

She paused, then added, “And to the first supper in the next one.”

They drank.

The wine tasted like endings and beginnings, like doors closing and opening, like garlic that would linger on their breath for days as a reminder.

---
