By the time they finally pushed their chairs back, it was late. The air in the house had cooled. The windows had fogged with breath and steam. The leftovers had been packed away into a small army of containers.

At the door, there were hugs. Too many, and not enough.

Sarah clung to Rosalia for a long moment.

“Who will I talk to about my terrible dates?” Sarah asked.

“Me,” Rosalia said. “On video. In my kitchen, with the sea out the window. It will be very cinematic. You can draw it.”

Ramon hugged Miles so hard that the shepherd made a small surprised noise.

“Do not let the badger redecorate in her grief,” Ramon whispered into his ear. “At least not without supervision.”

“I heard that,” Lillian said.

“You were meant to,” Ramon replied, releasing Miles and pulling her in.

He squeezed her, then stepped back to look at her properly.

“You will be fine,” he said. “You have your shed. Your songs. Your girl. Your dog. Your slightly overachieving husband. And you have us. Just with more salt in our hair.”

“I hate this,” Lillian said.

“I know,” he said. “I do too. That’s how we know it matters.”

She exhaled, then nodded.

“Okay,” she said. “Go. Before I chain myself to your banister.”

Ramon smiled. “We’d only have to feed you,” he said. “It would be more work.”

They stepped out into the cool night air.

The street was quiet. The stars, such as they were in London, blinked faintly overhead. The smell of garlic clung to them like a blessing.

Miles took Lillian’s paw as they walked the short distance home.

“They’re not gone yet,” he said. “The van doesn’t come for another week.”

“I know,” she said. “But that was the last supper when they were just… there. You know? Next door. Available at the drop of a hat, or a bad mood.”

He squeezed her paw.

“We’ll make more,” he said. “In Hastings. In our kitchen. In the shed. It’s not… finite.”

She glanced at him.

“Listen to you,” she said. “Mr. Abundance Mindset.”

“I’ve been reading Penny’s mindfulness blog,” he said.

She barked out a laugh.

“Of course you have,” she said. “Does it help?”

“Sometimes,” he said. “Mostly when she posts pictures of her cat.”

They reached their front door.

Inside, the house smelled faintly of whatever they had cooked the night before. The table was bare. The sink was empty. The quiet wrapped around them like a different kind of sauce.

Lillian hung up her coat and paused, looking at the thin wall they shared with Ramon and Rosalia’s house.

She pressed her paw flat against it.

“Still there,” she murmured.

For now.

Miles came up behind her, resting his paw over hers on the plaster.

“For now,” he echoed.

She turned to him.

“Promise me something,” she said.

“Anything.”

“When they go,” she said, “we won’t… try to replace them. With some new couple who also brings wine and judgement and emotional support.”

He smiled.

“Love,” he said, “no one would survive that audition process.”

She snorted.

“Fair,” she said.

He grew serious.

“We’ll let that space… be empty for a bit,” he said. “We’ll let it ache. And then, when it doesn’t ache so sharply, we’ll see what it wants to be. Maybe quiet. Maybe rented to a nice, boring accountant we barely speak to. Maybe a studio for someone. We don’t have to decide now.”

She nodded slowly.

“Okay,” she said. “But if an accountant moves in, I’m still making them lasagne.”

“Obviously,” he said.

They stood there for a moment, palms against the wall, feeling the faint hum of the house.

It wasn’t a last supper, not really.

It was the end of one long, overlapping meal. Plates cleared. New courses coming. The same table, the same chairs, slightly more worn.

And somewhere down the line, by a different sea, another table would be waiting — with a seat that always, always had their name on it.
# Episode 22: Lillian's Lullaby

The rain came back the week Lillian lost her song.

Not literally — the clouds over Islington were not in direct communication with her vocal cords, despite what Penny’s blog commenters might believe — but close enough.

It started on a Tuesday.

Lillian sat in The Den, microphone in front of her, headphones on, guitar in her lap. The fairy lights strung along the rafters glowed dimly against the grey afternoon. The little red recording light blinked like an impatient eye.

“Okay,” Miles’s voice came through the headphones, warm and practical. “Whenever you’re ready.”

She took a breath.

The song was there. She knew it was there. It had been pacing around the edges of her brain for weeks, humming under her skin while she made tea, while she weeded the herb bed, while she watched Sarah walk down the road toward her maybe-flat.

A song about staying.

About the sound of the kettle that always whistled a half-tone sharp. About the thud of Mozzarella’s paws on the stairs. About the way Miles’s key scraped the lock just-so when he came in at the end of the day.

The sound of the world not changing in the big ways, while everything shifted in the small ones.

She opened her mouth.

Nothing came out.

Not literally nothing — a thin, reedy approximation of a note faltered into the mic — but nothing of the thing she meant.

“Again,” she said, mostly to herself.

She strummed the opening chords. They sounded fine. Simple. The kind of gentle, looping progression that had seemed so right when she wrote it on the back of an old council tax bill.

She tried to sing.

Her voice wobbled. Cracked. Went flat.

“Again,” she said, sharper.

In the adjoining room, separated from her by the shed’s double-glazed window and a curtain they had found in the charity shop, Miles watched the levels on the laptop screen dip and falter.

He winced.

“Love,” he said into his mic, “do you want to take a bre—”

“No,” she snapped.

Silence fell heavy in his headphones.

He could see her through the glass, perched on the stool, shoulders up around her ears. The Lillian who existed on stage — and, by extension, in this shed-turned-home-studio — was usually bigger than the walls. She filled space. Claimed it. Turned it into a playground.

Today, she looked small.

He muted his channel, took off his headphones, and went into the main room.

Lillian refused to look at him.

“I am fine,” she said, before he opened his mouth.

“I didn’t say anything,” he replied.

“You were going to.”

He came around to stand in front of her, gentle but immovable.

“Look at me,” he said.

She did, reluctantly.

Up close, he could see it: the tightness around her eyes, the way she kept clenching and unclenching her paws on the neck of the guitar.

“It’s gone,” she said.

“What is?”

“The thing,” she said, waving one paw vaguely in the air. “The… juice. The… song. The whatever-it-is that makes noise come out and mean something instead of sounding like a sad kazoo.”

He hid a smile.

“You don’t sound like a sad kazoo,” he said.

“I sound like a kazoo that’s gone through a divorce,” she said.

“There it is,” he murmured.

“What?”

“The thing that makes noise come out and mean something,” he said. “You’re just using it for insults instead of music at the moment.”

She huffed, but a corner of her mouth twitched.

“I wanted this one to be good,” she said, softer. “Not just… another verse about being fat and fabulous and making men cry at open mics. I wanted it to be… quieter. Like…” She flapped her paw again, frustrated. “Like the bit between heartbeats.”

Miles’s chest squeezed.

“I think you might be putting slightly too much pressure on yourself,” he said.

“This is our last season,” she shot back. “I don’t have time to be chill.”

He reached out and gently pried the guitar out of her grip, leaning it against the wall.

“Walk with me,” he said.

“It’s raining,” she protested.

“Good,” he said. “Free atmosphere.”

---

The garden was a patchwork of damp earth and determined greenery.

The rosebushes along the back fence shook droplets off their heads as the drizzle eased temporarily. The basil in the herb bed sulked under its cloche. The path stones glistened.

Lillian stepped out under the overhang, arms folded, glaring at the sky.

“Happy now?” she asked.

“Very,” Miles said.

He leaned against the shed doorway, breathing in the wet-earth smell that had come to mean home almost as much as the kitchen did.

“The first time I heard you sing,” he said, “you were off-key.”

She blinked.

“Rude,” she said automatically.

“It was at that pub in Camden,” he went on, undeterred. “You remember? The one with the sticky floor and the fairy lights that flickered like they were haunted.”

She did remember.

“I was drunk,” she said. “And the monitor was broken. And the guitarist kept changing the key without telling me.”

“Exactly,” he said. “It wasn’t precise. It wasn’t… polished. But it was you. Loud and a bit messy and very, very certain.”

She stared at him, waiting for the point.

He pushed his glasses up his nose.

“The thing is,” he said, “this song… it isn’t about being certain. Is it?”

She opened her mouth. Closed it.

“No,” she said quietly. “It’s about… not being. But staying anyway.”

He nodded.

“Maybe,” he said, “it’s allowed to be a bit wobbly.”

She made a face.

“I don’t like wobbly,” she said. “I like… big. I like belting. I like people in the back row putting down their pints because they’ve just been emotionally assaulted by a badger with lungs.”

“I know,” he said. “And you’re very good at that. But you’re also very good at singing in the kitchen when you think no one’s listening.”

Her cheeks warmed under her fur.

“I am not,” she said.

“You are,” he said. “You hum when you stir things. You croon at the dog. You make up little nonsense songs about the laundry. That’s the sound of staying, to me. Not the big finale. The bits in between.”

She stared at the damp herb bed.

“So what, you want me to write an ode to baked beans?” she asked.

“If anyone can make baked beans moving, it’s you,” he said.

She snorted.

A raindrop slid off the shed roof and landed on her nose. She blinked, then sighed.

“I hate it when you’re right,” she muttered.

He smiled.

“I know,” he said.

---

They went back inside.

Lillian settled on the stool again, this time without the guitar.

“What if I just… talk first,” she said. “Into the thing.”

“The thing,” he echoed.

“The microphone,” she clarified, waving at it.

He nodded. “We can record whatever you want,” he said. “We’re not wasting tape.”

She put the headphones back on, though she left one ear slightly uncovered.

“Okay,” she said to the mic. “Hi. It’s me. Your… narrator. Your badger in chief. Today we are recording the song that is supposed to sum up the entire emotional thesis of a life spent choosing the small, stubborn, magnificent act of staying put.”

Miles, in the control nook, pressed record.

“Already too long,” she muttered. “Okay. Take two.”

She closed her eyes.

“What does it sound like,” she asked herself aloud, “when you decide not to go?”

There was a long pause.

In the headphones, she could hear the faint hiss of the room, the hum of the old fridge they had repurposed as a snack cupboard, the outside world muffled by walls and weather.

“It sounds like the kettle,” she said slowly. “Boiling at the same time every afternoon, because he always makes tea at four. It sounds like the upstairs neighbour’s washing machine that we don’t even hear anymore, because it’s just… part of the noise.”

Her voice settled, grew more sure, not louder but steadier.

“It sounds like the dog’s paws on the stairs at half six when he knows you’re coming home. It sounds like your daughter’s key in the door, even when she doesn’t live there full-time anymore. It sounds like… your own breathing, finally matching the rhythm of the house you built instead of the one you thought you should want.”

Miles listened, transfixed.

She opened her eyes.

“Right,” she said briskly. “Now put some chords under that before I lose my nerve.”

---

By evening, there was a skeleton.

Verse, chorus, verse. Nothing fancy. No clever key changes or unexpected bridges. Just a melody that rose and fell like someone walking from room to room, checking that the lights were off and the windows were shut.

Lillian stared at the notebook where she had scribbled the lyrics, the words blurring slightly as her eyes grew tired.

“It’s too simple,” she said.

Miles, who was fiddling with an EQ setting, shook his head.

“It’s honest,” he said.

She made a doubtful noise.

He glanced at the clock.

“Come on,” he said. “Let’s take a break. Sarah’s bringing over that friend from her workshop, remember?”

“Oh God, yes,” Lillian said, rubbing her temples. “The one who does performance pottery.”

Miles smiled.

“She seemed nice,” he said.

“They always do,” Lillian muttered.

---

Dinner was chaos in the good way.

Sarah arrived with Bea — small, wiry, glasses perpetually sliding down her nose, clay under her fingernails — and a bag of still-warm bread from the bakery where she’d started doing a Saturday shift.

“It’s a long story,” Sarah said, depositing the bread on the table. “But the short version is, if I model for them on their Instagram, I get free carbs.”

“Proud of you,” Lillian said. “Sit. Tell me everything. Bea, welcome. Do you have any allergies, phobias, or strong opinions about coriander?”

“Um,” Bea said, looking slightly overwhelmed. “I’m fine with coriander?”

“She’s a keeper,” Miles murmured.

They ate. They talked. Bea told them about the evening classes at the community centre where she and Sarah co-taught, persuading nervous adults to get their hands dirty.

“At first they’re all, ‘Oh, I’m not creative,’” Bea said. “And then you give them clay and ten minutes later they’ve made something deeply Freudian.”

Lillian cackled.

“Art therapy disguised as fun,” she said. “Respect.”

At some point, Sarah’s gaze drifted to the door that led out to the garden.

“How’s the song?” she asked casually.

Lillian narrowed her eyes.

“Fine,” she said. “Why?”

“Because Dad texted me earlier saying, and I quote, ‘Don’t panic if your mother is grumpy, it’s just a creative block,’” Sarah said.

Lillian glared at Miles.

“Traitor,” she said.

He lifted his paws in surrender.

“I was warning her,” he said mildly, “not gossiping.”

Bea tilted her head.

“You’re writing something new?” she asked.

Lillian sighed.

“A little homegrown number,” she said. “For the end of the world.”

Bea blinked.

“Wow,” she said. “Low stakes.”

Lillian couldn’t help it; she laughed.

“It’s just a song,” Sarah said gently. “It doesn’t have to hold everything.”

Lillian toyed with her fork.

“I kind of want it to, though,” she admitted. “This season… thing we’re in. All these changes. The staying. The leaving. The lasagne-based emotional crises. I want to put it somewhere.”

Bea considered this.

“In pottery,” she said slowly, “the trick is not to make the bowl too thin because you’re afraid of it being heavy. If you try to make it hold everything and weigh nothing, it explodes in the kiln.”

Everyone looked at her.

“Wow,” Lillian said. “Okay, Penny’s got competition.”

Bea blushed.

“I just mean,” she said, pushing her glasses up, “it’s allowed to be… solid. A bit weighty. It won’t break people to hold it.”

Sarah nodded, impressed.

“Can I steal that for my class?” she asked.

“Please,” Bea said. “I get all my metaphors from overthinking anyway.”

Lillian leaned back in her chair.

“Okay,” she said. “Fine. Maybe the song is a bowl. A very large, slightly uneven bowl. With chips.”

“Now you’re talking,” Miles said.

---

After dinner, when the plates were stacked and Bea had gone home with a Tupperware full of leftovers, Sarah lingered in the doorway of The Den.

“You want company?” she asked.

Lillian hesitated.

Part of her wanted to slam the metaphorical door — to guard the fragile, unfinished song, keep it safe from other eyes until it was perfect. The other part, the bigger one, was tired of perfection.

“Yeah,” she said. “Come in. Sit on that box. Don’t touch the knobs.”

Sarah obeyed, perching on a crate of cables.

Miles had retreated to the house to make tea and give them space. The shed felt smaller with just the two of them, the air thick with sawdust memories and new music.

“So,” Sarah said. “Let’s hear it.”

Lillian swallowed.

“Fine,” she said. “But if you make a face, I will revoke your soup privileges forever.”

She picked up the guitar, settled her paws, and began.

The first verse was shaky, but she kept going.

She sang about morning light on chipped mugs, about the familiar click of the front gate, about the way the street outside had grown up around them — new shops, new neighbours, the same old cracks in the pavement.

Her voice warmed as she went.

By the time she reached the chorus, it had found that lower, softer place in her chest — the one she used in the kitchen, not on stage.

> This isn’t the anthem they told me to write,
> 
>  No fireworks, no highway, no takeoff tonight.
> 
>  Just a kettle, a doorway, a hand on my sleeve —
> 
>  The sound that my heart makes when I choose not to leave.

She finished, the last chord ringing out into the quiet.

Sarah sat very still.

“Okay,” Lillian said quickly. “That was a draft. A sketch. A—”

“Mum,” Sarah said.

Lillian shut up.

Sarah’s eyes were bright.

“That’s it,” she said simply.

Lillian’s throat tightened.

“You’re just saying that because you love me,” she said.

“Yes,” Sarah said. “And because it’s true.”

She stood up and crossed the tiny space between them, wrapping her arms around Lillian’s shoulders from behind, chin resting on her head.

“That’s… what it feels like,” Sarah said softly. “When I stand on the platform in Brighton and have to decide whether to get on the train home, or stay an extra night. When I’m at Clare’s and she laughs in a way that sounds like me and it hurts, and then I come back here and the hallway smells like dog and garlic and paint. It’s not… drama. It’s that little… click. Inside.”

She tapped two fingers gently against Lillian’s chest.

“Like a latch closing,” she said. “Not trapping me. Just… holding.”

Lillian swallowed hard.

“I was aiming for ‘mildly catchy,’” she said. “Now I’ve apparently written a latch.”

Sarah chuckled, her breath warm against Lillian’s fur.

“You wrote a lullaby,” she said.

Lillian frowned.

“It’s not a lullaby,” she said. “No babies. No twinkly bits. Also, it’s in 3/4, which is notoriously anti-infant.”

Sarah squeezed her.

“Not for babies,” she said. “For us. For you. For here. A song you sing to yourself when the world tries to make you feel stupid for not chasing something shinier.”

Lillian went very quiet.

In her mind, she saw the Berlin skyline she had only ever known from films and Miles’s old stories. She saw the emails from the music venues who had once told her she was “too niche,” who now, years later, occasionally slid into her inbox with offers that were flattering and badly timed.

“Maybe,” she said slowly, “I do need a lullaby. For my ego.”

Sarah laughed.

“Don’t we all,” she said.

---

They recorded late.

Miles returned with tea and moral support. He sat in the control nook, adjusting knobs and nodding along, while Sarah curled up on the floor with her sketchbook, occasionally humming harmonies under her breath.

“Do that again,” Lillian said at one point, pointing at her.

Sarah blinked. “What?”

“That bit you just did,” Lillian said, attempting to mimic the little ascending line. “The ‘ah-ah’ thing. That was nice. Put it in.”

Sarah’s ears went pink.

“Oh,” she said. “Okay.”

They built the song like they built everything else in that house: piece by piece, with jokes and minor arguments and too many cups of tea.

At midnight, they had something that sounded very nearly complete.

Lillian stood in the centre of the shed, eyes closed, listening back to the mix.

Her voice floated over the simple guitar and soft, brushed cymbals Miles had added almost invisibly. Sarah’s harmony slipped in on the second chorus, a second thread weaving through the first.

> This isn’t the ending they write in the scripts,
> 
>  No suitcase, no runway, no burning of ships.
> 
>  Just a lamp in the window, a key by the door —
> 
>  The sound that my life makes when I ask for no more.

The last note hung in the air, then faded.

No one spoke for a moment.

Then Sarah sniffed.

“Okay,” she said thickly. “Rude.”

Miles cleared his throat.

“You know,” he said, “we could… put this online.”

Lillian’s eyes flew open.

“What?”

“Just… a little video,” he said, suddenly shy. “Like we did with the Shed Sessions. People liked that. They… it made them feel… something. This might too.”

Lillian hesitated.

Part of her wanted to hoard it. To keep it as their secret spell against the pressure of the outside world.

But another part — the part that had once stood on a sticky pub floor in Camden and yelled into a dodgy mic about bodies and rage and love — knew that songs were meant to travel.

“Maybe,” she said slowly. “Not yet. Not like a big ‘new single from local badger’ thing. But maybe… a clip. A verse. For the people who’ve been listening this whole time.”

“Soft launch,” Sarah said.

“Like a relationship,” Miles added.

Lillian groaned.

“I hate that phrase,” she said. “Fine. A soft launch. A… gentle lob.”

She turned to Miles.

“Is it weird,” she asked, “that the person this is most for is… you?”

He blinked.

“Me?”

She nodded.

“When you turned down Berlin,” she said, “I wanted to write you a big, stupid power ballad about sacrifice and courage and the triumph of domesticity.”

He winced. “Thank you for not doing that.”

“You’re welcome,” she said. “But I also… I don’t know. I wanted to say thank you in a way that wasn’t just words. For choosing… this. Us. The squeaky floorboard on the landing and the leaky tap and my snoring and Sarah’s half-finished mugs on every surface.”

He swallowed, hard.

“You don’t owe me a song,” he said quietly.

“I know,” she said. “That’s why I wanted to write one.”

He looked at her for a long moment, eyes shining.

“Okay,” he said. “Then I accept. On one condition.”

“Name it.”

“That you let me put in a ridiculous key change at the end,” he said.

She stared at him.

“You monster,” she whispered.

He laughed.

“Half a step up,” he said. “Nothing dramatic. Just… a little lift.”

She considered.

“You mean like… when you top up someone’s tea without asking,” she said. “Not a whole new pot. Just… a bit more.”

“Exactly,” he said.

She sighed.

“Fine,” she said. “But if I crack, we’re blaming Bea and her heavy bowls.”

---

The next morning, the rain stopped.

Sunlight pushed through the clouds, soft and insistent. It spilled into the kitchen, turning the steam from the kettle into a tiny, private aurora.

Lillian stood at the counter, phone in paw, thumb hovering over the screen.

On the display was a short video.

Just her, in The Den, guitar in hand. No fancy angles. No filters. Miles had propped the phone on an upturned plant pot and pressed record.

She took a breath, hit “post,” and set the phone face-down on the table like it might bite her.

Miles, at the stove, raised an eyebrow.

“Done?”

“Done,” she said. “It’s out there. Flying. Or lying face down in a ditch of apathy. We’ll see.”

He poured boiling water into the teapot.

“Whatever happens,” he said, “we have tea.”

“You and your priorities,” she said fondly.

They sat at the table. The house hummed its familiar morning song: the whir of the fridge, the tick of the clock, the faint roar of the bus on the main road.

After a few minutes, her phone buzzed.

She ignored it.

Then it buzzed again. And again.

“Oh God,” she muttered. “It’s the internet. It’s angry.”

Sarah, who had just come in the back door, shaking rain off her fur, peered over.

“Or it’s the internet crying in a good way,” she said. “Can I look?”

“No,” Lillian said automatically.

Sarah sat down anyway.

“Okay,” she said. “I’m going to look.”

“Don’t you dare,” Lillian said.

Sarah picked up the phone.

“Mum,” she said.

Lillian covered her eyes with her paws.

“Read me the mean ones first,” she said. “Ease me in.”

“There aren’t any mean ones,” Sarah said.

Lillian peeked through her fingers.

“Liar.”

Sarah scrolled.

“‘I didn’t know I needed a song about my nan’s teapot until now,’” she read. “‘This feels like when my parents decided not to move us abroad when I was nine,’ ‘I played this in my tiny rented kitchen while I made pasta and cried but like, in a healing way.’”

She glanced up.

“Also someone has made fan art of you as an actual badger with a guitar,” she added. “It’s… kind of incredible.”

Lillian’s heart did that complicated thing again.

“Oh,” she said.

Miles smiled softly.

“Seems the lullaby’s working,” he said.

“For other people,” she said.

“For you too,” he said.

She looked around the kitchen.

The same old cupboards. The same slightly peeling paint. The same view of the garden, where the shed sat solid and sure, little strings of light still hung around its roofline like a crown.

Her song was out there now, bouncing around in phones and laptops and other people’s kitchens. But its source was here.

She liked that.

“Okay,” she said, exhaling.

Sarah grinned.

“Okay?”

“Okay,” Lillian repeated. “Let’s make lunch. I feel an overwhelming urge to feed everyone who has ever liked a post.”

Miles laughed.

“We’d need a bigger table,” he said.

“We have a shed,” she said. “We’ll stack them.”

Sarah shook her head, laughing.

“You know,” she said, opening the fridge, “for someone who writes about staying put, you’re very ambitious.”

Lillian smiled, feeling the song still humming in her bones.

“Maybe,” she said, “staying is the biggest ambition of all.”

Outside, a lone cloud drifted past the sun. The light shifted, then settled.

The house, old and creaky and full of music, breathed with them.
# Episode 23: The House That Bark Built

The letterbox clanged with more enthusiasm than seemed strictly necessary.

Lillian flinched, sloshing tea onto the kitchen table.

“Every time,” she muttered, dabbing at the spill with a cloth. “It’s like the postman is personally offended by our door.”

Miles glanced over from the sink, where he was coaxing life back into a stubborn sponge.

“Maybe he is,” he said. “We have very strong opinions about parcel delivery.”

“That was one time,” she said. “And he left my records in the recycling bin in the rain. Art was at stake.”

Sarah padded in from the hall, holding a small stack of post in her paws.

“We’ve got a pizza menu, something from the council, and…” She squinted. “The Islington Gazette.”

Lillian froze.

“Oh no,” she said.

Miles dried his paws on a tea towel.

“They did say it would be this week,” he reminded her.

“I was hoping ‘this week’ meant ‘in three to five years when I have emotionally prepared,’” she said.

Sarah dropped the thin newspaper on the table with a theatrical thud.

“Front page, baby,” she said.

Lillian’s stomach lurched.

“If you are lying to me for comedic effect, I will put you up for adoption again,” she said.

Sarah flipped the paper around.

It wasn’t the front page.

The front page was, predictably, about a bin collection crisis. But there, on the bottom right, was a teasing little box:

> *Inside: The House That Bark Built — meet the family turning a garden shed into a home studio.*

Lillian groaned.

“No,” she said. “I refuse. Take it away. Burn it. Feed it to Mozzarella.”

From his bed in the corner, Mozzarella opened one eye at the mention of his name and then closed it again, unimpressed.

Miles reached for the paper.

“Let’s just… have a look,” he said.

Sarah grabbed it first.

“No, I’m reading,” she said. “It’s about me too, you know. I’m the moody artistic B-plot.”

Lillian dropped her forehead to the table.

“Wake me when my shame has passed,” she mumbled into the wood.

Sarah unfolded the paper to page three.

Her breath hitched.

“Oh,” she said.

Miles leaned over her shoulder.

There it was.

A full-colour photo of their shed, fairy lights on, door open. In the doorway, captured mid-laugh, stood Lillian and Miles. She had one paw on his arm, her head thrown back. He was looking at her, not the camera, expression soft and amused.

On the step below them, Sarah sat cross-legged with her sketchbook open, looking up, half caught in profile. Mozzarella, of course, had decided that was the moment to chase a fly, so his tail appeared as a blur at the bottom of the image.

The headline read:

> **The House That Bark Built**
> 
>  *Inside the Islington shed where songs, art and family find their home.*

Miles made a quiet, surprised noise.

“It’s… nice,” he said.

“Lies,” Lillian muttered into the table.

Sarah read aloud.

“‘At first glance, it looks like an ordinary North London garden shed: wooden walls, a sloping roof, a string of slightly tangled fairy lights. Step inside, though, and you’ll find microphones instead of lawnmowers, paint-splattered sketchbooks where spades should be, and a family who’ve quietly turned this small space into the heart of their home.’”

She stopped.

“Okay, that’s actually quite good,” she admitted.

Lillian lifted her head an inch.

“Is that the bit where they call me whimsical?” she asked.

“Not yet,” Sarah said.

She carried on.

> *‘Lillian, a singer-songwriter who once toured sticky-floored venues across the country, now records from The Den — the affectionate name for the converted shed at the bottom of her garden. Her husband Miles, an engineer-turned-handyman, built the studio himself over the last year, fixing, sanding, and wiring between kettle boils and dog walks.’*

Miles’s ears warmed.

“They mentioned the kettle,” he said.

“Of course they did,” Lillian said. “It’s your side character.”

Sarah read on.

> *‘Their daughter Sarah, a sculptor and art teacher, calls the space “the house-behind-the-house.” “It’s where all the in-between bits happen,” she says. “Where Mum writes songs about washing up and somehow makes everyone cry.”’*

Sarah paused.

“I don’t remember saying that,” she said.

“You did,” Miles said. “You just didn’t know he was recording.”

“Rude,” she said. “Accurate, but rude.”

Lillian finally sat up properly.

“Any talk of my emotional incontinence is slander,” she declared. “Let me see.”

She took the paper from Sarah, scanning quickly.

> *‘In an era obsessed with big moves and bigger ambitions, the family in this small Islington semi have made a quieter choice: to stay. When Miles recently turned down a prestigious job abroad, he did so in this very shed, deleting the email while the roses outside brushed the windows.’*

Lillian’s chest tightened.

“Oh,” she said softly.

Miles shifted his weight, self-conscious.

“I didn’t know he was going to put that bit in,” he said.

“It’s true, though,” she said.

She kept reading.

> *‘“People think staying is the easy option,” Lillian says. “Like you’ve just… given up. But it’s work, you know? Looking at the same walls every day and deciding to keep loving what’s inside them. Fixing the leaky bits instead of running away to somewhere shinier.”’*

She blinked.

“Did I say that?” she asked.

“Word-for-word,” Miles said.

“Wow,” she said. “I’m wise.”

Sarah snorted.

> *‘The Den has become more than a recording booth. It’s a micro-sanctuary (a term Lillian’s sister Penny, a mindfulness blogger, uses with a grin) where the family test out new rhythms. It’s also, increasingly, a community space: earlier this year, they started livestreaming “Shed Sessions,” informal gigs where neighbours squeeze onto cushions and the dog inevitably steals the show.’*

Lillian’s eyes flicked to the final paragraph.

> *‘Asked if they ever regret not chasing bigger stages, Lillian shrugs. “Sometimes,” she admits. “Usually when I see a tour bus. But then I look at this —” she gestures around the shed, to the posters, the plants, the scuffed floor where her daughter’s clay has left a permanent imprint “— and I think, this is our stadium. It’s just… smaller. And closer to the kettle.”’*

She stared at the words for a long moment.

“That’s not the worst thing I’ve ever been quoted saying,” she said.

Sarah looked at Miles.

“She’s underselling it,” Sarah said. “It’s actually really lovely.”

Lillian folded the paper carefully, then unfolded it again.

“People are going to… see this,” she said slowly.

“Yes,” Miles said.

“They’re going to know where we live.”

“They already knew where we live,” he pointed out. “The whole street knows you as ‘the woman who sings while taking the bins out.’”

“Those are show tunes,” she said. “They’re supposed to be loud.”

He smiled.

“I know,” he said.

Sarah’s phone pinged.

“Ooh,” she said. “Penny’s seen it.”

“Of course she has,” Lillian said. “She probably has a Google alert for the word ‘sanctuary.’”

Sarah read the message.

> *You look hot. The shed looks hot. I demand ten per cent of all future Den revenue as your marketing manager. Also, Mum, stop being weird about it and enjoy the love. x*

Lillian made a face.

“Traitor,” she said.

---

The article did what local articles do in the age of the internet: it escaped.

By mid-afternoon, a digital version was circulating on social media.

Penny posted it with the caption: *Proud of my chaotic family and their tiny cathedral of noise.*

Bea shared it to her pottery group, adding: *Come for the shed, stay for the emotional architecture.*

A small but enthusiastic corner of the “tiny studio” community on Instagram adopted The Den as their new aspirational moodboard.

By the time evening rolled around, Lillian’s phone was a buzz of notifications.

She sat on the edge of the sofa, scrolling reluctantly.

“‘Reminds me of my granddad’s shed where he fixed radios and told us stories,’” she read aloud. “‘I’m crying over strangers’ fairy lights, how did we get here,’ ‘Signed a lease on a flat today and was scared, this made me feel like maybe I can make it home.’”

She swallowed.

“Oh,” she said faintly.

Miles, who was sorting through a box of old cables for no reason other than that the box existed, glanced up.

“Good ‘oh’ or bad ‘oh’?” he asked.

“Too much ‘oh,’” she said.

Sarah flopped down beside her, chin on her shoulder.

“You did this,” Sarah said quietly.

“No,” Lillian said immediately. “We did this. You did this. The shed did this. The fairy lights did most of the work.”

Sarah smiled.

“Mum,” she said. “You know what I mean.”

Lillian kept scrolling.

Every few comments, there would be a photo: someone’s cramped kitchen, someone else’s balcony with two mismatched chairs, a bedroom corner turned into a makeshift studio.

Underneath, captions:

> *My Den.*
> 
>  *We don’t have a garden but this is our version.*
> 
>  *Listening to “The Sound of Staying” on repeat while I paint my rented walls mentally because I’m not allowed to do it physically.*

“People are going to get us evicted,” Lillian murmured.

Miles came to sit on her other side.

“Hey,” he said softly. “Look at me.”

She did.

“You wanted the song to… land,” he said. “To go somewhere that wasn’t just this house. It’s doing that. In a very… internetty way. Is that so bad?”

She exhaled.

“No,” she admitted. “It’s just…

She gestured vaguely at the screen.

“…a lot,” she finished.

Sarah nudged her.

“You built something,” Sarah said. “Not just the shed. The whole… thing. The house, the shed, the songs, the way people talk about it like it’s some kind of cottagecore sitcom. You and Dad literally bark-built this life.”

“Don’t say ‘bark-built,’” Lillian said weakly.

“It’s the headline,” Sarah said.

“It’s the punishment,” Lillian replied.

---

The Gazette wanted follow-up content.

“Just a short video for our website,” the hedgehog reporter said over the phone. “A little tour of The Den. People loved the piece.”

Lillian pinched the bridge of her nose.

“Define ‘loved,’” she said.

“We had more clicks than on the council tax story,” he said. “That never happens.”

She sighed.

“That’s not my fault,” she said. “The council should be more interesting.”

Miles, listening from the table, made the universal hand gesture for *be nice to the journalist.*

She relented.

“Fine,” she said. “Come on Saturday. No make-up. No whimsical direction.”

“No promises,” he said cheerfully.

---

On Saturday morning, the house hummed with nervous energy.

Lillian and Miles tidied The Den in the way that people tidy spaces which are fundamentally untidy: shifting piles from one surface to another, arguing about whether wires counted as décor.

“Don’t make it too clean,” Sarah said, leaning in the doorway. “The whole point of the article is that it’s real.”

“I am real,” Lillian said. “I am also allergic to dust on camera.”

Sarah smirked.

“I’m going to set up in the kitchen,” she said. “I’ll be your ‘natural B-roll.’”

“Don’t say B-roll in my home,” Lillian said. “This is prestige television.”

The hedgehog arrived with the same fox photographer and a much smaller camera.

“Just ignore us,” he said, as he stepped into The Den. “Pretend we’re not here.”

“That has never once worked on any sentient being,” Lillian said.

Still, they tried.

Miles fiddled with knobs he had already set correctly. Lillian tuned and re-tuned her guitar. Sarah, visible through the shed window, kneaded clay at the kitchen table as if this were a perfectly ordinary Saturday and not the documentation of their emotional thesis.

“So this is where the magic happens,” the hedgehog said, sweeping his paw in a slow arc.

“This is where the noise happens,” Lillian corrected. “The magic usually involves more snacks.”

They filmed some wide shots, some close-ups of the microphone, a lingering pan across the posters on the wall — old gig flyers, Penny’s meditation printouts, Sarah’s sketches of the family in various domestic poses.

“Can you play something?” the fox asked.

Lillian hesitated.

“What do you want?” she asked. “An upbeat number about tax returns? A dirge about damp?”

“The new one,” Miles said quietly.

She looked at him.

“You sure?”

He nodded.

“If we’re going to show people the house,” he said, “we might as well show them its soundtrack.”

She took a breath.

“Fine,” she said. “But no close-ups of my nostrils.”

The fox made an obedient face.

She stepped up to the microphone, fingers finding the now-familiar chords.

She sang.

She sang about the kettle, and the door, and the sound of choosing to stay.

The camera watched. So did the neighbourhood, in its own way; a couple of kids peered curiously over the fence, Ramon and Rosalia (still not yet moved) hissed encouragement from their back window.

When she finished, the garden felt… still.

The hedgehog cleared his throat.

“Thank you,” he said. “That was… beautiful.”

Lillian squinted at him.

“Is that on the record or off?” she asked.

“On,” he said. “Very on.”

---

A week later, the video went live.

It wasn’t flashy. Just a two-minute slice of their life: a slow shot of the shed door swinging open, Lillian laughing as Mozzarella tried to sit on her footpedal, Miles reaching up to adjust a mic, Sarah shaping clay in the background.

Over it, a rough mix of “The Sound of Staying” played.

The comments came in waves.

Some were from locals.

> *“That’s my kid’s school in the background of the shot at 0:32! Small world.”*

> *“Walk past this house every day. Had no idea there was a studio in that shed. Love it.”*

Others were from further afield.

> *“I live in a flat with no garden but this made me see my hallway differently.”*

> *“Watching this in my parents’ kitchen while deciding whether to move to another city. Thank you for making staying look like an active choice, not a failure.”*

One comment, from someone with a username that suggested at least three cats, read:

> *“My husband and I both turned down promotions this year because we didn’t want to uproot our kids. Been feeling low-key ashamed about it. This shed video gave me more comfort than my therapist tbh.”*

Lillian screenshotted that one.

She printed it out and stuck it to the fridge with a magnet shaped like a carrot.


“Souvenir of our propaganda,” she said, when Miles raised an eyebrow.

---

The attention brought small, odd consequences.

A woman in the supermarket stopped Lillian by the cheese.

“Excuse me,” she said. “Are you… the badger from the shed?”

Lillian, faced with the convergence of her natural habitat and her new-found notoriety, almost dropped the brie.

“Depends,” she said warily. “Are you about to complain about noise levels?”

The woman shook her head.

“No,” she said. “I… I just wanted to say thank you. My mum died last year. She had this tiny sewing room at the back of the house. Watching your video made me…

She swallowed.

“…made me miss the sound of her machine,” she finished. “In a good way.”

Lillian’s throat closed.

“Oh,” she said softly.

Without thinking, she put the brie down and opened her arms.

To her surprise, the woman stepped into the hug.

There, between the cheddar and the camembert, they stood for a moment.

Afterward, Lillian added “crying in the dairy aisle” to the list of places she’d had profound emotional experiences.

---

One evening, Penny came over unannounced.

She breezed into the kitchen carrying a bag of herbal tea and a printout of the Gazette article.

“I brought offerings,” she said. “And evidence of your impact.”

“We already have the article,” Lillian said. “Miles laminated it.”

Miles, at the counter, did not deny this.

Penny slapped another sheet of paper on the table.

“This isn’t the article,” she said. “This is the comments on my blog.”

She read.

> *‘I live alone and my “Den” is my armchair by the window. I light a candle there every evening now. Thank you for giving me permission to treat it like a sacred space.’*

> *‘I always thought I had to move to find peace. Maybe I just need to look at my flat differently.’*

> *‘Is it weird that a badger, a dog, and a golden retriever have made me reconsider my whole life?’*

Penny looked up.

“You see?” she said. “You’re starting a movement.”

Lillian buried her face in her paws.

“I don’t want a movement,” she said. “I want…

She paused.

“What do I want?” she asked.

“Tea,” Miles said.

“Validation,” Penny said.

“Less council tax,” Sarah shouted from the hall.

They all laughed.

Penny softened.

“You wanted to tell the truth about loving a small life,” she said. “You did. People recognised it. That’s all.”

Lillian peeked through her fingers.

“Does this mean I have to be a role model now?” she asked.

Penny snorted.

“Absolutely not,” she said. “Please remain deeply chaotic. It’s your brand.”

---

That night, after everyone was in bed, Miles went out to the garden.

The sky was clear for once. A few stubborn stars managed to pierce the light pollution.

He stood in front of The Den, hands in his pockets.

The shed looked so ordinary from the outside. A rectangle of wood. A door. Two small windows.

Inside, it was wires and instruments and soft rugs and mess.

And something else.

He thought of the email he had deleted in this very spot. The bland corporate language offering him a life elsewhere. The alternate version of himself who had said yes.

He thought of the article calling him “an engineer-turned-handyman.” How, once, he might have balked at that.

Now, he felt…

He searched for the word.

Settled, he decided. In a good way.

The back door creaked open.

Lillian padded out, wrapped in her old dressing gown.

“What are you doing?” she asked.

“Inspecting my empire,” he said.

She snorted.

“It’s very… rectangular,” she said.

She came to stand beside him.

“For what it’s worth,” she said quietly, “I’m glad other people can see what you built.”

He glanced at her.

“You built it too,” he said. “With songs and complaints.”

She smiled.

“I provided feedback,” she said.

They stood in comfortable silence for a moment.

“Do you ever…” She hesitated. “Do you ever wish we’d gone?”

He knew what she meant.

“Sometimes,” he said honestly. “In my head. In the versions of my life that play when I can’t sleep. The Berlin one. The big-studio one for you. The…

He gestured vaguely.

“…alternate universe where we’re both always on trains.”

She waited.

He looked at the shed.

“But when I’m here,” he said, “with you. With Sarah. With the dog snoring and Ramon banging on the wall because I’ve used the drill after ten. No. Then I… don’t. This feels like the life we were supposed to end up in.”

She swallowed.

“That feels like tempting fate,” she said. “The writers could still throw in a surprise twist.”

He smiled.

“Maybe the twist is… no twist,” he said.

She shuddered theatrically.

“How radical,” she said.

---

A few days later, Sarah ran a community workshop called “Draw Your Den.”

In the church hall down the road, a motley group of neighbours and strangers sat at long tables with paper and pencils.

Some drew literal sheds. Some drew window seats, bus stops, beds.

Lillian and Miles watched from the back.

On the far wall, on a scrap of paper Sarah had blu-tacked up, someone had written in big letters:

> *HOME: THE PLACE WHERE YOUR LIFE SOUNDS LIKE ITSELF.*

Lillian nudged Miles.

“Did she write that?” she whispered.

“I think so,” he said.

“Show-off,” Lillian muttered, but her eyes were shining.

At the end of the session, Sarah held up her own drawing.

It wasn’t the shed.

It was their kitchen table.

Three mugs. Three chairs. A fourth, empty one tucked slightly out, waiting.

In the corner of the page, as if almost forgotten, was a little square with fairy lights drawn around it.

“The Den is great,” Sarah said to the room. “But this is where the stories start. And end. And start again.”

Lillian felt something in her chest loosen.

Later, at home, she stuck Sarah’s drawing on the fridge, next to the screenshot of the comment about promotions.

“The house that bark built,” she said, tapping the paper. “Turns out it’s this one.”

Miles wrapped his arms around her from behind.

“And the shed?” he asked.

She leaned back into him, listening to the familiar sounds of their home — the kettle, the radio, Mozzarella’s gentle snore.

“The shed’s the echo,” she said. “The house is the note.”

Outside, The Den sat quietly at the end of the garden, small and stubborn and entirely theirs.

Inside, the last chord of a song about staying hung in the air, ready to be replayed whenever they needed to remember why they chose this life — and why, even with the whole world spinning beyond their street, it was enough.
# Episode 24: Golden Hour

For weeks, there had been clay in the sink.

Little ribbons of it, grey and tan and terracotta, rinsed off hands and tools and trapped in the metal strainer like tiny fossils. The first few times it had appeared, Lillian had grumbled.

“This is how it starts,” she’d said, scrubbing at the residue with unnecessary force. “First the sink, then the floor, then one day I’ll wake up encased in a tasteful ceramic sarcophagus.”

Now, on an early autumn afternoon when the light slanted low and stubborn through the kitchen window, she watched another coil of clay twist down the drain and felt… oddly proud.

“Is this my legacy?” she asked the kettle. “My house turned into an art supply graveyard?”

From the table, Sarah looked up from the list she was making.

“You say that like it’s a bad thing,” she said.

“It is a slightly slippery thing,” Miles said mildly, stepping carefully around a tray of drying tiles. “High potential for breakages.”

“You fix breakages,” Sarah pointed out. “It’s literally your brand.”

He conceded this with a tilt of his head.

Lillian leaned against the counter, mug in paw, and looked at her daughter properly.

Sarah sat at the kitchen table — still their kitchen table, despite the new table in her maybe-flat — surrounded by lists and scraps of paper. A stack of flyers sat neatly to one side, freshly printed and slightly curled at the corners.

On each, Sarah’s hand-drawn lettering twined around a simple illustration of The Den and the house behind it.

**GOLDEN HOUR**

*An exhibition of small lives, held together in clay.*

By Sarah Bark.

It still tickled something in Lillian, that surname. They’d done the paperwork years ago, when Sarah had asked, quietly, if she could officially be a Bark as well as whatever the forms had said before. Seeing it on an exhibition poster made Lillian’s heart perform a small, ridiculous dance.

“You sure about calling it that?” she asked now, nodding at the flyers.

“Golden Hour?” Sarah asked.

“Mm,” Lillian said. “Won’t people think it’s about, I don’t know, skincare or cocktails or something?”

Sarah smiled.

“If they turn up expecting cocktails, they’ll just have to cope with tea,” she said. “It’s what I’ve done my whole life.”

Lillian huffed in amusement.

“It’s my favourite time of day,” Sarah added. “You know that. When the light comes through here and hits the table just right. And The Den. And the neighbour’s washing line. Everything looks… deliberately placed, even when it’s not.”

Miles glanced at the back window.

“You’re basing your marketing concept on the sun’s cooperation,” he said.

“Yes,” Sarah said serenely. “Very on brand for me.”

Lillian grinned.

“Spoken like a true artist,” she said.

---

The exhibition was small by any official gallery’s standards.

It was, in fact, a clever reconfiguration of the spaces they already had.

The Den would host Sarah’s clay figures — a series she’d been quietly working on for months, even before she’d known what they were for. The kitchen would be for tea, biscuits, and the inevitable lingering conversations. The garden, if the weather held, would be for overflow, children, and dogs.

“You sure about having strangers tromp through our house?” Lillian had asked, weeks earlier, when Sarah first suggested it.

“Strangers?” Sarah had echoed. “Mum. This street knows the colour of your pyjamas.”

“Rude,” Lillian had said.

Now, with the date circled on the calendar — TODAY – GOLDEN HOUR – CLEAN THE LOO — there was no turning back.

Lillian stood in the doorway of The Den, paws on her hips, surveying the transformation.

Where there had once been amp cases and half-finished songs, there were now plinths.

Not real plinths — Miles had fashioned them from old crates and bits of shelving — but enough to give the impression of intentionality.

On each sat one or more of Sarah’s figures.

They were small. None taller than a mug. Some barely the size of a thumb.

But they were… precise. Not in the sense of being smooth or perfect — many were lumpy, all thumbprints and texture — but in what they depicted.

Here was their kitchen table, rendered in clay: four chairs, one slightly pulled back. A tiny mug with a crack carefully carved into its side. A plate of something that could be biscuits or could be worry.

There, the corner of the living room sofa, dented in exactly the place where Lillian always sat to watch late-night television with Miles.

On one plinth sat a series of backs: three little figures on a bench, shoulders hunched together, looking out at what might be the sea. The tilt of their shoulders made Lillian’s throat ache.

On another, The Den itself: fairy lights, window, door. Through the door, if you peered closely, you could see a clay microphone and a clay stool.

“It’s like we’ve been burglarised by a very sentimental mouse,” Lillian said, half to herself.

Sarah appeared at her elbow.

“Good?” she asked, slightly too casual.

Lillian turned.

“Terrifying,” she said. “In that way where my insides feel like they’re made of meringue. So yes.”

Sarah let out a breath.

“Okay,” she said. “Okay, good.”

Miles walked in, dusting his paws on his jeans.

“Sign’s up,” he said. “Penny’s already taken a photo for the blog. We’re officially on the mindfulness circuit.”

“Of course we are,” Lillian said. “I always knew we’d end up there.”

Sarah fiddled with one of the plinths, nudging it half an inch to the left.

“How’s the lighting?” she asked.

Miles looked up at the fairy lights, then at the small spotlights he’d rigged up with clamps and bulbs purchased in an extremely stressful trip to the hardware store.

“We’re at the mercy of the actual golden hour,” he said. “But we can fake it a bit.”

Sarah smiled.

“That’s all art is, isn’t it?” she said. “Faking the light until it feels right.”

Lillian pointed a dramatic claw at her.

“Stop stealing my job,” she said. “I’m supposed to be the one who says pretentious things and gets away with it.”

---

By four o’clock, the house smelled like biscuits and nerves.

Rosalia arrived first, because of course she did.

She swept in carrying a tin the size of a hat box.

“Emergency biscotti,” she announced. “For when the British ones run out.”

“You think we’ll get enough people for two tiers of biscuits?” Lillian asked.

Rosalia arched a brow.

“Please,” she said. “Half this street would come just to see what you’ve done with the shed.”

Ramon followed with a bottle of prosecco.

“For the artist,” he said, handing it to Sarah and kissing her cheeks. “I know it’s more of a gallery opening thing than a kitchen thing, but I believe in importing traditions.”

Sarah laughed, cheeks pink.

“Thank you,” she said. “I’m not sure I can drink and host and stop Mum from heckling at the same time, but I appreciate the thought.”

“I don’t heckle,” Lillian said. “I provide constructive commentary.”

Penny appeared next, wielding her phone.

“I come bearing content,” she said. “And calming tea.”

“Is the tea calming or are you calming?” Miles asked.

“Bit of both,” Penny said. “I’ve pre-scheduled three posts and a story. All you have to do is exist and I’ll do the rest.”

“That’s what I said when you were born,” Lillian said.

Penny blew her a kiss.

The doorbell kept ringing.

Neighbours. Sarah’s classmates from Brighton who’d made the trek into town. Bea, balancing a tray of tiny, wonky clay badges she’d made as “take-home talismans.”

Even the hedgehog reporter from the Gazette turned up, shyly holding a plant.

“For your… Den,” he said to Sarah. “Or the kitchen. Or anywhere.”

Sarah took it, touched.

“Thanks,” she said. “We’ll find it a good windowsill.”

Soon, the house was full.

People flowed between kitchen and garden and shed, cups of tea in hand, murmuring.

Lillian, who had expected to need to fill every silence with commentary, found there weren’t many silences.

People stood in front of Sarah’s tiny table and tiny chairs and tiny beds and made their own connections.

“That’s my nan’s house,” one woman said quietly, looking at a clay armchair with a dent in the cushion. “She always sat like that.”

“My dad had this mug,” a man murmured, pointing at a chipped clay cup on a plinth. “Well. Not this one. But it feels like it.”

In the corner, a small child reached out to touch a figure.

“Ah-ah,” Sarah said gently. “Eyes only, sweetheart. But if you want to squish something—”

She led the child to a low table in the corner where a large, forgiving lump of clay waited for exactly such impulses.

“There,” Sarah said. “No wrong answers.”

The child plunged their paws in with glee.

“Look at her,” Lillian whispered to Miles, watching from the doorway. “Our girl. Running a pop-up therapy session in a shed.”

Miles’s eyes were bright.

“She’s good,” he said simply.

“Understatement of the year,” Lillian said.

---

As the afternoon sloped into evening, the light did its trick.

It slid at just the right angle through the kitchen window, across the table, down the hallway, and into The Den.

Clay figures that had been quietly interesting all day suddenly glowed. Their edges softened; their shadows lengthened.

Sarah stepped back and watched it happen.

“This is it,” she murmured.

Bea stood beside her, smudged with clay from supervising the squish table.

“You did it,” Bea said.

Sarah shook her head.

“It did it,” she said, nodding at the sun. “I just… timed the invitations well.”

Lillian drifted to her side.

“Do you want to… say something?” she asked quietly. “To the crowd?”

Sarah eyed the gathered people.

They weren’t a crowd, not really. Two dozen at most, plus one dog and one toddler who currently had more clay on their face than on their sculpt.

But still. They were here.

“Do I have to?” Sarah asked.

“You don’t have to do anything,” Lillian said. “Except be loved, eat snacks, and maybe clear your plates occasionally. But if you want to… they’d listen.”

Sarah hesitated.

Then she took a breath and clapped her paws together.

“Hi,” she said, as conversations quieted.

The room gently turned toward her.

“Thank you for coming,” she said. “I know some of you had to take two buses and one existential crisis to get here.”

A ripple of laughter.

“These are…” She waved one paw at the figures. “These are bits of here. Of us. But also of… you lot. Not specifically you lot, that would be weird, but… you know. The idea of you. The way kitchens look at five p.m. when everyone’s tired. The way sofas sag in the middle. The way someone’s hand leaves a mark on a doorframe because they always lean there when they’re thinking.”

She swallowed.

“I spent a long time,” she admitted, “thinking I had to leave to make anything worth showing. That art had to be about… somewhere else. Somewhere better. But then I came home and realised… this is pretty good material.”

There was a softer laugh at that.

“So,” she went on, “this is me saying thank you to this house. And that house.” She nodded vaguely in the direction of Ramon and Rosalia’s. “And to The Den. And to all of you, for letting me pinch your mugs and your stories and your corner-of-the-eye moments.”

She took a breath.

“And if you take one of Bea’s little badges on the way out,” she added, “I hope when you look at it later, wherever your house is, or your room, or your bench, or your bus stop, you remember that it counts. That it’s… enough to make art out of. To make a life out of.”

Her voice wobbled on the last line.

Lillian, in the doorway, pressed a paw to her mouth.

“She’s good,” she whispered again.

Miles nodded.

“She’s ours,” he whispered back.

---

After the speeches and the biscuits and the quiet crying (mostly Lillian, if she was honest), the guests thinned out.

Rosalia hugged Sarah so hard she squeaked.

“You have to do this in Hastings,” Rosalia declared. “I will find you a community hall. Or a slightly confused café. People need to see these.”

Ramon nodded enthusiastically.

“We will be your coastal reps,” he said. “Art and lasagne. A winning combination.”

Bea gathered up the leftover clay and badges, chattering about future collaborations and pop-up stalls.

Penny filmed exactly four seconds of Mozzarella sniffing a clay figure and declared it her most successful content of the month.

Eventually, it was just the three of them again.

House. Shed. Badger. Shepherd. Golden Retriever.

The sun lingered, then finally dipped.

The fairy lights took over.

Sarah sank onto the back step, exhausted and glowing.

“That was…” She leaned her head back against the doorframe. “A lot.”

Lillian sat down beside her with a small groan.

“Welcome to event hangovers,” she said. “Non-alcoholic, all emotional.”

Miles joined them, balancing three mugs of tea.

He handed them out like medals.

Sarah wrapped her paws around hers, letting the warmth seep in.

“Did you like it?” she asked quietly, not quite looking at them.

Lillian stared at her.

“Did I like it?” she repeated. “I lived it. I am going to be talking about this in therapy for the next ten years. In a good way.”

Miles smiled.

“I loved it,” he said simply. “It… made me see this place the way you see it. All the time.”

Sarah ducked her head.

“Yeah, well,” she said gruffly. “You built it.”

“We all did,” Lillian said. “Even the dog. Especially the dog. His contributions are mostly olfactory, but they count.”

Mozzarella, curled up at their feet, twitched an ear.

They sat like that for a while.

The garden hummed with end-of-day sounds: a neighbour’s radio, a distant train, the rustle of leaves.

Sarah exhaled slowly.

“I’m not going back to Brighton,” she said, without preamble.

Lillian’s mug paused halfway to her mouth.

Miles’s ears twitched.

Sarah kept her eyes on the sky.

“I mean, not to live,” she clarified quickly. “I’ll visit. Obviously. Clare’s there. And my friends. And the pier. But I don’t… I don’t want to move back. I kept thinking I was supposed to. Like it was some test I hadn’t finished. But… I think my studio is here. Or… between here and the maybe-flat. You know.”

She waved the mug vaguely.

Lillian’s heart did the complicated thing again, but this time it felt… steadier.

“Are you sure?” she asked, as gently as she could, which for her still sounded like a polite interrogation.

Sarah nodded.

“I like the side door,” she said. “I like… orbiting. I like that I can host something like this in your shed and then go back to my place and sleep under my own dodgy landlord ceiling. It feels… right. For now.”

Miles’s shoulders loosened.

“For now is allowed,” he said softly.

Sarah smiled at him.

“And you?” she asked. “Any more secret Berlin emails?”

He shook his head.

“No,” he said. “Just spam. And Penny’s blog updates.”

“Same thing,” Lillian muttered.

He laughed.

“I’m here,” he said simply. “I’m… happy here.”

Sarah looked between them.

“You know,” she said slowly, “it’s weird. I spent so much of my teenage years imagining leaving this house in a blaze of glory. Dramatic exits. Storming out. Monologues. And now here I am, voluntarily building a tiny clay version of it and inviting strangers to coo over our mugs.”

“That’s character development, baby,” Lillian said.

Sarah snorted.

“You’re one to talk,” she said. “Miss ‘I will never be domesticated’ who now has a favourite brand of dish soap.”

“It’s environmentally friendly,” Lillian protested. “And it smells like lemon cake.”

Miles raised his mug.

“To side doors,” he said.

They clinked.

The last rays of sunlight slipped away.

The garden fell fully into fairy-light territory.

From inside The Den came the faint echo of Lillian’s recorded voice, looping quietly as some streaming platform played “The Sound of Staying” on someone’s faraway playlist.

Sarah listened to it, then to the real house — the creaks, the hum, the low murmur of the fridge.

“This is my golden hour,” she said softly. “Right here. With you two. With the dog. With the clay in the sink.”

Lillian felt tears prick.

“You’re trying to kill me,” she said.

Sarah leaned her head on Lillian’s shoulder.

“Not until the finale,” she said.

---

The next morning, the house looked like the aftermath of a very well-behaved party.

Empty mugs. Stray napkins. A few crumbs of biscotti that Mozzarella diligently hoovered up.

The clay figures rested on their plinths, the exhibition officially “closed to the public,” which meant the three of them drifted in and out all day, pausing to touch, to smile, to remember whose comment had been about which piece.

Lillian stood in front of the clay kitchen table.

“It’s weird,” she said. “Looking at the tiny version when the real thing is right there.”

She nodded toward the actual table through the window.

Sarah came to stand beside her.

“That’s the point,” Sarah said. “You can hold this one. Well, not literally, please don’t pick it up, but… mentally. In your hand. Wherever you go.”

Lillian tilted her head.

“You’re very into portable metaphors these days,” she said.

“It’s my thing,” Sarah said.

Miles poked his head round the door.

“Tea?” he asked.

“Yes,” they both said.

He shook his head, amused, and disappeared.

Sarah glanced at the clay table again.

“Anyway,” she said. “It’s good practice.”

“For what?”

“For when I do an off-site version,” she said, matter-of-fact. “Maybe at the community centre. Or in Hastings, if Rosalia gets her way. I can’t lug our whole kitchen there, but I can take these.”

Lillian’s chest pinched.

“Taking our kitchen on tour,” she said. “How very on point for this show.”

Sarah smiled.

“Don’t worry,” she said. “I’ll always bring the originals back.”

She bumped her shoulder gently against Lillian’s.

“Besides,” she added, “the real golden hour lives here.”

Lillian looked at the window again.

The light was different now — softer, cooler, the season shifting under their paws.

But the shape of it on the table was the same.

She nodded.

“Yeah,” she said. “It does.”

She took one last, lingering look at the tiny clay table, then turned toward the real one.

Tea was waiting.

So was whatever came next.

The hour between things.

Her favourite part.

Theirs.
# Episode 25: Home

On the morning of the last episode, nothing dramatic happened.

The kettle boiled. The post arrived. The dog snored.

Outside, the street hummed in its usual, unremarkable way: bins being trundled, someone’s radio playing an old song too loud, the distant hiss of buses.

Inside, in the kitchen that had seen everything, Miles stood at the counter, waiting for the tea to steep.

He watched the steam curl, the way it always did, toward the window.

Behind him, Lillian hummed under her breath.

It was not one of her big songs. Not the belt-it-to-the-back-row numbers. It was the little, looping melody she tended to produce when tidying: part lullaby, part complaint.

Sarah sat at the table, sketchbook open, pencil moving.

It could have been any morning, in any year.

Except it wasn’t.

“Are you narrating in your head?” Lillian asked suddenly, breaking her hum.

Miles blinked.

“Maybe,” he admitted.

She narrowed her eyes.

“You’re doing it in that tone,” she said. “Like the beginning of a BBC drama. ‘On the morning of the last episode…’”

Sarah snorted.

“He is,” she said. “I could feel the gravitas from here.”

Miles lifted the teapot in mild protest.

“I was just thinking,” he said.

“You were having thoughts,” Lillian said. “In my kitchen. How dare you.”

She crossed to him and took one of the mugs he held out, brushing his paw with hers.

“So,” she said, blowing on the tea. “Last episode.”

“Don’t say it like that,” Sarah groaned. “You make it sound like we’re all about to die.”

“We’re not,” Lillian said. “We’re just… reaching the bit where the camera cuts away. There’s a difference.”

Miles watched them both.

He had always been slightly in awe of the way they could talk about their lives as if they knew they were being watched. As if they understood, instinctively, the shape of a story.

He had, for years, been the one who fixed the scenery while they improvised.

Now, as he poured tea into three chipped mugs, he realised with a small jolt that this was, in its own quiet way, the climax.

Not the dinner in Berlin. Not the job offer. Not even the shed’s transformation.

Just this: three of them, in the same room, choosing an ordinary morning.

“Stop looking poignant,” Lillian said, nudging him with her hip. “You’ll get wrinkles.”

“I already have wrinkles,” he said.

“Yes,” she said. “Mostly from me.”

Sarah looked up from her sketchbook.

“What are we doing today?” she asked. “Like… plot-wise.”

“Plot-wise,” Lillian echoed.

She looked out of the window.

The garden wore its early autumn outfit: a bit overgrown, a bit tired, still defiantly green. The Den sat solid at the back, fairy lights off in the morning light.

“Nothing,” she said decisively. “Absolutely nothing special. That’s the only right answer.”

Miles nodded.

“There’s a loose handle on the bathroom cupboard,” he said. “I was going to fix that.”

“Of course you were,” Sarah said.

“And I might repot the basil,” he added. “It’s looking a bit cramped.”

“Riveting,” Lillian said. “I’ll sell the rights immediately.”

“And you?” he asked her.

She considered.

“I might do a big tidy,” she said. “You know. The full works. Take everything off the shelves, wipe underneath, regret my life choices, put it all back.”

Sarah smiled.

“And me,” she said, “I have a class at the community centre this afternoon. Intro to Clay. Six adults who think they’re not creative and one teenager who’s too cool to be there.”

She shrugged lightly.

“Then I’ll come home,” she added, as if it were the most obvious thing in the world.

Home.

Not the maybe-flat. Not Brighton. Not Hastings.

Home.

The word settled in the room like something fragile and warm.

Lillian lifted her mug in a little toast.

“To nothing special,” she said.

They clinked.

---

The day unfolded the way days did.

Miles fixed the cupboard handle.

It was, as he’d suspected, a simple case of a stripped screw. He replaced it with a slightly longer one, the sort Ramon would have approved of.

As he worked, he thought of all the things he’d fixed in this house: leaky taps, squeaky doors, the time the washing machine had tried to flood them out.

There were things he hadn’t been able to fix, too: arguments, bad days, the ache in Lillian’s chest when Sarah had first left for Brighton.

But he had been there.

Present.

Holding the spanner, holding the kettle, holding the space.

That counted for something.

Lillian, true to her word, embarked on a full-scale tidy.

She started in the living room, pulling books off shelves, making piles.

“Donate,” she muttered, dropping a slim volume of self-help into one stack. “Keep,” she said, placing a battered songbook back with reverence.

Behind one row of books, she found an old notebook of her own.

Lyrics, scrawled in her younger handwriting. Setlists from gigs in places she barely remembered.

She flipped through it, smiling wryly at her past self’s ambition and outrage.

One page caught her eye.

A list titled: *Things That Will Mean I’ve Made It*.

Play the Roundhouse.

Tour Europe.

Be on Jools Holland.

Have someone get a tattoo of my lyrics.

Have a kitchen that’s mine.

She stared at that last line.

“Well,” she said to the dust motes. “Four out of five isn’t bad.”

She ran a paw along the spines of the books.

“I did okay,” she told her younger self. “Just… sideways.”

In The Den, she put a few things away. Coiled cables. Straightened posters. Touched the microphone, just once.

“Stay,” she told it, as if it were a dog.

It had, she thought, done its job.

Not as a ticket out, but as a way to echo in.

Sarah went to teach.

The community centre smelled of old sports halls and new coffee.

Her students arrived: the anxious accountant, the retired nurse, the barista who’d been drawing on cups for years and wanted to try something more permanent.

And the teenager, hood up, headphones dangling, who slouched in with a look that said this had been their mother’s idea.

“Welcome,” Sarah said, cheerful but not too cheerful.

On the board, she wrote: *Clay: Holding the Everyday*.

She watched their faces as they wrapped hands around the cool, resistant material.

“Make something small,” she said. “Something from your house. A cup. A chair. A light switch. Doesn’t matter if it looks ‘good.’ Just make… a bit of home.”

As they worked, she moved among them, offering tips and encouragement.

The teenager’s slab of clay slowly became a game controller.

“This is the only thing that’s mine,” they muttered.

Sarah smiled.

“That counts,” she said.

On the walk home afterward, she felt a quiet satisfaction.

She was, she realised, doing exactly what she’d wanted: making things, teaching others to make things, going back and forth between spaces that felt like they belonged to her.

The sky was that particular London grey that threatened rain without delivering.

She pulled her jacket tighter and turned the corner onto their street.

Home came into view.

The same scruffy front garden. The same slightly peeling door. The same curtain in the front window, tugged slightly askew by Mozzarella’s latest patrol.

Her steps quickened.

---

In the late afternoon, the three of them converged again.

Miles finished repotting the basil and washed his paws at the sink.

Lillian pushed the last of the book piles into their new configurations and flopped into her usual spot on the sofa.

Sarah came in the back door, cheeks pink, clay under her claws.

“Hi,” she called.

“Shoes,” Miles and Lillian said in unison.

She laughed, kicked off her boots by the mat, and padded into the kitchen.

“How was class?” Miles asked.

“Good,” she said. “I made a group of adults cry over their own light switches.”

“Proud of you,” Lillian said from the sofa.

Sarah leaned in the doorway and watched them for a moment.

Her parents.

Her house.

She opened her sketchbook and began to draw, the lines flowing quickly.

The way Miles stood at the counter, weight evenly distributed, tail relaxed.

The way Lillian sprawled, filling the sofa, one paw flung dramatically over the back.

The mugs on the table. The basil on the sill.

She did not have to imagine it.

She only had to look.

---

As evening settled, there was a knock on the door.

“Please be pizza,” Lillian said.

“It’s four thirty,” Miles said. “Even you have limits.”

She scowled.

“We have no limits,” she said.

He went to open the door.

On the step stood Ramon and Rosalia.

They were travelling light: just themselves, no food, no suitcases.

“Hi,” Rosalia said softly.

Miles’s breath caught.

“You’re…” He searched for the right tense. “You’re still here.”

“For another twelve hours,” Ramon said. “The van comes at eight in the morning. We have measured every box and cried over every spoon. We thought we would pop by.”

Lillian barrelled down the hall.

“Absolutely not,” she said.

Rosalia blinked.

“Absolutely not what?”

“You are not allowed to leave this borough without one last cup of tea at my table,” Lillian said. “It’s in your contract.”

Ramon smiled.

“We were hoping you’d say that,” he said.

They came in.

Shoes off. Kettle on.

It was, in many ways, an ordinary visit.

They sat. They talked. They complained about the estate agents and joked about the seagulls.

But underneath, there was a new layer.

A knowledge that this was, in fact, the last of something.

Not the end of their friendship. That was absurd.

Just the last time the five of them — six, counting Mozzarella — would sit in this exact configuration, in this exact kitchen, as next-door neighbours.

Ramon leaned back in his chair and looked around.

“Well,” he said. “This is very meta.”

“What?” Sarah asked.

“The finale,” he said. “You know? Classic trope. Friends gathered in the kitchen one last time. Jokes. Tears. Possibly a montage.”

“Stop,” Lillian said. “You’re making me self-conscious.”

Rosalia reached across and squeezed her paw.

“Too late for that,” she said.

They clinked mugs.

“Promise you’ll come,” Rosalia said. “To Hastings. We have a sofa bed with your name on it.”

“You stitched our names into the cushions,” Ramon added. “Literally. It’s tacky but heartfelt.”

Lillian swallowed.

“We’ll come,” she said. “We’ll complain about the pebbles and the seagulls and the lack of decent Thai food.”

Ramon grinned.

“Perfect,” he said. “It wouldn’t be a visit from you otherwise.”

Miles looked at them all.

“Promise you’ll call when you get there,” he said.

Rosalia smiled.

“Of course,” she said. “We have to show you the kitchen.”

---

Later, after they’d gone back next door to their last night in the attached house, the three of them stood for a moment with their paws pressed against the thin wall, as they had many times before.

“Still there,” Lillian murmured.

“For now,” Miles said.

Sarah closed her eyes.

She could almost hear the ghost of tomorrow’s emptiness.

It hurt.

But it was, she realised, an ache she could carry.

She opened her eyes and looked at her parents.

“Can we…

She hesitated.

“Can we take a picture?” she asked. “The three of us. Here. Now.”

Lillian blinked.

“We’ve got hundreds of pictures,” she said.

“Humour me,” Sarah said.

Miles smiled.

“Of course,” he said.

They set up Sarah’s phone on the windowsill, propped on a stack of coasters.

Self-timer. Ten seconds.

They scrambled into place at the table.

Lillian in her usual chair. Miles beside her. Sarah on the angle, squeezed in but perfectly slotted.

“What do we do?” Miles asked.

“Just… be,” Sarah said.

The countdown blinked.

Three. Two. One.

The camera clicked.

In the picture, which they looked at later, they were all mid-laugh.

Mozzarella, uninvited but inevitable, had leapt into frame at the last second, tongue out.

The mugs were slightly out of focus. The basil was tilted.

It was, Sarah thought, perfect.

She printed it the next day and stuck it to the fridge with the carrot magnet, next to the comment about promotions and the drawing of the table.

Layers of home, accreting.

---

That night, when the house had quieted, they ended up, almost by muscle memory, in The Den.

Not for a gig. Not for a recording.

Just… to sit.

Lillian perched on the stool, guitar across her lap.

Miles leaned against the wall, paws in his pockets.

Sarah sat cross-legged on the rug.

“Feels weird to end in here,” Sarah said softly.

Lillian nodded.

“It started in the kitchen,” she said. “Feels only fair to close the loop.”

She strummed a chord.

“What do you want to hear?” she asked.

Sarah thought.

“Not the big ones,” she said. “They belong to everyone now. Something… small.”

Miles considered.

“The laundry song,” he said.

Sarah barked a laugh.

“You made a laundry song?” she asked.

“Of course I did,” Lillian said. “I’m a professional.”

She cleared her throat and, with only the slightest self-consciousness, launched into the ridiculous little tune she’d invented years ago about socks and spin cycles and the inevitability of mismatches.

It was silly. It was domestic. It was, in its own way, a hymn to this life.

They were all laughing by the second verse.

When she finished, Miles clapped, Sarah whooped, and
