Mozzarella, who had joined them at some point, wagged his tail as if he’d been waiting for this encore his whole life.

“Perfect,” Sarah said.

Lillian tilted her head.

“One more,” Miles said.

She knew which one he meant.

She didn’t ask.

She played “The Sound of Staying.”

Not the recorded version, not the polished studio take.

Just her voice, the guitar, the slight rasp at the edge of her range that came out when she was tired and happy.

Sarah hummed the harmony without thinking.

Miles didn’t sing.

He listened.

When the last note faded, they sat in the quiet.

“Okay,” Lillian said, after a long moment. “If I do any more feelings today, I’ll explode.”

Sarah stood, stretching.

“Come on,” she said. “Let’s go back to the kitchen.”

They did.

---

Later, as the night deepened and the world outside blurred into streetlights and the occasional distant siren, they found themselves in their original formation.

Lillian at the table, nursing a cup of tea.

Miles at the counter, rinsing mugs.

Sarah in the corner, sketchbook open.

The same as the first episodes. The same as the middle. The same as the days that would come after the last credits rolled.

“The thing about finales,” Lillian said, tapping her mug, “is that they’re nonsense.”

Sarah looked up.

“In what way?”

“In the way they pretend something ends,” Lillian said. “When actually, the people just go on. They brush their teeth. They take the bins out. They have another argument about who used the last of the milk.”

Miles glanced at the nearly empty carton on the counter and decided now was not the time.

“So,” Sarah said slowly, “you’re saying we refuse the finale?”

“I’m saying we refuse to behave differently just because some imaginary audience wants catharsis,” Lillian said.

She paused.

“Although,” she added, “I wouldn’t say no to a tasteful montage.”

Sarah smiled.

“We’ve had a montage,” she said. “In clay. In songs. In blog posts. In that cursed shed video.”

Miles dried his paws.

“What would you put in it?” he asked.

Sarah considered.

“Loose screws,” she said. “Sticky floors. The first time Mum brought me here and I thought the house looked too small for all the noise inside it. Mozzarella as a puppy trying to eat the sofa. Ramon and Rosalia’s first lasagne. The night I got my Brighton letter. The day you got your Berlin email.”

She smiled faintly.

“The power cut,” she added. “The silent room. The train station. The wall. The shed. The clay.”

“And tonight,” Miles said.

“And tonight,” she agreed.

Lillian listened, feeling her chest fill.

“Not bad,” she said. “We’re very bingeable.”

They laughed.

---

Eventually, the tea ran out.

The clock on the wall ticked toward a sensible bedtime.

Sarah closed her sketchbook.

“I should go,” she said.

Lillian’s ears twitched.

“To your flat?” she asked.

Sarah nodded.

“Yeah,” she said. “I’ve got an early class. And if I stay, I’ll never leave.”

Lillian made a face.

“I see no problem,” she said.

Miles squeezed her shoulder.

“It’s ten minutes,” he reminded her.

“I know,” she said. “I just… I reserve the right to be dramatic about it.”

Sarah came around the table and hugged her.

It was not the desperate, clinging hug of a teenager leaving for months.

It was the comfortable squeeze of someone who knew they’d be back before the milk went off.

“I’ll be here tomorrow,” she said into Lillian’s fur. “We promised Ramon we’d wave them off.”

Lillian sniffed.

“Fine,” she said. “Go. Be independent. Traitor.”

Sarah hugged Miles next.

He held her a little longer than usual.

“Text us when you’re in,” he said.

“I always do,” she said.

She pulled on her coat, slipped into her boots, and opened the door.

The night air kissed her face.

“Love you,” she said, over her shoulder.

“Love you,” they chorused.

She stepped out.

The camera — if there had been one — might have followed her for a moment, down the path, past the garden that had witnessed so much. It might have watched her turn at the gate, look back, smile at the warm square of kitchen light.

Then it would have watched her walk away, down the familiar pavement, toward the life she was building ten minutes down the road.

But the camera wasn’t here.

Just them.

Lillian closed the door gently.

The house settled.

Miles turned off the big light, leaving only the lamp in the corner.

They stood for a moment, listening.

Kettle. Fridge. The tick of the clock.

Home.

“Do you regret it?” Lillian asked, out of nowhere.

He knew what she meant.

“No,” he said.

She exhaled.

“Me neither,” she said.

They moved around each other in the small, practiced dance of bedtime.

Dishes. Lights. Lock.

At the bottom of the stairs, Lillian paused and looked back at the kitchen.

The table. The mugs. The basil.

The ghost of their younger selves, sitting where they sat now.

“Goodnight,” she told it.

Then she went upstairs.

---

In the morning, Ramon and Rosalia’s van arrived.

There were hugs in the street. Tears. Promises. Jokes about seagulls and spare rooms.

Then the van turned the corner and was gone.

The house next door stood, for the first time in years, truly quiet.

Lillian squeezed Miles’s paw.

“It hurts,” she said.

“I know,” he said.

Sarah slipped her paw into theirs.

“But look,” she said softly.

They turned.

Their own house.

The same crooked windows. The same front step. The same letterbox that clanged too loud.

Inside, a kitchen. A table. A kettle.

A badger. A German Shepherd. A Golden Retriever.

Home.

They went back inside.

The camera, if there had been one, might have stayed on the street, watching the door close.

It might have drifted up toward the upstairs window, catching a last glimpse of them moving through the rooms.

It might have pulled back slowly, up and out, until the little house on the quiet Islington street was just one square of light among many.

But there was no camera.

Just the sound of a kettle starting to boil.

The low murmur of voices.

The faint, familiar hum of a house that had been lived in well.


Fade to black.

A teacup clinked.

A small chuckle.

Silence.

Home.
